Depth of field can be used to show a character's living space; perhaps their possessions reveal personality traits that visually communicate information to the audience. Showing a character's environment, as Ridley Scott did in *Thelma and Louise*, reveals character traits in a subtle way. When we see Louise's apartment, neat and tidy, and then we see Thelma's house, messy and disorganized, the visuals provide early clues about the personalities of these characters and how their personalities change on the journey they share together. Being able to see the environments in focus in the shot is important.

Using "Hitchcock's Rule," the size of an object in the frame should equal its importance in the scene at that moment. In *Notorious*, the prominence of the cup of tea filled with poison is communicating to the audience its vast importance.

![Black and white still from the film Notorious, showing a woman (Ingrid Bergman) looking forward, and a man (Cary Grant) smiling slightly to her right. The scene is dimly lit, suggesting an indoor setting.]()Notorious

In the many westerns directed by John Ford, the wide-angle lens is used to show the cowboy as being a part of his environment. As the character rides off into the sunset at the end of a film like *The Searchers*, it is as if the land just goes on forever, swallowing the lone hero into it. As the subject moves away from the camera with a wide-angle lens on him, the audience feels like the character is riding away slowly into the vast openness of the plains. The feeling is enhanced through the use of depth of field.

![Black and white photograph of a desert landscape featuring two large rock formations (buttes) in the distance, with a stagecoach visible in the foreground.]()StagecoachIn horror or suspense films, wider lenses are often used, because they allow us to see the character in their environment and we may also see their stalker prior to the character turning around. That sense of suspense works to engage the viewer into the story. As cinematographer John Lindley, ASC, said of shooting the suspense thriller *Mr. Brooks*:

■ I like the idea in this kind of movie of getting closer to people with a wider lens and creating a little bit more depth of field. I wasn't too concerned with whether the photography was terribly flattering to the actors. I felt comfortable choosing the lens and lighting it in ways I felt were right, and not trying to create too much of a high-fashion look. There's a fair amount of handheld work, which lends itself to wider-angle lenses.  
(John Lindley, ASC, ICG, June 2007, 26)

Using a wide-angle lens on a camera that is either on a Steadicam or being handheld is helpful in keeping focus as the camera moves around the room. Since these moving shots are frequently point-of-view shots, they do imply that we are seeing subjectively through the character's eyes. With a wide-angle lens, more of the background remains in focus, similar to the way a human eye would see. From a technical standpoint it is more difficult to pull focus on a moving shot, so the added depth of field is sometimes used simply to keep more in focus in the shot.

Increased depth of field is a characteristic that inherently comes with shooting digital video due to the electronic zooms most cameras have. Trying to eliminate depth of field when shooting in a digital (DV) format requires the use of prime lenses. As previously mentioned, High Definition has a depth of field that is closer to that of Super 16mm than to that of 35mm. There are some ways to try to reproduce a film look with digital cameras, such as the use of film lenses or neutral

density filtration used to lower the f-stop and reduce the depth of field. Lower end digital cameras that have a fixed electronic zoom cannot reduce depth of field, which is one of the features that make them look very digital, in addition to the resolution issues. Of course things are changing rapidly today, with film lenses being used on High Definition cameras. But you are more apt to see a shallow depth of field on a film that was shot in 35mm, Super 35mm, or in the anamorphic format than on a digitally shot project. This is something to consider when determining how the visuals will affect the way you represent your story.

The aesthetic reason to eliminate or reduce depth of field in a shot is to put sole attention on the subject selected to be in focus in the frame. With everything else in the shot soft except for one character in focus, the audience's attention is concentrated only on that subject or object. It can be used in many ways, but one example is when the subject is shown in a busy city environment yet remains the only figure in focus; this may imply that the subject is either alone in this busy world or not yet a part of it. It can also be used as the subjective point of view of another character seeing just that person, despite all the chaos around them. It is specifically telling the audience where to look and that what they are looking at is the only important thing in the shot, since everything else is blurred.

Another reason to soften the background by using depth of field is to mask the location, to obscure the details of the environment, to "fake it" — to make it appear to be somewhere else. Using or eliminating depth of field is both a technical and aesthetic tool that directors need to understand how to utilize properly, so that they can enhance the visual storytelling in their films.

Depth of field should also be considered when intercutting between shots. If you want to maintain continuity between two characters in conversation, they should each be shot with a similar focal length lens to balance a similar depth of field in the background. If one subject is shot with a long lens, softening the background, and the other is shot with a wide-angle lens with a great depth of field, when the shots are intercut, the scene will feel very odd.

Understanding how to manipulate focal length can be used for psychological effect. For instance, you might want to make the audience feel that something is not right in the scene. But if you are not aware of what you are doing with depth of field, you can create connotations that you never intended and give the editor headaches trying to cut a scene maintaining an objective continuity.

Focal length and depth of field are important visual storytelling tools to communicate emotions, provide information, and establish a visual tone to the film. Since the lens is the eye of the camera, it becomes the eye of the audience telling them where to look and interpreting the information they are given. What lens to use and for what scene is a conversation that can easily be shared between director and cinematographer in the planning stages of the film.

## VIDEO VILLAGE AND THE DIRECTOR

There is some debate as to where the director should be when the film is being shot. Should they be near the camera? Should they be in video village? Should they be riding on the dolly with the operator? Most feel that what is important is that the director concentrate on the actor's performance and leave the technical aspects of the framing to the cinematographer. Most cinematographers feel that it is important for the director to be in the actor's eyeline once "cut" has been called so that they are the first person seen by the actor to direct the performance as needed. Others feel that the director should be watching the frame.

Cinematographers are often either standing by the camera or operating, so it is natural that once "cut" has been called, the actor is going to look toward the camera, but the cinematographer is not the one to comment on the performance — that is the director's domain. The first set of eyes an actor connects with should be the director's.

### *Where would the director be? Next to the camera? At video village?*

■ If you're Spike Lee, you are sitting at a monitor watching two or three cameras. If you're Darren Aronofsky, you are standing right next to me by the camera with a clam shell. If you're Joel Schumacher, you're sitting at the monitor. If you are Mathieu Kassovitz, you've got a clam-shell. Every director is different, but they are looking at the image. Directors need to see the frame. They should have an opinion, and I actually feel uncomfortable when they are not looking at the frame.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

■ The monitors are pretty magnetic. Most of the time directors will be by them, but they are moving them closer to where the performers are, rather than being in video village far away from the camera. Alejandro (Iñárritu) will be right behind the camera with a clamshell, Ang Lee will be right near the camera with a small monitor. On Alexander there was a video village, but that was because we had several cameras, and it was necessary for Oliver to see all of them. I have found that directors like to see what is actually going to be captured on the film, and being closer to the performers is what the directors I've been working with do.

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ Nobody should be standing in "vidiot village," except maybe the continuity person, because they are doing a mechanical job. But the director has no business being there while an actor is acting. I don't have to be looking at a monitor to know how my operator is framing a shot, or whether they blew a shot. I can see out of the corner of my eye. I know from experience — why waste my eye on a video monitor?

(Richard Crudo, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

It is important that the director know that they got the performance. Before video assist was used, the director always relied on the camera operator for affirmation that the shot was executed properly. The trust between director and

cinematographer is an important facet of the relationship. They are professionals and know whether or not they got the shot.

## WHAT A DIRECTOR SHOULD KNOW ABOUT LENSES

1. It is helpful to understand how to use focal length to tell your story. What does a wide-angle lens provide? What does a normal or telephoto lens provide?
2. Know basic depth of field issues: What is depth of field and what does it communicate to the viewer? Why would you want to maintain depth of field (focus in foreground and background) and why would you want to diminish it?
3. Understand basics of exposure: what being at a certain f-stop gives you when it is “wide open” or “closed down.” What is the technical effect of that information?
4. Understand that there are certain elements of the craft that a cinematographer must adhere to: Depth of field and maintaining focus are two very specific issues.
5. Given that low light makes depth of field difficult due to the fact that the f-stop is wide open, what does this say about selecting focus?
6. When you call for a specific lens, understand the properties of that lens aesthetically in terms of how far or how close you are to the subject. For example, a wide-angle lens close to the subject may start to pull apart their features, in a way that a normal lens slightly further away will not. A telephoto lens will keep the camera further away from the subject and may provide a voyeuristic perspective, and will soften the background considerably.
7. Understand that sometimes the cinematographer may have to force you into selecting who is in focus at a given time in a low-light situation.
8. Look through the viewfinder of the camera to see the focal length and composition you have selected. If it doesn't work for you, change it to what you prefer.
9. Consider the continuity of depth of field in matching shots.

## VISUAL REFERENCES

Cinematography is not about equipment, technology or even beautiful sunsets or vistas. I believe we affect the audience in a much more subtle way. It's about composition and lighting and storytelling. I believe actors respond to light. Just look at a Rembrandt or Caravaggio painting, or any of the Dutch masters, and tell me light isn't important.

(Wally Pfister, ASC, interviewed by Bob Fisher, [www.cameraguild.com/interviews/chat\\_pfister/interview](#))

Cinematographers generally regard themselves as visual artists rather than technicians. The art of cinematography requires an intuitive sense of light and composition as well as an understanding of how to fully utilize the technological elements of the medium.

The artist in the cinematographer can see light in a way that many people cannot, and with the mastery of their craft they have the ability to re-create a certain light or look on film. In the same way a painter mixes and applies paint to a canvas, a cinematographer blends the various tools of their medium such as lights, gels, flags, silks, and filters to “paint” their canvas on celluloid. Many cinematographers have described themselves as “*painters with light*.”

There are numerous cinematographers who originally came from a background in the fine arts such as painting or photography, so their visual references come from these mediums. They often know art history and the paintings of the masters and have a vast knowledge of film history and the various genres of filmmaking.

■ When I started to shoot color, I still lit like it was black and white, because that's the only way I saw movies. Later on, when soft-lighting techniques came along, I tried to use them but never really enjoyed it. I find soft lighting very boring. I grew up studying painters like Caravaggio and Georges de la Tour, whose lighting is more realistic, with light coming through windows and from sources like candles or fires. For me, lighting is always about trying to duplicate the romanticism of sources. I think the more abstract forms of lighting, like soft-lighting techniques, don't create any tension in movies, especially crime movies. When you're doing a crime film, you have to create shadows. The Black Dahlia was certainly that kind of movie.

(Vilmos Zsigmond, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, September 2006, 34)

Many cinematographers working today have been inspired by the black-and-white cinematography in films from the late 1920s through the early 1950s. In the documentary film *Visions of Light: the Art of Cinematography*, pioneering cinematographers from the classic period of filmmaking are praised for their brilliant black-and-white photography. The beauty of their work when the craft was new is an inspiration to cinematographers to follow. One early inspiration was Billy Bitzer, the innovative cinematographer who worked with D.W. Griffith in the early 1900s on the groundbreaking films *Birth of A Nation* and *Intolerance*. The photography he accomplished with the limited means he had is greatly admired by his peers. He and Griffith invented new ways to shoot as they went along and created a style of filmmaking that had not been seen before. (You can read more about the Griffith/Bitzer collaboration in Chapter 10.)

The classic film noirs of the 1940s are also a major influence on contemporary cinematographers, due to their strong visual style, graphic compositions and sparse single source lighting. Noir films are noted not only for their pessimistic storylines, evil femme fatales, and convoluted plots, but also for their stark low-key lighting and unusual camera angles. The crime and mystery novels of Dashiell Hammett, Cornell Woolrich, James M. Cain, and Raymond Chandler were often the source of inspiration for these films, which conveyed a mood of doom and darkness set primarily in an urban environment. No one is to be trusted in film noir, and the cinematography worked in harmony with the plots to amplify a sense of doom and destruction through the use of dark shadows and skewed compositions. Classic noirs such as *Double Indemnity* (1944), *Murder, My Sweet* (1944), *Laura* (1944), *The Maltese Falcon* (1944), *Mildred Pierce* (1945), *The Postman Always Rings Twice* (1946), *The Killers* (1946), and *Out of the Past* (1947) all share similar stylistic traits.

Film noir as a stylistic movement was inspired by the dark, eerie mood of the early German Expressionist films (*Nosferatu* and *The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari*). German directors such as Fritz Lang (*M*), Josef Von Sternberg (*The Blue Angel*), and F. W. Murnau (*Sunrise*) drew inspiration from the Expressionist films as well as from German Expressionist painters such as Emile Nolde, Franz Marc, and Karl Schmidt-Rottluff, whose paintings were characterized by heightened, symbolic colors and exaggerated imagery. German Expressionism dwelled on the more sinister aspects of the human psyche. In its broadest sense expressionism describes any art that raises subjective feelings from objective observations. The paintings aim to reflect the artist's state of mind rather than the reality of the external world.

By 1950 the storylines in film noir became more focused on psychotic behavior with more ruthless, eccentric narrators, which led to an even darker visual tone. Mirrors were used to suggest disorientation and the possibility of a dark and distorted shadowy self within the anti-hero. Films such as *Sunset Blvd.* (1950), *Lady from Shanghai* (1947), and *Touch of Evil* (1957) fall into this later category. Orson Welles' masterpiece *Citizen Kane* (1939) clearly reveals elements of film noir, but his later work in *Lady From Shanghai*, shot by Charles Lawton Jr., and *Touch of Evil*,

shot by Russell Metty, both utilize long continuous takes with overlapping dialogue to create a sinister world of oddball characters and ultimately end with death.

In film noir the lights not turned on are as important as the lights that are turned on. Many films have a sequence in which characters emerge from complete darkness into light, often carrying a gun. These films have been an inspiration partly because the cinematographers did so much with such a basic element as the sharp contrast between darkness and light.

French New Wave directors like Jean-Luc Godard and Francois Truffaut were greatly influenced by film noir. They associated American detective and crime films with a “pure” cinema of image and ingenious creativity in the rich use of shadows and contrast. There is a tremendous amount of popular criticism and scholarly study devoted to film noir and numerous films to study.

Also, John Alton and John F. Seitz are cinematographers who helped create the look of many film noir classics and are often cited as mentors to younger cinematographers.

![A black and white photograph showing a dimly lit interior scene, likely a room with a chandelier and a desk, viewed through a doorway or partition.]()Out of the Past

■ We certainly use other films as references. They [directors] will show me clips off their computer and go “look at this, love this.” So you think, OK, we’re going to copy that? And they go “well, it’s not copying.” [Laughs]. So it could be anything. Everything is a reference, as far as I’m concerned.

(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

For the film *There Will Be Blood*, Paul Thomas Anderson and Robert Elswit, ASC, did a lot of preparation through visual references. They visited museums together, looked at countless period photographs, and studied the industrial silent footage of actual oil crews filmed at work during the time period in which the film takes place. Additionally black-and-white films were an inspiration.

■ I have so much love for the John Huston film *The Treasure of the Sierra Madre* (1948). It's kind of hard to talk about without gushing. On this film [*There Will Be Blood*] we were trying to keep things simple — the simplest kind of frames, the simplest number of shots — and we tried to follow the influence of filmmakers like Huston. The movies of the '40s are incredibly straightforward. They're the ones I love the most, really.

(Paul Thomas Anderson, *American Cinematographer*, January 2008, 46)

![Black and white still from the movie There Will Be Blood, showing a man (Daniel Plainview) wearing a hat and mustache, looking intensely at the camera.]()There Will Be Blood

Black-and-white still photography has always been a great inspiration for cinematographers. Lighting for black-and-white images is generally based on the zone system of exposing and printing photographs, of which two notable photographers stand out, Ansel Adams and Edward Weston. Their work in the zone system inspired numerous photographers to employ the system as well. Using the zone system incorporates the blackest black and the whitest white at the extreme levels and every shade of gray in between into each individual photograph. Ansel Adams' "Monolith" is a classic example of the zone system in use. Black-and-white cinematography of the 1940s into the early '50s also exemplifies the zone method.

When a viewer looks at a painting or watches images on film, the brightest object in the frame will attract the eye first. So the light should illuminate the most important area or subject in the frame. For example, Marlene Dietrich was always lit a little brighter than anyone else sharing a scene with her, so the audience would have their eyes glued to her. This technique recalls the practices of Renaissance and Neo-Classical painting mentioned earlier as well as the influential photographers using the zone system.

![Black and white photograph titled 'Monolith' by Ansel Adams, showing a massive, dark rock formation dominating the frame, with snow visible at the base.]()Ansel Adams, "Monolith"

![Black and white film still showing a man and a woman (Marlene Dietrich) in a dimly lit scene, illustrating the concept of lighting.]()Visions of Light: Marlene Dietrich was always lit brighter than other actors in a scene.

## MASTER CINEMATOGRAPHERS

There are many cinematographers working today who greatly admire the skills of the “masters” in cinematography. Billy Bitzer, James Wong Howe, Gregg Toland, John Alton, Charles Lang, Stanley Cortez, Robert Burks, Gordon Willis, Vittorio Storaro, László Kovács, Vilmos Zsigmond, Conrad Hall, Owen Roizman, Néstor Almendros, William A. Fraker, Stephen H. Burum, Michael Chapman, Allen Daviau, Haskell Wexler, Caleb Deschanel are a few of the best known. To name just one of each of their films does them an injustice — it is their body of work that reveals their expertise, because many of the masters became proficient in more than one genre or style. If you don’t know the names above, I strongly suggest you go to the IMDb right now and look them up! Then go to Netflix, Blockbuster, or a “movies on demand” channel and watch some of their films. From watching the films you will gather a sense of their individual styles, which will give you a point of reference in communicating with your cinematographer. Someone might be a fan of Gordon Willis, who took chances in low-light situations and with his use of top light in *The Godfather* films. Someone else might be a fan of Conrad Hall and the precision of his light and “happy accidents” or with the patterns of light in a room such as Vittorio Storaro’s work with Bertolucci.

It would also be advisable to watch the documentary *Visions of Light*, which shows many of the masters talking about films that have inspired them, while providing a historical perspective on the history of American film and the craft of cinematography. Having an awareness of the cinematic styles of the masters will open doors in your conversations with your cinematographer, who will invariably know their work.

Just as painters study the work of the masters in oil (Rembrandt, Caravaggio, Vermeer, the Dutch masters, the French Impressionists, the German Expressionists or the Baroque painters) and perhaps replicate their work to learn how they painted, cinematographers will do the same with their “masters,” perhaps emulating the work of Vilmos Zsigmond, Conrad Hall, or Gordon Willis to learn their style of lighting and composition. Since film is a medium of motion, acting, and sound along with many other elements beyond a flat and stationary canvas, cinematographers will study films photographed by “master” cinematographers not only to analyze their lighting styles, composition, and movement, but the other components of the medium as well.

Who is using top light? Who is using cross light? Is there a single source light, or is the key light balanced by the fill light? What kind of light is it? A hard light or a soft light and how does this affect the image? These are all questions your cinematographer will be grappling with and issues you should be aware of, especially how the light will affect the storytelling of your film.

![A close-up shot of a man (likely Al Pacino as Vito Corleone) from The Godfather, illustrating the use of top light. The lighting is dramatic, highlighting the contours of his face from above.]()The Godfather: An example of the use of top light

By now the message should be clear that the best collaborations with a cinematographer will occur with directors who have at least a basic understanding of painting, photography, and film history. How can you create a film like *The Black Dahlia*; *Lust, Caution*; or *The Man Who Wasn't There* if you are not aware of the stylistic movement of film noir? Or the brilliant color cinematography in the melodramas of Douglas Sirk or the camera placement and compositions of John Ford or Orson Welles? Besides having a basic knowledge of the fine arts, it is very helpful to know movies, specific directors, cinematographers, and the styles and genres they work in.

As a director yourself, you may be inspired by the work of a particular director. Take note of whether or not that director works with a particular cinematographer. This could be a point of stylistic reference just in terms of explaining to a cinematographer who you are and what stylistic taste you have in film and composition. The “*auteur*” (French for author) theory of filmmaking that has been so important to the practice of filmmaking in our industry assumes that directors — in collaboration with cinematographers — develop a signature and personal mark that dominates all their films. Perhaps you have the ambition of developing such a strong style yourself; to do so, gaining awareness of the signatures of great directors and cinematographers is essential.

Directors and cinematographers should also communicate with whatever visual means are necessary to come up with how they conceive “the look” of the film and to discuss and share the images that personify that representation. Collaboration could be as simple as sitting down at a café and watching the light at a certain time of day or the way someone walks down the street, or it could be something that is not visual at all but actually more about the tone or the essence of the film. Listening to music can also work to help develop a visual reference — whether it is classical, new age, hip hop, or a single guitar, whatever mood the music conveys can be helpful in establishing the visual tone of the film. Often a director will come to the cinematographer with images from magazines, photography books, architecture books, paintings, or DVDs. Or the director and the cinematographer may screen films together.

■ *The biggest thing I do with a director is talk to them in references. Sitting down and watching DVDs is one way. Spike Lee was amazing on Inside Man. He'd get film prints from the Martin Scorsese library and screen them twice a week. He'd say, "I want to watch '70s films," so he'd set up a screening room and we'd watch French Connection, Midnight Cowboy, film after film on print. Darren (Aronofsky) and I watch DVDs together or look at books with the production designer. We pore over books. Spike (Lee) is now sending me DVDs in the mail, saying "Watch this film." He's more film oriented. Joel (Schumacher) is more obscure. [For] the*

![A black and white image showing a man wearing a dark jacket and sunglasses, adjusting a light-colored cloth or mask over his face. The background is dark and blurry, suggesting an outdoor or high-contrast setting.]()Inside Man

last film we did together, Number 23, he wanted me to watch all these film noirs and then for Tigerland I gave him a reference for an old handheld documentary that was black and white and he ran away with that idea. That was what he wanted to do. Sometimes it's a flurry of references, sometimes it's just one. But references are the only way you can both articulate the same thing. Spike sits right next to me (when we're watching films), and he'll say "I like that right there" and we'll have a frame of reference. I think that really helped the style of Inside Man. Spike's knowledge of American film is astounding.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

■ Peter Weir is very visual. He would have a whole board pinned with still frames, clips, photographs, a piece of material he thought might be nice for a character's coat. Sometimes he'd put in extra boards and keep pinning things up: storyboards, a clipping out of a magazine so you could go peruse that for his references. Others don't do much at all. The production designer comes in and does everything. There was one director I worked with who initially I had a hard time getting on his wavelength. Finally we went to lunch and I thought "Oh good, now I can pick his brain and see what's he's really thinking." It was an hour-and-a-half lunch and at the end of it, he gave me a book and said, "have a look at this, it will give you an idea of what I'm thinking." It was quite a thick coffee table book on still photographs of interiors of cafés in Europe. It was this gorgeous book and that's all I got. So we went off and shot this movie, and it was Academy nominated and all of that.

(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

It is important for a director to have an idea of what kind of light or mood the script calls for and what kind of feeling she or he hopes to convey to the audience through the visuals. A dark, gritty film would emphasize a dark, grim story so you would find the visuals that convey the essence of that feeling to communicate with the cinematographer. For *The Illusionist*, written and directed by Neil Burger and photographed by Dick Pope, BSC, Burger had envisioned an early-cinema look for the film from the time he wrote the script.

■ In films like *Nosferatu*, there's something very creepy in the image itself, in the grain and density the way it flickers, the vignetting. Those create a disquieting feeling and I wanted a disquieting, unnerving undertone for this picture. I didn't necessarily want to make it look old; I wanted it to be real and otherworldly, to inhabit the realm of dream and mystery.

(Neil Burger, director of *The Illusionist*, *American Cinematographer*, September 2006, 51)

Although Burger wanted the film to have a dark look, he also wanted the audience to be able to connect with the characters. He showed his cinematographer, Dick Pope, a book on period photography from 1903 entitled *The Art of the Autochrome*, which is an early technique of color photography. That text was to be the visual reference for the film. It was then up to the cinematographer to figure out how to capture that look with modern film stock and technology. After lots of testing of lab processes, they finally settled on the DI to complete the final look of the film. The cinematographer also chose to use more wide-angle lenses and to avoid Steadicam shots to maintain the integrity of the period imagery during production.

![Two stills from the film The Illusionist. The top image shows two figures sitting in a dimly lit room, possibly a train car, illuminated by warm light. The bottom image is a close-up of a man in a tuxedo, looking forward, with candlelight visible in the background.]()The IllusionistI start with photographs before I start with films. Directors usually come up with films. On Requiem for a Dream, Darren (Aronofsky) wanted me to look at the work of Philip-Lorca Di Corcia, who was a great reference, but I was also looking at the work of Nan Goldin and I saw there was a great similarity there. So I knew we were together on what we wanted the film to look like. There's a naturalism that spoke to the characters that both those photographers exhibited. Photographs for the longest time have always been my most important reference. In other cases it's just a look. For Gothika directed by Mathieu Kassovitz, I loved the work of John J. Muth, who is a graphic novelist and writer, and he was definitely a reference for the film. For me it's visual references, period, whether films or photography, illustrations or paintings. (Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

![A close-up, dimly lit shot of two young men, likely from the movie Requiem for a Dream.]()Requiem for a Dream

■ For The Assassination of Jesse James by the Coward Robert Ford, the idea was to create the feeling of an old-time camera. We weren't trying to be nostalgic, but we wanted those shots to be evocative. The idea sprang from an old photograph (director) Andrew (Dominik) liked, and we did a lot of tests to mimic the look of the photo. Andrew had a whole lot of photographic references for the look of the movie, mainly the work of still photographers, but also images clipped from magazines, stills from Days of Heaven and even Polaroids taken on location that looked interesting or unusual. He hung all of them up in the long corridor of the production office. That was a wonderful idea, because every day we'd all pass by images that immediately conveyed the one of the movie he wanted to make.

(Roger Deakins, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, October 2007, 37)

Many scripts today are derived from comic books or graphic novels. With these “franchise” films, the director and cinematographer usually try to retain the integrity and feeling of the original comic book such as was done in *Iron Man*, *The Dark Knight*, *Batman Begins*, *Superman*, *X-Men*, *Dick Tracy*, *300*, and *Daredevil*. The comic books themselves obviously serve as direct visual references for the films.

■ How do you articulate a comic book's suspension of disbelief in a film? You have to make it a little more sophisticated, but you still have to honor the comic — it's a tough game.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, May 2008, 32)

■ Frank Miller's graphic novel *300* was the blueprint for the look of our film. We were not just making a film using his characters and story, we were specifically making a film out of his book, so its style and compositions were integral to everything we were doing. But we took license when necessary and also took advantage of what we could do on the big screen. Movies are an extension of illustration that adds the dimensions of time, space, and movement to color and composition, so there are major differences in what you can do visually, but the story and characters are really what it's about.

(Larry Wong, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, April 2007, 52-53)

![Three stacked film stills illustrating visual references. The top image shows two figures on horseback in a snowy landscape with a farmhouse in the distance. The middle image shows a figure standing in a field of dry grass with a small white building in the background. The bottom image is a close-up of a figure indoors, possibly a woman, with her hand near her face.]()The Assassination of Jesse James by the Coward Robert Ford

Visual references can come from a variety of sources and should provide a point of departure for the feeling of the film. It all depends on what kind of story you are telling. A family drama might be made more believable by using references closely attuned to the story. If nostalgia, warmth, and pleasant home memories are to be evoked, you might refer to Norman Rockwell for a 1930s or 40s setting, a *Life* magazine Kodachrome photo for the 1950s, or warm amber hues.

■ I think storyboards are more important than visual references in a way because they focus you on what the scene is about. There are no hard and fast rules. It's whatever gives you a starting point to talk about the visualization of the script. It doesn't matter what methods you use. (Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

If the story has a more urban theme, the reference could be from the feeling of a Nan Goldin photograph with a sparse de-saturated hue. But the most important thing for a director looking for a visual palette for their film is to communicate with the cinematographer about the strongest thematic elements of the story, so that the images will “visually punctuate” the essence of the film.

■ On Lorenzo's Oil a very interesting thing happened. George called me over and said, "Johnny, this is the music for the hospital scene, do you want to listen to it?" And it was so ethereal, I thought, he's going to want to do a long dissolve, because we had talked about a long dissolve into the hospital scene. I said, "that music is so beautiful, if we put a touch of smoke in that corridor with the shafting of the sun, because I knew that the sun would be there from the surveys..." He said, "Okay put it in." I reassured him that it would be very light, just so that the music has an ethereal sort of look to go with it, so the reference really came from the music as to what we were going to do photographically. So you are using everything you are seeing and hearing, whatever moves you creatively. (John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

![A dark, moody interior scene, likely a hospital corridor, with a figure sitting on a bench in the foreground, illuminated by shafts of light from a window in the background.]()Lorenzo's Oil

## THE MASTERS IN PAINTING

Another aspect of the use of light in painting and photographic masterworks is what has been come to be known as a “filmic” quality, the idea that such earlier art anticipated what would later be done in film. Caravaggio, Georges de La Tour, Vermeer, Rembrandt, David, Edward Hopper, and Andrew Wyeth have very different painting styles, yet all tell their stories through light and composition. They

create a mood through the direction of light and guide the viewer's eye around the canvas with careful composition. Along with these elements, there are perfectly placed highlights to draw the eye to specific areas of the frame. Study the symmetry in the paintings, the calculated ruptures of symmetry, and be aware of the direction and quality of light. Ask yourself, is there a strong source light in the painting? Where is it coming from? How is that light balanced by the fill side or is it not balanced by the fill side, creating sharp shadows and contrast? Is there a top light illuminating the subject? What kind of mood is created within the painting? How does it apply to the genre or theme of your film? Looking at paintings from the masters will also help you consider the balance of composition in the frame and the use of the entire canvas to communicate the story.

Several paintings have been reprinted in this chapter as examples, but I encourage you to see more artists' work and to visit museums to see the actual paintings. The experience of paintings in their original format and size is often much more intense than seeing them in reproduction.

![Painting titled 'The Angel Appearing to St. Joseph' by George de La Tour. The scene is dimly lit, primarily by a single candle held by the angel, illuminating the faces and hands of the angel and St. Joseph in a dramatic chiaroscuro effect.]()George de La Tour, The Angel Appearing to St. Joseph

In the above painting there is a very strong light source illuminating the subjects from below with a candle (the candle is the motivated source of light). The shadows fall off into darkness. Faces are also heavily shadowed due to the single light source, which is entirely believable for the situation. This painting clearly exemplifies the look of "chiaroscuro" light.

“Chiaroscuro lighting” is a style of lighting borrowed from painting and used in many films with extreme contrasts of light and shadow. The definition of “chiaroscuro” comes from a movement in art from the seventeenth century, generally visible in paintings, but also used in woodworking, etchings and sculpture. Some painters such as Caravaggio, Rembrandt, and La Tour frequently use this quality of light in their paintings. There is usually a strong single source light from one angle (the key side) that creates deep dark shadows on the opposite (the fill) side. Many of these paintings are religious in nature and that strong light such as in *The Calling of St. Matthew*, by Caravaggio, is ethereal, heavenly. In Rembrandt’s work, the light is not necessarily from a religious source, but may be from candlelight or a single light source originating from a window or doorway. Again deep shadows are created and the highlights fall on areas of the painting quite intentionally to direct our eyes accordingly. Light in film can be used in the same way.

![Rembrandt's painting, The Philosopher in Meditation, showing a man sitting in a dimly lit room, illuminated by a strong light source from the left (window) and a small fire in the right corner.]()Rembrandt, The Philosopher in Meditation

In this painting the main source of light is coming from the window with a soft amber glow, but balancing out the composition there is also a small fire visible in the right corner of the frame that brings our eye from left to right.

![A scene from The Illusionist, showing a man's face dramatically illuminated by a single candle, illustrating chiaroscuro lighting.]()The Illusionist: A scene illuminated with a candle

**Chiaroscuro** (Italian for clear-dark) is a term in art for a contrast between light and dark. The term is usually applied to bold contrasts affecting a whole composition, but is also more technically used by artists and art historians for the use of effects representing contrasts of light, not necessarily strong, to achieve a sense of volume in modeling three-dimensional objects such as the human body. Further specialized uses of the term are “chiaroscuro woodcut,” used for colored woodcuts printed with different blocks, each using a different colored ink, and “chiaroscuro drawing,” used for drawings on colored paper with drawing in a dark medium and white highlighting.

In film “chiaroscuro is used in cinematography to indicate extreme low-key lighting to create distinct areas of light and darkness in films, especially in black-and-white films.”

(<http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chiaroscuro>)

![Rembrandt's painting, Parable of the Rich Man, showing a man illuminated by a single source of light, surrounded by darkness, illustrating chiaroscuro.]()Rembrandt, Parable of the Rich Man

In the painting above, a very definite single source of illumination is being used, with shadows surrounding, falling into darkness.

Many films also utilize chiaroscuro lighting, which employs a single light source to illuminate the scene. Think of the ending sequence in *Apocalypse Now* shot by Vittorio Storaro, with Brando's face only partially illuminated by a single light source, or the classic example of Stanley Kubrick's *Barry Lyndon*, with numerous scenes lit only by candlelight. Almost any film that is using a single light source such as a strong key light with no fill, creating deep black shadows, is modeled after the chiaroscuro style in painting.

![A close-up shot of Marlon Brando's face, partially illuminated by a strong key light, creating deep shadows, characteristic of chiaroscuro lighting in Apocalypse Now.]()Apocalypse Now

■ While I was walking around inside the church (the San Luigi Dei Francesi), I discovered the Contarelli Chapel, decorated with extraordinary paintings by an artist whose name I didn't know at the time. One in particular took my breath away: The Calling of St. Matthew, which I later learned was a work by Caravaggio. The thing that struck me was the extraordinary vision of something that I was actually putting in scenes (in my films) back then: a clean separation between light and shadow. I felt immediately impelled to try to understand and deepen the relationship between these two elements.

(Vittorio Storaro, ASC, AIC, *American Cinematographer*, September 2007, 56)

■ Caravaggio was a great filmmaker before there was film. He conceptualized the subject and the composition, chose the figures, designed the costumes and sets, and illuminated them like a master cinematographer, and like the filmmakers who came centuries later he eventually moved from natural light to artificial light.

(Vittorio Storaro, ASC, AIC, in reference to the film *Caravaggio* directed by Angelo Longoni, *American Cinematographer*, September 2007, 57)

Renaissance Italian painters influenced the work of the Dutch masters in the seventeenth century, as both groups became masters of the emotional effect of the treatment of light. Often the Dutch and Flemish painted scenes of ordinary life, where the light was one of the most important elements. The work of one of the Dutch master painters, Johannes Vermeer, has very often been referenced for films due to the use of a strong directional light source usually coming from a window

![Caravaggio's painting, The Calling of St. Matthew, depicting a dramatic scene with strong directional light coming from a window on the right.]()Caravaggio, The Calling of St. Matthew

to the left as well as the subtle use of color. In film, a strong directional light coming from a window would be considered a “source light,” usually the strongest light in the shot, also known as the “key light.” The quality of light in Vermeer’s paintings is not of extreme contrast, as in chiaroscuro, but instead of muted pastel shades. Shadows are softer and the colors are more muted than chiaroscuro pieces, especially depending on the time of day. Early morning light is generally cooler than the amber hues of late afternoon. Vermeer’s interior paintings that are composed away from a window reveal more contrast due to the candle or lantern light sources used at the time. In these paintings the light becomes very directional from the lamp or candle, with amber hues and dark shadows created from the lack of a “fill” light.

In the 2003 film *Girl with a Pearl Earring* directed by Peter Webber and beautifully photographed by Eduardo Serra, the entire film utilizes the style, color, and light of Vermeer’s paintings. Since the story is about Vermeer, it is an example of direct influence in a film emulating art, and the same kind of influence can be perceived, and compared in execution, in films about Warhol, Kahlo, O’Keeffe, Dali, Goya, Van Gogh, and Michelangelo, all of whom have been favorite subjects of scriptwriters and directors in fiction and documentary projects.

![Painting of 'Girl with a Pearl Earring' by Vermeer, showing a young girl in a blue dress and white bonnet standing near a window and a table with a red cloth.]()Girl with a Pearl Earring![Painting of 'The Milkmaid' by Vermeer, showing a woman in a yellow dress pouring milk from a jug into a bowl, standing next to a table with bread and other items.]()Vermeer, The MilkmaidIn *Witness* for Peter Weir, we referenced Vermeer, and many people see that film and see Vermeer in it, left to right window single source reference light — and that's exactly what we did, copied Vermeer. We went down to the Philadelphia Art Museum, Peter bought the book, and that was a big reference for the look of the film. Other funny little things happened on that film. Like everybody else back then, I was using smoke for atmosphere, best filter you could get. But then Kelly McGillis started coughing like crazy and she blamed the smoke, so Peter said, John, can we go

![A scene from the movie Witness, showing two figures in a dimly lit interior, possibly a bedroom or study, with a window visible in the background.]()Witness![Johannes Vermeer's painting, The Astronomer, depicting a man in a dark interior, illuminated by a window, examining a globe and books on a table.]()Vermeer, The Astronomerwithout the smoke, and he was cranky because her performance was getting weak. From that day on I've rarely used smoke, except in a film like Poseidon where I used it all the time because of the situation; the air would be full of smoke from all the rubbish in the air and I needed it for the torches. (John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

## OTHER MOVEMENTS IN ART THAT INFLUENCE FILM

Being aware of various movements in painting will help to isolate which one best suits the film you are creating. Perhaps your film is more of a Vermeer than a La Tour or Caravaggio? Or perhaps it is more realistic, like an Edward Hopper or Andrew Wyeth? Perhaps inspiration is drawn more from still photography and the works of Ansel Adams or William Eggleston? There are numerous influences that can be drawn from contemporary art and photography. Some filmmakers also use fashion magazines or coffee table books as visual references.

The distortion of reality for emotional effect in German Expressionism cited earlier for its influence on early cinema, continues as an important inspiration for cinematographers and directors. Frequently the paintings reveal emotional angst or despair, exemplified in the paintings of Emile Nolde, Karl Schmidt-Rottluff and, famously, in Edvard Munch's *The Scream*. We see this influence in early films such as *The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari* (1920), *Nosferatu* (1922), Murnau's *The Last Laugh* (1927), and Joseph Von Sternberg's *The Blue Angel* (1930), but this movement has also influenced contemporary films, such as Tim Burton's *Edward Scissorhands*, *Batman Returns*, and *Sweeney Todd*.

**Surrealism** is another cinematic influence that appeals to some filmmakers, because of the dreamlike nature of the images drawn from the subconscious mind with little or no logical connection. Surrealism as a movement in painting grew out of dadaism and was prevalent in Europe after World War I. Andre Breton, the theorist and a key leader of the surrealists, was inspired by the writings of Freud and Jung, although where they sought to achieve a therapeutic affect by dream interpretation, Breton saw the unconscious mind as a wellspring for painting, prose, fiction, music, architecture, and even political opposition to conventional rules. Painters such as Salvador Dali, Max Ernst, René Magritte, Joan Miró, Yves Tanguy, and Giorgio di Chirico all delved into the unconscious. Each of them did so with different purposes in mind, but they all explored terrain where reason is outdone by association and in which often haunting and frightening, frequently sexual, desires bubble up to overturn the everyday world. In turn, these painters influenced filmmakers such as David Lynch (*Eraserhead*, *Blue Velvet*, *Mulholland Dr.*), Terry Gilliam (*Brazil*, *Time Bandits*, *12 Monkeys*, *The Fisher King*), and even Alfred Hitchcock. For *Spellbound*, Hitchcock hired Dali to create a dream sequence for the film that incorporated the painter's vision of sexual predation and decay, which Dali said he took from Freud. Although Dali's sequence was cut drastically in the theatrical version of the film, the sequence has become an art and museum cult favorite. Salvador Dali and Luis Buñuel did, however, make the surrealist film *Un chien andalou* (1929), and Buñuel's films made in Mexico contain surrealistic elements, as does perhaps his best known work, *The Discreet Charm of the Bourgeoisie* (1972). Maya Deren's *Meshes of the Afternoon* (1944) is also a surrealist film.

Although perhaps surrealism's strongest influence has been on "experimental" films — such as those of Stan Brakhage, Kenneth Anger, Bruce Conner, Barbara Hammer, Su Friedrich — it is also found in the more widely distributed films of revered directors such as Stanley Kubrick and well-known ones such as Tim Burton. Such directors keep the challenging flame of surrealism alive.

![Black and white image showing a man standing in a dark room, possibly a dream sequence, with a large, blurry, abstract shape (resembling a foot or cloud) visible in the background.]()Dali created this dream sequence for Alfred Hitchcock's *Spellbound*. Although much of this dream sequence was eventually edited out of the film, the surrealist influence is clearly present.

**Impressionism** is another movement that has been inspirational to filmmakers. The movement was named after a painting by Claude Monet entitled "Impression, Sunrise." The style of the artists known at the Impressionists included visible brush strokes and an emphasis on the changing qualities of light through seasons. It was their intention to objectively record visual reality in terms of the transient effect of light and color. The paintings also reveal more open compositions than had previously been seen in French painting. But the quality of light is what attracts cinematographers and directors to this movement. Look up the web gallery of Claude Monet, or any of the Impressionist painters listed below, and you will see they were genius in capturing mood through the ever changing qualities of the light of day.

Some of the best known French Impressionist painters are Claude Monet, Edgar Degas, Pierre-Auguste Renoir, Mary Cassatt, Paul Cezanne, Edouard Manet, Frédéric Bazille, Camille Pissarro, and Alfred Sisley.

## REALISM

There are many realist painters who focused on the quality of light either in the landscape, on the nude, on the still life, or in ordinary rooms. Realist painters include Thomas Eakins, Winslow Homer, Andrew Wyeth, James McNeil Whistler, Norman Rockwell (who was an illustrator for *The Saturday Evening Post*), and Edward Hopper.

Director Sam Mendes was inspired by the paintings of Edward Hopper for *Road to Perdition* (2002), which was adapted from a graphic novel and the last film that Conrad L. Hall photographed. Hall won an Academy Award posthumously for his work on the film.

■ You look at an Edward Hopper painting and you can study where the light sources are. Often, the key to his paintings is where he places the light sources. In "New York Movie," which shows an usherette standing at the side of a cinema, the lighting of the scene is absolutely the source of its poetry. The fact that her face is partially obscured creates a sense of loneliness and desolation. You begin to invent your own story from the imagination [depicted] in the world of the painting. Compositionally, Hopper constantly ensures that your imaginary eye is guided off the frame of the picture. You begin to imagine what is on either side of the frame. In other words, what's important is what is off camera.

(Sam Mendes, *American Cinematographer*, August 2002)

![Edward Hopper's painting 'New York Movie' (1939), depicting a woman in a blue dress standing in a dimly lit cinema lobby, looking towards the screen. The lighting is dramatic, highlighting the woman and the red curtains, while the rest of the scene is dark.]()Edward Hopper, New York Movie

![A still from the film 'Road to Perdition' (2002), showing a dimly lit interior scene. A man is sitting on a bed in the right side of the frame, illuminated by a small light source. The left side is dark, with a doorway visible in the background.]()Road to Perdition

Mendes had also worked with Hall on the 1999 film *American Beauty*, which won both of them Academy Awards for their collaborative efforts.

■ Any great painter who uses light in a particular way is working off instinct, and Conrad is instinctive. If you show him a scene, he'll feel the atmosphere in a corridor and he'll light what he feels to be the atmosphere as opposed to how he thinks it ought to be lit logically or rationally. (Sam Mendes, *American Cinematographer*, August 2002)

■ It seems like when I get into discussing paintings with directors they sometimes turn me on to new ones, but Edward Hopper is one that is always in my library of references. Sometimes it's a film reference like *Road to Perdition* or *KLute* or *Manhattan* and Christopher Doyle's *In The Mood For Love*. It's become on par with *The Conformist* and *Last Tango in Paris* for me, some of the most beautiful films of all time. They might not apply directly to the film you are prepping, but they drive you to be better at what you do. (Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

The specificity and particularity of visual references rather than a general background of images can be extremely important for a director communicating with the cinematographer and vice versa. Many cinematographers will come up with a visual reference to present to the director as a means of opening up the conversation to a visual style or color palette.

■ With Alexander, for example, I presented Oliver with a set of scanned photographs and paintings with references of color composition, grain and lighting, including the photos of Sebastião Salgado and paintings from Rembrandt and Caravaggio. We also looked at the Jean-Luc Godard film *Contempt*. (Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

![A dark, close-up image of a man's face, likely a scene from the film Amores perros, illustrating the visual style discussed in the accompanying text.]()Alexander

■ For *Amores perros* when Alejandro presented the script to me, we had worked together on many commercials before, in this sleek style, precise lighting. He knew my past work, and he was afraid I wouldn't want to do a gritty, dirty movies, so I read the script and came in to our first meeting to talk about the visuals with a book from Nan Goldin that I had recently discovered. Alejandro had the same book with him as a reference, and he said you may not like this, but

here are some references for the movie, and I pulled out the same book, so our starting point was the same. For *Brokeback Mountain*, we used some William Eggleston's photography and also referenced the paintings of Andrew Wyeth and Vilhelm Hammershøi. For *21 Grams* I referred to William Eggleston, Nan Goldin, and Laura Letinsky. On *Babel*, we watched *The Battle of Algiers* for the Moroccan stories, and looked at the photographs of Rinko Kawachi, Mona Khun, and Gueorgui Pinkhassov for the Japanese section. For the Mexican stories, we looked the still photography of Patrick Bard. For *Lust, Caution* we referenced the Skagen school of painting (from Denmark) and Henri Rousseau.

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

![A still image from the movie Brokeback Mountain showing a man in a cowboy hat and jacket standing in a field, framed by dark vertical elements.]()Brokeback Mountain

Many directors will come up with their own set of images to clearly articulate what they envision to the cinematographer, so that they can figure out how to capture the essence of the film. This could happen during the second meeting, after the thematic elements of the script have been discussed. Understanding the thematic elements provides a point of reference, and both director and cinematographer can start thinking of tests to shoot to re-create a particular “look” for the film.

■ I thought quite a lot about Jesse's costume [for *The Assassination of Jesse James* by the Coward Robert Ford] and certain settings in terms of how everything was going to read onscreen. I did a slight bleach bypass on the negative to enhance the blacks. Those considerations were important in terms of rendering detail. Andrew (Dominik) had very specific ideas about the blackness of the costumes against the snow.

(Roger Deakins, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, October 2007, 37)

If images are to be faded they may think of de-saturating the film photochemically, or flashing the film in camera. They may intentionally select a film stock that is less saturated or have the lab print it a specific way to enhance the contrast and de-saturate the colors. The cinematographer and production designer should talk about how wardrobe and the color of the set affects the overall visual concept of the film. (You will find more on the color palette in Chapter 5.)

■ I scan photographs and log things off the Internet and put a reference folder together in my computer. When I look back at the past five films I've done, I noticed the references are very similar: John J. Muth is in that folder, Nan Goldin is that folder.... If you look at the inspirations, they are all very different, but there is something that strikes me as something I like doing, and manipulating what the inspiration is to make it fit my taste in photography.... I am always being open to other things. I was motivated by the last Matthew Barney exhibit in San Francisco, which was all about creating art through restraint. He was in a harness being held away from what he was trying to draw. It spoke so much to being a cinematographer. It's that power of creativity that motivates me, whether it comes from painting, photography, music, or films — it just comes from a creative energy.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

■ If there is any personality to your work as a cinematographer, it is because you are the sum of all things that have influenced you in your life as your life unfolds. It's okay to say I want my film to look like Caravaggio. That could be a reference to a quality of light. Andrew (Dominik) had a lot of references to paintings, oil lamps and firelights and stuff. Most of those painters were trying to reproduce reality, so if you've got a fire in a room and people huddled around it's going to have a certain look. I don't need a painting to tell me what it is going to look like. It's what is around you everyday.

(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

■ Visual references are open to almost anything from tear sheets in magazines, newspapers, books, any publication, photo books. I go down to my favorite bookstore in LA, Hennessey and Ingalls in Santa Monica. They have a tremendous amount of high-end art books, photography books, architecture, and design books... [a] tremendous photography library there. You go find what is applicable; you could scroll through your DVD collection, whatever applies to what you are going for. You could just walk outside and say "you see where the light hits that building? That's it." Any kind of reference, anything at all — and lots of talking.

(Richard Crudo, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

The use of period photographs, Edward Hopper paintings, the light of Caravaggio, La Tour, Vermeer, or Rembrandt, a black-and-white film from the silent days, music, old *Life* magazine pictures, the light in a specific location at a certain time of day, or any number of combinations of these things is the visual language in which a director and cinematographer can communicate. This research will ultimately involve the director, the cinematographer, and the production designer.

## How do the Director and Cinematographer Communicate?

■ Paintings are always a good way for a director to talk about light with a cinematographer. You can talk about music videos, films, commercials, and something that is quite practical are tear sheets from magazines. Somehow I don't feel quite as plagiaristic about that, if I see things that I can just tear them from the magazine and hand the director 40-60 pictures and say this is kind of what I'm thinking about. You can also hang out with the director and go into junk and antique stores and see what appeals to them, which can tell you a lot. You and the director have to like the same things, similar things and then it's a matter of shuffling those things into the movie.

(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ Often times I've gone to the DP with visuals, pictures from magazines, but more often than not the DPs come to me with kind of a book they've put together from pictures ripped out from magazines and whatever on how they think the film should look. It helps a lot; I can leaf through it and say okay, love it, hate it, etc.

(Donald Petrie, DGA, Frost interview, December 2006)

Every situation varies depending on the kind of film you are doing and the personalities of the director and cinematographer involved. You can look back to the work of the “masters” or you may prefer more contemporary artists or photographers for your film. You may also incorporate pieces of other films that could be used for mood more than style or color. If the film is a period piece, then references to old magazine photographs or picture postcards, or other period films, paintings from the time period, documentary films, whatever applies to what you are doing can be used as a reference. All of these visual elements can help you to brainstorm and come up with a look that will best suit your film. But the essential point is that both director and cinematographer come to the table with ideas, share those ideas and talk about the essence of the film. In preproduction, talk about what you want the film to visually represent while always being aware of how the visuals will underscore the thematic elements of the film. Test visual styles, film stocks and lighting, and screen the tests together to decide what will be used for the film.

■ Viewing movies together is the most immediate way of having some common points of reference when discussing style. Good knowledge of a wide range of painters and photographers is the next important step in facilitating the communication between the director and the cinematographer. Being able to describe a certain style, as one resembling a given painter, or knowing where to look for examples of a palette of desired colors helps immensely in arriving at the mutually understandable look of the film.

(Caleb Deschanel, ASC, in J. Kris Malkiewicz and Barbara J. Gryboski, *Film Lighting: Talks with Hollywood's Cinematographers and Gaffers* [New York: Simon & Schuster, 1992], 5)

■ Usually I don't use movies as a reference, I go more to still photography and painting, but mostly still photography. I try to approach lighting in a naturalistic way. I like to capture magical moments of real life or atmospheres that will communicate something. In still photography there are instances that capture that, but they don't have to worry about continuity because it is a single image.

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

![A scene from the movie Brokeback Mountain, showing two men in a dimly lit room. One man is sitting at a table, and the other is lying on a couch, illuminated by a bedside lamp.]()Brokeback MountainAs Federico Fellini once said "*Films are light.*"\* Remembering the quality of light at a certain time of day is an essential skill for a cinematographer. They will notice the quality, color and angle of light entering a room at a certain time of the day, or what the color of the light is just before sunrise or sunset, or the quality of light in a certain location on a cloudy day. They may take still photographs of certain lighting scenarios and present them to the director as an example of a quality of light they are considering, especially if it is one they are supposed to recreate on a sound stage. They could use the 35mm short ends of film from the same stock they will use when shooting the film.

It has been said that when Sven Nykvist and Ingmar Bergman were in preproduction for *Winter Light* they spent an entire day observing the changes in light in a country church in Northern Sweden in order to be able to reproduce that winter light on a sound stage. For the cinematographer, watching the light is essential to their craft, so they can contribute to the visual character of the film.

\*Malkiewicz, Kris, *Film Lighting* (New York: Prentice Hall Press, 1986), 1.

![A still from the movie Road to Perdition, showing a diner building labeled 'BLEWOOD' and a large sign reading 'EAT' in the distance, set in a desolate landscape at dusk or night.]()Road to Perdition, photographed by Conrad Hall, reveals a direct influence from the paintings of Edward Hopper.

![A still from the movie Apocalypse Now, showing a dimly lit interior scene with strong chiaroscuro lighting. A figure is silhouetted in a doorway, and another figure is partially visible in the foreground.]()Apocalypse Now, photographed by Vittorio Storaro, uses strong chiaroscuro lighting.

![A still from the movie Girl with a Pearl Earring, showing a dimly lit interior scene with a woman standing near a bed, illuminated by candlelight.]()This still from Girl with a Pearl Earring looks like a Vermeer painting.

## WHAT A DIRECTOR SHOULD KNOW ABOUT VISUAL REFERENCES:

1. Know your script and what kind of look you want the film to have to prepare your visual references or to accept those of the cinematographer. These references could be images from a magazine, still photographs of a certain photographer, paintings, films, postcards — anything that can visually refer to the style of the film.
2. Have a basic understanding of film genres and the historical context of films. Have a list of films that could be referenced as a stylistic influence or that capture the mood that you would like to attain.
3. Have a basic understanding of how paintings could influence the look of your film. Study art history or visit museums for the inspiration of light, composition, and mood for your film. (*The History of Art* by H.W. Janson is an excellent and extensive guide to art history.)
4. Research still photographers (either contemporary or historical) as references for ways to light, depending on the time period and location of your film.
5. Be willing to accept ideas and influences your cinematographer may bring to you, and communicate clearly on how these visual references would pertain to the style and look of your film. (No cinematographer would want to replicate the look of another film, but they can be influenced by the style and feeling it conveys.)
6. Screen *Visions of Light* to get acquainted with cinematographers and a basic historical overview of the history of cinema.
7. Research films and cinematographers that you are interested in and screen their work with your cinematographer. Talk about what you like about the film and what you would like to incorporate in your own film.
8. Look for postcards, or *Life* magazine online, or any other visual reference that deals with the historical time period of your film and the visual style you wish to convey (consider production design, costume, and light).
9. Keep in mind that being subtle works best: don't overkill a period piece with constant reminders that it is a period piece. Work with your production designer on ideas and communicate with the cinematographer about these ideas.
10. Take the time to discuss with your cinematographer the look and style you would like for your film and share openly the visual references you have both come up with.
11. Visit locations at certain times of day, take still photographs, and shoot tests.
12. Talk about the quality of light with your cinematographer. Look at light and discuss how it will enhance the thematic elements or mood of the story.

## THE COLOR PALETTE OF FILM

Sometimes I'm at the genesis of the color palette. On The Fountain, for example, I was in on very early conversations, and the color was determined by myself, Darren, the production designer, and even the visual effects guys, and that palette was strictly adhered to by all of us.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

The color palette is a subtle way to visually enhance the emotional aspects of a film and guide the viewer to respond to it viscerally. Understanding the basic components of color — what are warm colors? what are cool colors? — and how the audience responds to these colors is essential in communicating with a specific color palette.

The cinematographer's job is to interpret the screenplay in a visual form and guide the viewer's emotions through color, light, shots, angles, and movement. But it's the director's job to know what she or he envisions for the film to begin the collaborative process with the cinematographer. A conversation regarding the interpretation of the script and what the color palette and look of the film might be should come up early in preproduction. It is important that both the director and cinematographer understand the thematic elements of the story and how to enhance it through color and light.

Designing a color palette is the step beyond visual references. The references provide the director, cinematographer, and production designer a starting point, a means by which to communicate a visual look. The color palette is the actual visual character of the film being created for the screen. Some films have a grainy, de-saturated color palette, others a slick, saturated palette, others a monochromatic palette, and others a brown dusty palette. The color palette begins as a direct visual interpretation of the script that makes it a reality on film, where it then takes on a subtle character of its own. The color palette can convey a mood or feeling that stays with the viewer even after the film has ended.

■ When I first talked with Ridley (Scott) about this movie [American Gangster], he said he wanted it to feel gritty, he wanted the image to have the color sucked out of it, to look almost black and white. We didn't end up going that far, but that was the catalyst for our approach. I want my photography to support the story and feel like part of the story, rather than be

*something that draws attention to itself. With American Gangster, I was concerned that a total de-saturation of color would be too much, too stylized for the material.*  
(Harris Savides, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, December 2007, 48)

The production designer and the director will begin discussions about the color of the film early in preproduction, sometimes even before the cinematographer comes on to the project. Then it becomes a meeting of three minds on how the set design will look, the color of the costumes, and how the film will read these colors, and what effect they will have on the overall emotional feeling of the film. The director will articulate ideas about the color palette to the production designer, who will translate the concepts into tangible sets and props, and will present swatches of material, sample colors of paint, and a general set design to the director. The cinematographer then comes into the mix with the technical aspects of manipulating the color to capture the mood or essence of the story and capture it effectively on celluloid. If extensive special effects are being done, even more testing may be involved.

■ [Color palette] is an early conversation beyond getting to know the director. The chief responsibility of the cinematographer is to have discussions with production designers, costume designers, and set dressers about how they see the film. When I work with Darren (Aronofsky) we always talk about the palette first, because it's an element we try to limit. We enjoyed the black-and-white experience so much on Pi that when we went into Requiem for a Dream, we decided to limit the color palette in every way we could. That approach helped us to come to grips with our images, so they didn't get confused. Once Darren sets the palette, it becomes my job — along with the production designer and costume designer — to adhere to that choice. The key colors in The Fountain are gold, representing the Mayans — a sort of fool's gold, a false truth — and white, representing morality and truth. In Requiem For a Dream, it was a very easy delineation between summer, fall, and winter, and the color of the light delineated the moods of those time periods.  
(Matthew Libatique, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, November 2006, 53)

![A scene from the film Requiem for a Dream, showing a group of people sitting on the sidewalk outside a building at night, illuminated by warm, yellowish light.]()Requiem for a Dream

![A still from the film Requiem for a Dream showing a person in a red shirt running or struggling in a dark, snowy environment.]()Requiem for a Dream![A still from the film The Fountain showing a close-up of a tree trunk and roots in a desolate, earthy landscape.]()The Fountain■ On some films I've done enormous amounts of testing. For *O Brother, Where Art Thou?* I did a lot of testing, because we started out trying to reproduce the look of the film photochemically and hit a wall. So then we decided to take the risk and do it digitally as a DI. So that was quite a lot of testing. I did a lot of testing on *The Man Who Wasn't There*, because of the black-and-white look we wanted. I did a lot of testing with Andrew Dominik on *The Assassination of Jesse James* by the Coward Robert Ford, because there was a specific look he wanted for the film in terms of the photographic effects of the vignetting. He wanted certain sequences where the black in the frame was actually a dark red, he wanted color introduced into the shadows and he wanted a certain kind of vibrancy to the image that wasn't a typical photochemical process. So we mixed it photochemically and digitally to get the kind of looks that he

wanted. When we started shooting Jesse James, I had done twenty different shots of interiors, exteriors, and night shots, and I manipulated them in the camera and then afterwards to get these specific looks that we both settled on for a template for the movie.  
(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

![A wide shot of a house on a hillside at dusk or night, with several figures standing near the base of the hill. The scene is dimly lit, suggesting a dramatic or suspenseful moment.]()![A dark, close-up shot of a figure, possibly a man in a suit, standing in a wooded area. The image is heavily shadowed, emphasizing the mood of the scene.]()The Assassination of Jesse James by the Coward Robert Ford

## COLOR TEMPERATURE

To understand how to manipulate color you should first understand the unique qualities of film emulsion and how it responds to light. These are the tools of the craft that the cinematographer will be dealing with to create the color palette of the film.

Natural daylight is not the white light our eyes perceive it to be. Our eyes work more like a video camera automatically white-balancing everything we see. But film reads light much differently than the human eye does — instead interpreting the color of daylight as a bluish hue. This is reflected in the nature of daylight film stock, which has a color temperature reading of 5500 degrees Kelvin on a color temperature meter. Daylight balanced film stock is made to work without any color correction filtration outdoors and is also balanced at 5500k.

The sun is actually a very bright hard light and reads quite hot on the Kelvin scale: blue is actually hotter than amber in color temperature. Film emulsion is sensitive to color temperature and reads the light as it sees it, so daylight balanced

film is designed to absorb that light without any color correction filtration. The film reads daylight at 5500k; it absorbs the blues of daylight naturally. HMI lights are also color balanced at 5500k to match the color of natural daylight. Using HMI lights to augment daylight works well on daylight balanced film, because they are the same color temperature. Even adding a half blue CTB to a tungsten light will bring the color temperature closer to daylight. Playing with color temperature is to a cinematographer what mixing paint is to a painter — part of the craft.

Tungsten film is balanced at a cooler color temperature of 3200k, which is an amber-looking light. Tungsten light is similar to what a regular incandescent light bulb in your home lamp looks like, casting a warm amber glow.
