Haynes and cinematographer Edward Lachman, ASC, paid homage to Douglas Sirk and his cinematographer Russell Metty, ASC, copying much from the original, such as rich saturated images, dark shadows, and the frequent use of mirrors in the shots.

![Black and white still from the film All That Heaven Allows, showing a scene with several people in a dimly lit interior setting.]()All That Heaven Allows

![Black and white still from the film Far From Heaven, showing a man and a woman standing indoors, with a framed picture visible on the wall.]()Far From Heaven

Nicholas Ray's family melodrama *Rebel Without A Cause* (1955) stars James Dean, Natalie Wood, and Sal Mineo as troubled teens ostracized by their families, each of which has something very wrong with its father. The three join together to create their own "family." A tragedy ends the film, but typical of the melodrama, the sad note is paired with an indication of hope. The melodrama audience is often given a choice to focus on a ray of hope or a larger and fateful event in the film's closing

segments. When melodrama follows Nicholas Ray's pattern, it is dark in tone and mood, often set in a suburb, with a tragic ending softened by a small positive sign of transformation.

![Black and white still from the film Rebel Without a Cause, showing a young man looking forward and a woman leaning in close to him.]()Rebel Without a Cause

The resurgence of the old Hollywood genres in a new form in the 1970s did not leave the melodrama behind. It returned with a vengeance with the tear jerker *Kramer vs. Kramer* (1979), directed by Robert Benton and starring Dustin Hoffman and Meryl Streep, centering on a divorced couple and a custody battle set in New York City. *Ordinary People* (1980), directed by Robert Redford, starring Mary Tyler Moore, Donald Sutherland, and Timothy Hutton, is about a family that falls apart after the death of one child and the attempted suicide of the other in an affluent suburb of Chicago. *Terms of Endearment* (1983), directed by James L. Brooks with Debra Winger, Shirley MacLaine, and Jack Nicholson, concerns a mother-daughter relationship in which the daughter battles cancer and a disintegrating marriage. All these films were squarely in the melodrama tradition and all were as popular as, or

![Black and white still from the film Kramer vs. Kramer, showing a man and a woman embracing and kissing.]()Kramer vs. Kramer

even more popular than, their tearjerker ancestors. All three films were also nominated for Academy Awards for their performances. The genre provides an emotional range for an actor, with interesting and tortured characters to embody.

The melodrama continues today in stories of dysfunctional families, but is now mixed with comedic elements and more subtle musical scores. A few examples are *Steel Magnolias*, *American Beauty*, *Moonlight Mile*, *The Family Stone*, *Thirteen*, *Pieces of April*, and *The Ice Storm*, which all fall into this genre. Today's melodramas are lit in a style closer to ordinary reality, with darks and lights motivated directly from light in the home or from the windows. Shadows fall naturally on the characters' faces. One of the more beautifully shot melodramas from the recent group just named is *American Beauty*, which was photographed by the late Conrad Hall.

![A black and white still from the film American Beauty showing three people seated around a dining table with candles and food.]()American Beauty

**Film noir** has already been discussed stylistically at some length in the Visual References chapter. The French term *film noir* (which means “dark film”) has stuck with this genre classic, whose key period was between 1940–1950. The darkness of the films was natural, given the underworld and hard-boiled detective stories they retold from pulp fiction. “Trust no one, especially the femme fatale” could be the film noir motto.

The black-and-white format for the films was largely abandoned, but the themes and other elements re-emerged in the modern, “color noir” version of the genre, as seen in films like *Chinatown*, *Body Heat*, and *L.A. Confidential* in the 1970s and early ’80s. They recalled the quintessential noir films such as *Double Indemnity* (1944), *Laura* (1944), *Mildred Pierce* (1945), *Out of the Past* (1947), and *Sunset Blvd.* (1950). Favorite images from those classic films are dark lonely streets. Stylistically shadows prevail, characters walk out of darkness with slashes of shadow across their faces. Even during the day, darkness is the predominant feeling. Pessimism and doom are certainties.

Many filmmakers make references to this genre, such as the recent Brian De Palma film shot by Vilmos Zsigmond, *The Black Dahlia*, which has a more traditional noir feel to it, or Ang Lee's *Lust, Caution*, which draws elements from the noir film with a sadomasochistic love story and the mentality of “trust no one” found in the traditional noir films.

The movie I just finished now [Lust, Caution], directed by Ang Lee, is a genre movie, like a film noir set in late 1930s. It's a spy thriller and it has a spiral of complicated issues regarding love, spying, and betrayal. We toyed with the idea of really going for it and lighting like the movies of the '30s and '40s, going for that film noir look, but we decided against it and instead created our own faded film noir. It was tricky to come up with what that meant exactly, so I tried not to dwell too much on it. The main thing for me was that the lighting was a little more deliberate than previous movies, a little more controlled, which made sense with the subject matter, because the main character is this young girl who is in theater and acting, and her whole plot is based on her performing. So I allowed myself to be more theatrical with the lighting, but instead of doing the hard lighting of film noir, I went with soft lighting and it was all about controlling the soft lighting, making it more precise even though it's soft, and that was the challenge, while still maintaining shadows. So it doesn't look like a film noir movie.

We also played with depth of field separating foreground from background with shallow depth of field, shooting wide open at T1.3 with master primes and contrasting colors with different color temperatures clashing at times, red with green in one instance, a lot of purple with gold, so instead of shadow versus light it was color of light versus another color of light. That was our own version of film noir.

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

![Black and white still image from the movie Lust, Caution, showing a woman in a trench coat and hat, looking slightly to the side.]()Lust, Caution

**The horror film** is another enduring genre that evolved very soon after cinema itself was created. The earliest sources came from German Expressionist films such as *The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari* (1919), directed by Robert Wiene, and the first vampire film, *Nosferatu* (1922), directed by F. W. Murnau and loosely adapted from the 1897 novel *Dracula* by Bram Stoker. The genre became very popular in the hands of American filmmakers in the 1920s. The essence of the genre was always to frighten audiences and allow them to vicariously experience their worst fears without any genuine danger, a variety of safe voyeurism.

The horror genre has adapted to changes in audience. The degree of shock, violence, and revolting imagery has been increased due to audience expectations.

![Black and white still from the silent film Nosferatu, showing Count Orlok (Lon Chaney) and a woman in a dimly lit room.]()NosferatuThe silent film star Lon Chaney was an icon of the early genre, appearing in many films such as *The Hunchback of Notre Dame* (1923) and *The Phantom of the Opera* (1925). In the early 1930s, horror was in its classic phase in Hollywood, when *Frankenstein* (1931) and *Dracula* (1931) were huge hits. Universal Studios was the main force behind the creation of the American horror genre, frequently using Boris Karloff and Bela Lugosi in films such as *The Mummy*, *The Invisible Man*, and *The Wolf Man*. The horror genre prevailed throughout the next six decades with remakes of *Dracula*, *Frankenstein*, *The Wolf Man*, and *The Mummy* being made over and over again.

After the demise of the Production Code in 1964, the horror film started to change into the slasher/gore films of the early 1970s, such as *Carrie* and *The Texas Chainsaw Massacre*. *Rosemary's Baby* and *The Exorcist* from the 1970s were not gore but pure horror. The *Halloween* films, the *Freddy Kruger* films, and *Nightmare on Elm Street* all were horror/slasher films. Directors like Wes Craven and John Carpenter became associated with the genre. Bram Stoker's *Dracula* (1992), directed by Francis Ford Coppola, had an “A” list cast starring Gary Oldman, and featuring Brad Pitt, Tom Cruise, and Winona Ryder, and was created as a homage to the classic horror film.

The horror genre is still going strong in the twenty-first century with films like *The Grudge*, *The Ring*, *Black Water*, and many others influencing Japanese and Asian directors, which reveals the influence and runaway success of horror fiction. In the U.S. the *Texas Chainsaw* remake, *Amityville Horror*, and *Silent Hill* remakes and franchise films, such as *Frankenstein*, *Dracula*, and *Wolfman*, attract loyal fans. The box office for horror films continues to be strong, keeping this genre alive. Stylistically, it is a visually creative genre that can be dark and creepy or the horror can take place in ordinary brightly lit situations, such as in the *Final Destination* films. The camera

can be loose and handheld, with shifting point of views that can create tension and suspense in the viewer.

■ On *Alien* vs. *Predator*, [there had been seven previous versions of the franchise] and the only one I had ever seen was the first one, Ridley's *Alien*. [I was] up in Vancouver prepping with the Brothers Strause. I told them I hadn't seen any of the other films, and I asked them what I should watch to prepare. They looked at each other and one said, *Texas Chainsaw Massacre*, the remake. I started laughing and the director said, "just do your thing." He said "when Ridley did the original, he said, 'this should be *Texas Chainsaw Massacre* in space.' That's the inspiration, that's why we hired you, we want that kind of vibe, that kind of horror." (Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

![A dark, blurry image showing a close-up of a person's face, possibly in a confined space, illustrating the concept of 'Captive'.]()Captivity

The action film is now frequently derived from comic book figures or graphic novels. Today they are more of a franchise film than a genre, by which I mean that the studios make sure there is room for numerous sequels if the first one is successful, such as the comic book action hero films, *Spiderman*, *Batman*, *X-Men*. Because of the special effects that are now available, these comic book films have evolved into a new genre that didn't exist years ago.

The action-figure film is generally targeting the younger market, so it must maintain at least a PG-13 rating. The generic conventions must fit existing comic books, and that always involves some sort of superhero who must overcome an evil force to save the city or the girl. But the special effects are in a sense the "stars" of the film, since audiences expect to be dazzled by fantastic explosions, combat, weaponry, or the morphing of characters. They have become the family popcorn movie of the blockbuster summer or holiday season. Since they are also franchise films, the endings are left somewhat open so that a second and third film can be made.

## GENRES & BUDGETS

Predetermined conventions of a particular genre often coincide with basic assumptions about budgets for each type of movie. Romantic comedies are generally given

a decent mid-range budget depending on the cast. “A” list actors might swell the budget, but it would still be far from the \$100 plus million that a franchise action film receives. Romantic comedies do not usually include costly special effects or the building of models, green screen shooting, animatronics, etc.

■ I really haven't done any Hollywood romantic comedies, but I know that the guys say they have to light it bright. The producers and the studios say, "this is a romantic comedy and I want to see everybody and everything." To go against it would be to maybe light for reality, to go even further against it would be to light it as though it was dark, and they don't let you do that. There is a whole edict in Hollywood.... Producers will say "there's \$40 million bucks up there, and I want to see their eyes!" There's a lot of things you have to balance out to get what you and the director want and not get the studio on your backside, telling you to reshoot it, because they can't see both of the actors' eyes. They basically want to see where their money is going. I would be really difficult to go against the Hollywood genre of bright, airy, glossy, and colorful for romantic comedies.

(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

Understanding the genre that your film fits will predetermine numerous stylistic and thematic elements, whether you are conscious of them or not. In preparing for your film in preproduction, you may watch numerous films within the genre with your cinematographer, to be aware of its conventions, even if you choose to go against some of them. But even if your goal is breaking the genre or using it in your own way, the best way to do so is to understand the genre thoroughly and intimately.

## LIGHTING STYLES

■ In the best case scenario a cinematographer is an artist, but we are craftsmen first and foremost. Sometimes something is making you wrap that light around closer to camera than you usually do and it's an instinct. You may start lighting differently based on what you are shooting, whether your inclination is to light it bright or dark and moody. There are guys who make a living by lighting things bright and flat, so if they have to light something more dramatic, then they move the light away from the camera. As cinematographers we know the difference between a light right next to the camera and a light 90 degrees away from it, because we have a frame of reference for what something should look like in our minds. So it's really because of craftsmanship. But you strive to deviate from norms. That's what keeps you going.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

By now the genre-typical films we have discussed and the general outline of the lighting styles associated with each genre should be clearly in mind. The established and expected genre conventions are familiar and always ready at hand. Yet each director and cinematographer tries to approach a film as an individual entity, even though we have already been programmed to have certain expectations of how a suspense film will look or how a romantic comedy will look. Making a choice to go intentionally away from a more established look is often a decision that

extends not just to cinematography but all the elements of a picture, from production design to wardrobe.

There are lighting styles that are generally associated with specific genres, such as the romantic comedy being “high key” or “up key” or the film noir or thriller being “low key.” There are also films that use primarily soft light or films that use more hard light to create the shadowed effects.

■ In a particular scene where you expect everybody to be in a dark spooky house, let's play it up key, turn on all the lights and let's find the horror in the light. The Exorcist is a perfect example. It's not a scary lit movie; it's lit, for the most part, in a pretty conventional way, the way you would expect things to look in real life. The horror comes out of what is happening in the story in this conventional setting. You see what looks like a normal living room — but here comes the devil.

(Richard Crudo, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

The 1950s films of Alfred Hitchcock — such as *Rear Window*, *Vertigo*, *The Birds*, *North by Northwest*, *To Catch A Thief*, and *The Trouble with Harry* — all share a similar visual style. They are in color, they are lit fairly brightly, yet the subject matter is dark and would imply a more ominous lighting style. In this series of films made in America and beautifully photographed by cinematographer Robert Burks, ASC, Hitchcock chose to set his bizarre stories in normal and mundane situations. In *Rear Window* a neighbor is actually doing something gruesome and unthinkable, and in *The Trouble With Harry*, a body keeps disappearing in a quaint Vermont town. In *Vertigo*, San Francisco becomes the beautiful background to a story of a man haunted and obsessed, and in *The Birds* a quaint little seaside town is terrorized by a flock of killer birds. These films embody Hitchcock's famous remark that terror and suspense are most powerful when “ordinary people are put in extraordinary

![A black and white still from the film Rear Window, showing four people indoors. A woman is standing in the center, looking concerned, while a man is seated in the foreground right, holding a camera.]()Rear Window

situations.” We can add that terrifying drama results from pairing ordinary landscapes and lighting with the force of extraordinary events.

## HIGH-KEY AND LOW-KEY LIGHTING

Genre lighting tends to remain consistent. Romantic comedies are generally going to be lit more brightly with what is known as “high-key” or “up-key” lighting, as in films such as *Something's Gotta Give*, *The Holiday*, *How to Lose a Guy in 10 Days*, and *My Best Friend's Wedding*. Some independent films are considered comedies and may be lit less high key, like *Little Miss Sunshine* or *Juno*, but the genre films tend to be similar. The visuals are there to underscore the thematic elements of the film, so if nothing dark or ominous occurs, why would shadows be present in a comedy? It would send a different message to the viewer, who would subconsciously wonder what was lurking in the shadows.

In the three-point lighting setup, the “key” light is usually the brightest source of light in the scene. It will be the light that generally augments the source light in the shot. The source light is an existing lamp or a window that may be visible or implied in the scene. The key is usually a motivated light that the audience will accept as being created by the source light whether visible or invisible. A strong key light illuminates one side of the scene. If left alone, dark shadows would be created on the opposite side. So a “fill light” is used to balance the shadows that the key has created. How much or how little fill used determines the contrast ratio of the scene (how much shadow difference there is from one side of the subject’s face to the other). If the fill light is of equal strength with the key light, it could almost balance or completely fill in the shadows. The resulting contrast between the key and fill side would be very minimal, a balanced contrast ratio, with very little difference between the key and fill side, meaning that the ratio is 1:1 or 2:1, a “high-key” (bright) light.

The third component of the three-point lighting setup includes a back light or a kicker, which is used to separate the subject from the background. In a high-key lit environment the background will be fairly flat and without shadows.

In the comedy or romantic comedy, the lighting style is considered “high key,” because the contrast range between the two sides of the face would be almost the same. For example, if the key side had a reading of  $f/4$  the fill side could have a reading of  $f/2.8$ , meaning there is only one stop difference between the two sides of the face, making the contrast ratio 2:1, which is considered “high key.” If the lighting were even brighter, the two sides of the face would read exactly the same making the contrast ratio 1:1. The background would also be readable, perhaps just one stop under from the subject. A bright, non-threatening atmosphere has been created in a high-key setup. Of course many other lights are used in this situation, but overall the lighting structure is generally derived from the three-point lighting concept.

■ *A happy film should be a brightly lit film, because your mood changes with the density of the photography.*  
(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

![A black and white photo showing a group of people sitting around a table in a dimly lit room, likely a scene from a film.]()![A black and white close-up photo of a smiling woman with blonde hair, likely a scene from a film.]()The Holiday: High-key lighting

Something else you might notice in high-key films are that the “source lights” present in the scene, such as lamps in the room that will all be turned on. This lighting style tells a romantic comedy audience to relax, because nothing ominous will suddenly appear from the darkness.

■ *Sometimes some of the boys are doing something that may be too heavy, too obviously a genre .... I don't like to take the audience out of the movie. If you walk into an office and suddenly find it's so dark you can hardly see the people, it's almost ridiculous. I go to movies and think, that's just too dark. It's not logical, and I'm a bit boringly logical. I like the logic of*

*continuity and I like the logic of the lighting of a situation. As soon as you go period or futuristic, you can do anything you feel, because nobody's been there.*  
(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

Low-key lighting is when the fill side either doesn't exist or is much darker than the key side, such as in all of noir films, many thrillers and suspense films, and horror films. In these films the backgrounds are not always lit brightly. Source lights are not all turned on and it is implied that anything or anyone could come out of the shadows. This darkness creates the tension and suspense that these genres are known for. Low-key lighting could have a contrast ratio of say 4:1 or higher depending on the amount of fill light used to bring up the shadow area created by the key light, it could be 10:1 which is more of a chiaroscuro effect. The backlight could be very dim or non-existent depending on the mood being created. A recent film like *Lust, Caution* has a dark low-key look to it that underscores the darkness of its theme.

![Black and white still image from the film Lust, Caution, showing a man in a suit and tie in a dimly lit setting, illustrating low-key lighting.]()Lust, Caution

Dramas are more realistically lit. There are some shadow areas and faces are not always evenly lit. There is more room to play with light and shadow in these films, depending on what the thematic elements of the story are and where the film takes place. The use of location shooting and seasons creates a more realistic effect in many films where the light becomes a way of bringing to life the environment represented.

As previously mentioned in Visual References and The Color Palette chapters, sometimes characters are assigned specific colors that indicate something about their emotional state. This also becomes part of the lighting. A film that takes place in the Northeast in the winter is going to have a cooler bluer feel to the exterior than one that takes place in the autumn with a more amber feel. Interiors can be created to be warm or cool depending on the situation. A cozy home would reveal

warmer hues, whereas a cold environment may have more blues present in the light, with perhaps a little more shadow. But contrast has to do with the difference between highlight and shadow, not necessarily color.

In *Ordinary People* the mood of the film is primarily somber and it was shot and lit quite realistically by cinematographer John Bailey, ASC, with a very unobtrusive camera. The audience feels as if they are watching what is happening in this unraveling family. It is not high key because natural shadows fall from the source lights, creating shadows on characters faces at times. The house décor is bland and beige, which is a reflection of Mary Tyler Moore's character, who seems to care more about upholding appearances than she cares about her son. Her own pain is masked by a repression that is reflected in the perfection of her home. As her son Conrad seeks to unravel his demons at the psychiatrist's office, there is both warmth and a contrast in the light. The warmth comes from his ability to reveal himself to the doctor and the contrast is essentially the darkness of his pain. The light and camera-work in the film is so subtle that it allows the actors' performances to shine through and touch the audience. We register the emotion in their eyes. It is a classic family melodrama, with natural lighting.

![Black and white still from the movie Ordinary People showing three characters (Mary Tyler Moore, Timothy Hutton, and Michael O'Keefe) standing indoors.]()Ordinary People

■ Genre movies in general aren't necessarily auteur driven. Unless you are dealing with one person, you are not going to be able to articulate a voice. It's like, why is the food so bland on a film set? Not everybody can eat spicy food, but everybody can eat bland food and put salt in it. That's how cinematography is for genre movies: it's okay. I'm serving more than one master. The director wants to do this, but these five producers think it should be this way and amongst those five, there is a disagreement and there are three versions of what to do. That's not going to lead you to have a concise look to a film. I learned that recently. To deviate from the norm to create a vision and tone [you need] the voice of one (the director). What will stop you from that is the voice of many. When you realize the situation you are in, you have to embrace it and make the best film that you can.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

## CASE STUDIES OF GENRE FILMS

There are auteur directors known for working in a specific genre and other directors who are successful in many genres. The same is true for cinematographers. Michael Ballhaus, ASC, is a frequent collaborator with Martin Scorsese in films that are dark and violent in subject matter and style. Ballhaus can go from shooting *Gangs of New York* for Scorsese to the romantic comedy *Something's Gotta Give* for Nancy Meyers. He has shot in almost every classic genre, including the horror film (*Dracula*, 1992), the Western (*Wild, Wild West*, 1999), the gangster film (*Goodfellas*, 1990), and other genres.

*Something's Gotta Give* definitely falls under the genre of romantic comedy, with its glossy, high-key look (as do nearly all of Meyers' films, such as *What Women Want*, *The Parent Trap*, *Father of the Bride*, and *The Holiday*). The characters in *Something's Gotta Give* are rich and the very bright atmosphere is reinforced by an upbeat soundtrack, clearly telling the audience how to emotionally respond. The house in which most of the film takes place is an idyllic beach house in the Hamptons with a very white décor. In the film's dinner scene there is classic high-key lighting, with a strong top light, yet none of the characters are shadowed and every source light in the room is lit. There are details visible in the background, because there are virtually no dark areas. Contrast is kept to a minimum as much as possible.

![A black and white photograph showing a group of people seated around a dining table, likely a scene from the film Something's Gotta Give. The lighting is bright and high-key.]()Something's Gotta Give: The dinner scene

In the scene in *Something's Gotta Give* in which Jack Nicholson's character has a heart attack, the way in which it is shot, performed, and lit — along with the music of Marvin Gaye's “Let’s Get It On” in the background — tells the audience that, despite the seriousness of the situation, it’s okay to laugh. The light is bright and continues to be bright as the character is wheeled into a hospital that appears safe and inviting, where the attending physician is Keanu Reeves. The dangerous episode leads straight to the lighter comedy in the rest of the film.

![A black and white photo from the movie 'Something's Gotta Give' showing three women sitting in a hospital waiting area, looking towards a man in a white coat (a doctor).]()Something's Gotta Give: Hospital scene

Another hospital scene, this one in the *City of Angels*, directed by Brad Silberling and beautifully photographed by John Seale,ASC, is a drama that conveys an entirely different mood. The character of the physician (Meg Ryan) is being watched by an angel (Nicholas Cage) as the doctors try to save a little girl. But this hospital is dark, ominous, bloody, and messy. As the little girl dies, the angel watches from the darkness. It feels sad and tragic, with the light clearly reflecting the tone. A later scene shows a melancholy Meg Ryan in her extremely dark office lit only by a single lamp. The lighting and imagery tell the viewer this is not a comedy, and it is not okay to laugh. Something terrible and unexpected could happen at any moment.

![A black and white photo from the movie 'City of Angels' showing a long, dark hospital corridor with a single overhead light source, and a figure walking away from the viewer.]()City of Angels: Hospital scene

![A dimly lit interior scene, likely a bedroom or study, featuring a bed, a desk, and two lamps. This image is associated with the film City of Angels.]()City of Angels

These two examples suggest how powerful the effect of lighting and visual elements can be in the same basic location, with different effects carefully managed in a combination of lighting, set design, casting, and music.

■ I have noticed in watching the films of today that they are fighting the preconceived idea of the genre film a bit. There's a change of gears in Hollywood right now. Maybe some are going a little too far with the handheld camera, available light, dark, moody, out of focus, like Tony Scott dumping a whole lot of stuff in the films to keep the energy going. To me it doesn't work. If it isn't written in the script, and you haven't got a decent bloody story to start with, then energizing the photography or handheld or double printing doesn't make it a good film. All it does is create this incredible MTV two-hour rock video.

(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

We all like certain genres of film more than others, according to our individual tastes. The visual styles of our favorite genres may well be part of what attracts us to them as well. In the romantic comedy, we may want the couple to get past all their quarreling and end up together. In the Western, we want the hero to take down the bad guy with a shoot-out in the street. In the musical, we want to see amazing dancing and romance. We want the gangster to be violent with a certain charisma, and for the action films to have emotional tension as explosive as the cars or gas tanks that will catch fire. In a melodrama, we may just want a good cry. In a thriller, we may be in the mood to be manipulated by the suspense. From horror we may want to scream. As a director, you'll need to satisfy such audience expectations, but in a way that is fresh and distinctive enough so that the audience feels that they have experienced something new.

## WHAT A DIRECTOR NEEDS TO KNOW

1. A point made in other chapters holds especially true here. Immerse yourself in film history: take a course, read the many books on popular genres, and watch movies with a critical understanding of how they resemble one another and where they revel in inventing variations on a theme.
2. Pay special attention to classics of the genres. That way it is easier to see where current films have altered the genre's conventions.
3. Observe stylistically how a particular genre you are interested in is portrayed visually. What is the production design, what kind of sets? What level of wealth, poverty, conformity, quirkiness are in the environment? What about the lighting — is it high key and bright or low key and dark? Are long shots primarily used or close-ups? Take note as you watch.
4. Consider the tone of your film in relation to its associated genre. Music can be considered early on, as it is often a guide for everyone involved in a film to keep in mind the emotional response desired from the audience.
5. Talk to your cinematographer about how you want to represent your genre. Do you want to go along with the pre-established conventions or try to alter them? If going along with them, do you want to pay “homage” to what has been done before, thereby accentuating the conventions (as was done in *Far From Heaven*)?
6. Know who the target audience is for your film. For example, for the horror film, is the target audience the same demographic as the *Final Destination* films or the *Texas Chainsaw Massacre* films? Teen films can be considered a subgenre of their own. There is a difference between horror films based on Steven King novels and teen slashers like *Scream*. Know the audience you are addressing.
7. Be careful in your selection of cast based on the genre you are making. It is a Hollywood maxim that good casting is 90% of directing.
8. Selection of locations and environments should be carefully considered based on the genre you are working in as well.

## References for Chapter 6

Cook, David A., *A History of Narrative Film*. New York: W.W. Norton, 1981.

Kawin, Bruce F and Gerald Mast, *A Short History of the Movies*, 10th ed. New York: Pearson/Longman, 2008.

Pramaggiore, Maria T., and Tom Wallis, *Film: A Critical Introduction*. Boston : Pearson/Allyn and Bacon, 2006.

Schatz, Thomas, *Hollywood Genres: Formulas, Filmmaking, and The Studio System*. Philadelphia: Temple University Press, 1981.

## THE TOOLS AND AESTHETICS OF MOVEMENT

The camerawork and movement has to fit into the performance and the emotion of the scene. That way the audience won't notice it, but the energy is all there.  
(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

Movement is an integral part of motion pictures; it is what they were designed to do. But the question of when to move the camera and when the camera should remain static often arises in a director's mind. What should be considered is how the movement will help to tell the story. Camera movement should always be incorporated into the visual interpretation of the script for the screen.

Cinematographers think visually, not only in terms of light and shadow but also in terms of camera movement. When a cinematographer reads a script, movement will inevitably come to mind for certain shots or scenes, and she or he may suggest these ideas to the director. In other cases the director may have a specific idea already mapped out regarding camera movement and will request that the cinematographer execute those moves. Whatever the situation, one must carefully consider the how and why of camera movement.

Movement can be subjective, where it is the point of view of one of the characters, such as through the use of a handheld camera or Steadicam shots. It can also be objective, where it simply observes a character with no implied point of view. The camera in this case may just follow the subject from point A to B.

There are always a number of classic moves that come to my mind when I am reading a script and I will jot them down, such as a camera move will be great here or a crane shot there. So moves can definitely come to mind on the first reading of the screenplay. Actually most of my ideas come from the first reading; the second and third is more about getting the details straight. Suggestions on camera movement could all be made by the DP, or all made by the Director, or in the morning the Director and the afternoon the DP. What you ultimately hope for is a happy and harmonious collaboration where both Director and DP are smiling at the end of the day and say "that was great." I prefer that to just doing it all myself, and I think directors do as well.  
(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

Historically movies began as static images recording events. Thomas Edison Laboratories are credited with the invention of the first true motion picture camera, the Kinetograph, back in 1889. But the only motion in those early films was what the subjects were doing in front of the camera; the camera itself never moved. Filmmakers of the time period, including George Melies and the Lumiere Brothers, shot hundreds of films, but never moved the camera. The first movement of the camera is a small pan that is seen in the 1903 film *The Great Train Robbery*, directed by Edwin S. Porter. But the camera did not become truly mobile until the collaboration of D.W. Griffith and his cinematographer Billy Bitzer, who were the first to use tracking shots in *Birth of a Nation* (1915) and *Intolerance* (1916).

The silent film years to follow were very creative in terms of moving the camera, since it was unencumbered by any sound equipment. As the storylines became

![A black and white still from the film 'The Great Train Robbery' showing a scene in a wooded area. A figure on horseback is prominent on the right, and another figure is visible further left.]()![A black and white close-up still from the film 'The Great Train Robbery' showing a man with a mustache and bow tie, looking intensely at the camera.]()The Great Train Robbery

more sophisticated, the camera quickly became a mobile storytelling tool and was used to great effect in numerous silent films. But this all came to a screeching halt when sound was introduced in 1927, which took the moving camera and regressed it to a static position for close to a decade, because the camera had to be “blimped” (encased in a sound-deadening box) so that the microphones wouldn’t pick up the camera noise. By the late 1930s sound equipment greatly improved and portable blimping devices made the camera mobile again. Some say that the integration of sound on film pushed the evolution of cinema back several decades due to the fact that once sound was introduced, a new style of filmmaking was needed.

In the early days of cinema filmmakers did not have the various devices and equipment available to filmmakers today. Everything they came up with had to be invented. There was no Steadicam, dolly, jib, or crane — all these tools would be invented by cinematographers and their technicians as the means to solve particular logistical problems for a shot they were trying to create.

Just as in the early days of cinema, student filmmakers and low- to no-budget filmmakers must be resourceful when creating moving shots. Without the assistance of dollies, jibs, cranes, or any other devices, there are still some basic moves that the camera operator can do to keep the movement of the film flowing. These are moves that can be done with just the camera and a tripod. Just as in the silent film days, the lack of equipment stimulates resourcefulness. Well thought out and creative use of these basic moves can still provide a sense of movement to your film.

## THE PAN

This is a horizontal movement of the camera that is locked down to a tripod in a static position. With the head of the tripod loose in pan mode the camera can do a sweeping move from left to right or right to left. It is a move that can show the width of the screen and all that is visible. Pans can be used to follow a subject from one side of the frame to the other or can be used in combination with a tilt. All that is needed to create this move is a camera and a good fluid or gear head tripod. Generally the pan is a more objective movement recording what is happening by following a character from point A to point B. It is a shot that lacks depth, since it is moves horizontally and is often used in combination with other moves.

## THE TILT

This is a vertical move where the camera is locked down to the tripod but the head is loose enough to smoothly move up and down. This can be used to tilt up from the feet of an actor to their face or to tilt up as an actor moves from a sitting position to a standing position, but the camera is not moving from where it is situated — just the head of the tripod allows this movement to take place. One could tilt down as an actor goes from a standing to sitting position. Used in combination with pans, one could follow the actor across the room, tilt down as they sit, tilt

up as they stand and follow them out of the room. A tilt does provide movement within the frame. Like the pan, it is a move that is not creating any depth, because the camera is staying in the same objective position. What is needed for this move is also a good gear head or fluid head tripod.

## THE ZOOM

This creates an illusion of movement without any real movement at all. The camera is locked down to the tripod and the zoom lens can bring the subject either much closer to the viewer or much further away depending on whether it is zooming in or out. “Zooming in” brings the subject from a long shot to a close-up, but since it is zooming in, it is also flattening out the background, making it become soft focused and diminishing the depth of field, what is visible in the background. “Zooming out,” you may go from a close-up to a long shot, which will have the affect of moving away from the subject, although the camera is not moving at all — the lens is going from telephoto to normal to wide angle. This has the effect of pushing the background away while increasing the depth of field as you zoom out. When you want to move in on a moment, the zoom is a device that will give the frame a sense of movement where none exists. Cinematographers’ opinions vary greatly on whether or not to use zooms, but they are frequently used in combination with dolly moves, which hide the movement of the zoom. (See page 45 for an example of a zoom out from *The Graduate*.)

## THE TRACKING SHOT

With a tracking shot the camera is moving in a vehicle either away from the subject, following the subject, or moving beside the subject. The camera could be mounted on a high hat, sand-bagged to weigh it down, and placed on the back of a convertible with the top down. The camera operator holds onto the camera and shoots while the car drives away and the subject being photographed runs toward the car. Another way a basic tracking move is created is from the vehicle slowly driving beside the subject with the camera either shooting out of the car window or from the side of a van or sunroof, whatever is available on the vehicle you are using. This is a low-budget way of creating movement in your film. There are many devices that have been created to support the camera while an actor is driving a car being towed by the camera truck and the camera is “tracking” the move. This type of shot could be used to follow a subject down the street or as they run toward you or away from you. It is still more of an objective point of view, as the viewer is watching the movement rather than participating in the movement.

Technically a dolly move is also a tracking shot, since it can also be moving beside or in front of the subject. But dolly moves use a different terminology such as “dolly in” and “dolly out,” rather than tracking. To dolly in, the camera will push toward the subject, who may be stationary or may be moving away from the

camera. To dolly out, the camera mounted on the dolly will pull back as the subject moves toward the camera. Technically both are tracking moves.

## HANDHELD

This form of movement takes the camera off of the tripod and mobilizes it into the scene. The effect is somewhat shaky, since the camera operator cannot completely stabilize the movement and the camera may exaggerate the movements of the camera operator as they walk or swish pan to follow an actor. Since the camera is not locked down, the horizon line may become skewed. This type of movement creates a tension or confusion in the scene. It is a loose movement that until recently had been used exclusively in documentary filmmaking.

The handheld camera is quite different from one that is locked down to a tripod, because the camera is on the shoulder of the operator and can follow actors around. The movement gives the scene more energy and creates a subjective perspective that involves the audience in the action. Therefore it differs from the previous movements described. The decision to go handheld is going to change the movement from an objective point of view to a subjective one, and should be used for that reason. The handheld camera involves the audience in the story from an emotional standpoint.

![Two black and white stills from the movie Jarhead. The top image shows a soldier in close-up, looking distressed, with smoke or dust in the background. The bottom image shows several soldiers walking across a desolate landscape, possibly a beach or field, with a vehicle in the distance.]()Jarhead

■ We basically shot Jarhead handheld. I love shooting handheld, but it might not be right for another movie. We talked about shooting Valley of Elah handheld, but didn't because we decided a much more classic and natural feel would work better — handheld might have been too active and drawn attention to itself for that film. So it varies, I don't really have a preference [for what kind of movie], although I love operating and love shooting handheld. But you don't want to restrict your mind to any one way of working.

(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

Another question for the director to consider: Is the camera moving or is the actor moving in front of a static camera? If the camera is static, the movement takes place in front of the camera with actors moving within the frame. Good blocking and choreography of the actors can make an otherwise static scene quite interesting. In *Manhattan* (1979), directed by Woody Allen with cinematography by Gordon Willis, there is a scene where the camera is static in the apartment, but the actors move from one room to another while the camera observes. At times dialogue takes place off screen forcing the viewer to listen to what is going on. Visually the camera has made the audience objective observers waiting in the other room. This type of movement involves blocking the actors in front of a static camera that may not move at all or may pan or tilt to accommodate the movement of the actors. It gives the audience the feeling of being a voyeur more than a participant in the action. In the example above, the viewer is left in the room listening to what is going on, but is not involved in the action itself. It forces the viewer to listen to the dialogue and to watch from an objective standpoint, somewhat like the way one would watch a play. The static camera takes the objective stance of the audience watching. Actors can move all around the frame, the camera can pan, tilt, and zoom, all while the camera is on sticks in a static position. (See example on page 161.)

■ You take a master like Woody Allen or Ingmar Bergman, who brought the actors from the foreground to the background with a static camera, but the actors are moving. You take a look at a film like *Manhattan*, which takes place in a small apartment: All the action is happening in the foreground and the background and back into the foreground. An auteur like Woody Allen could avoid the conventional coverage and shoot a scene as one shot, because they know where they can place the camera to single-shot scenes and commit to them. Now that I've been on more studio films, I see that's rare. So many people need coverage, and it gets tedious, and the camera movement is just used as another tool and technique to get you out with more interesting coverage.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

![A dark, dimly lit interior scene, likely from the movie Manhattan, showing a bookshelf and a person standing in the background.]()Manhattan

![Four sequential frames from the movie Manhattan, showing a scene where the camera is static. The frames depict a man standing in a doorway, looking out into a city street, and later, a close-up of a woman (likely Annie Hall) looking towards the camera.]()Manhattan: In this scene the camera is completely static as the actor continues his dialogue off screen. In effect, the audience members become voyeurs, intently listening and waiting in the next room.

A camera that is moving — whether it is on a dolly, handheld, or on a Steadicam — provides more of an involvement on the part of the viewer. With the camera moving, the viewer is taken into the action and experiences it just as the actors are experiencing it. If they are fighting and the camera is handheld, we are in there fighting with the characters, almost taking the hits, such as in *Raging Bull* or *Fight Club*. If the characters are dancing and the camera is swirling around, then the viewer is dancing, too. If the actor is drunk and the movement is handheld and off kilter, then the viewer is drunk and not seeing clearly, either. A moving camera takes on a subjective point of view, involving the audience in the action of the story. The moving camera can also provide various points of view, perspectives of one or more of the characters. The viewer can see what they see, and subsequently feel what they feel.

Sometimes camera movement is really born out of the situation at hand. There's a stylized camera movement that takes you from one place to another and there is movement that services the blocking of the actors. I'm a big fan of both. The shots that stylize the film are more predetermined, but the shots that are more motivated by the movement of the actors are maintenance within the body of coverage. That will allow you to create an energy to the scene, because the actors are able to move. It can be a static shot that shows the whole space and everybody walking back and forth or a shot that dollies from one place to another. Those two styles of master shots are very different and one is born out of the blocking of the actors.  
(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

Camera movement has become very apparent in films and episodic television shows, often setting the tone of the story. In traditional motion pictures, movement is used in a much more functional way. The stylized camera may not be clearly underlining the essence of the story, but is used to create energy on the screen. An action film may be shot handheld from start to finish to underscore the tension and energy of the characters involved or to create an immediacy to the moment, a pseudo-documentary feeling. Some might say the stylized use of camera movement can be distracting from the storytelling process of the film. A director should not strive for a pretentious camera, one that the viewer is constantly aware of, because the camera is there to tell the story unobtrusively and add energy when it is a part of the story itself. The audience should not really be aware of the movement; it should just come naturally out of the scene.

To move the camera just for the sake of moving, that I don't understand. There should be a dramatic or conceptual reason to move. All you are trying to do with movies is impart information, tell a story. So everything should be oriented to telling the story, each shot should tell the audience some piece of information. So that camera should be motivated when it moves by something in the frame. Or motivated by the concept, such as in horror movies to build suspense. To just be moving the camera — anybody can do that. It doesn't tell the story.  
(Richard Crudo, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

There are some directors — Martin Scorsese, Orson Welles, Stanley Kubrick, and the Coen brothers, just to mention a few — who are known for the brilliant

use of their camera movement. There are many other directors who use movement for specific emphasis and there are others who overuse it and therefore may negate its impact.

■ *Movement depends on the director and where the director is coming from. I will always have a suggestion if I feel it's the right thing to do. Let's put it this way: I wouldn't want to work on a film where I couldn't have a suggestion, or I don't know what I'd be getting from it. I don't mean I want my suggestion to always be taken, but I want to at least have the opportunity to say it. As long as I can have that idea, I'm happy. Some directors would say "no, this is it, and you have no input on what the camera does." On some films I like to see the blocking of the scene and design a few shots and talk to the director about it. So it really depends on how they like to work.*

(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

Generally camera movement should be motivated, whether it is a subjective motivation, such as something a character has realized internally — for example, that the person they thought was their lover is actually a murderer — or a physical, external motivation, like following an actor from one place to another. This type of camera movement is more functional and is used to serve the movements of the actors as they walk around a room (panning) or stand up from a seated position (tilting). In combination with a zoom lens, a director could go in for a tighter shot when the moment becomes more intense by simply zooming slowly and not moving the camera at all or zooming while the camera gently pushes in on a dolly, which brings the viewer in closer to the action.

Any movement or lack of movement is an aesthetic visual choice and should underscore the story itself. Lately it has become fashionable to go handheld for aesthetic reasons and this has become an accepted practice in numerous contemporary films and television shows. But keep in mind that a technique that becomes overused tends to lose its impact.

A handheld camera was used by Woody Allen in *Husbands and Wives* (1992) to represent the tension of two crumbling marriages, but it was rejected by audiences at the time who felt “sick” watching the film, with some walking out of the theater unable to complete viewing the film. Allen was simply ahead of his time with that concept, which worked brilliantly to provide the chaotic emotional roller coaster of these two couples. To balance out the handheld work he also intercut static interviews of the couples with the handheld footage to provide a breather for the audience.

Today's handheld films don't bother to give us a break and will continue to be handheld throughout. For example, a film like Catherine Hardwicke's *Thirteen* uses the handheld camera technique to underscore the tension building between a mother and daughter as the film reaches its climax. As the daughter spirals out of control, the handheld camerawork is exaggerated as well. It represents the subjective turmoil and confusion of the characters and works well within the context of the story.

Very often directors choose to go handheld during an argument sequence, because the handheld energy adds to the tension within a scene that involves action or aggression. Movement can be used to create energy within a scene but will lose its impact if it is not used in combination with static images. If something is moving around the frame and suddenly stops, it forces the viewer to really look at what the camera is focused on. In contrast, if something is static for long periods of time and then finally moves, it creates energy in the scene.

Experimental films played with this concept decades ago. They would hold on a scene without moving the camera or turning it off until the film rolled out, or would hold on a door until suddenly it started to open, then cut to black. It was a minimalist concept meant to challenge the audience. But in the entertainment industry, what we watch is meant to engage us in the characters and in the story, and where the camera is in a particular scene or whether it is moving or static is an essential storytelling tool for the director to utilize.

![Black and white photograph showing a woman looking forward, with motion blur in the background, suggesting movement or handheld camera work.]()![Black and white photograph showing two women in an indoor setting. One woman is lying down, smiling, while the other woman stands over her, gently touching her head.]()Thirteen

Audiences today are more accustomed to a handheld camera and will readily accept it since it is used on episodic television programs as well as theatrically released films. Many television programs use a constantly moving camera; *ER* has been using Steadicam for almost the entire show for years to give the audience the sense of urgency in the Emergency Room. Many other programs have followed suit with either Steadicam movement or handheld camera movement to provide an energy and immediacy to their programs. The handheld camera doing a swish pan gives a documentary feel that implies urgency, as if it the event is happening at that moment.

*Studio 60 on the Sunset Strip* and *The West Wing* used the “walk and talk” sequences that Aaron Sorkin has become known for to cover long pages of dialogue in one long moving shot. Stylistically, this is what audiences have come to expect from these types of dramatic episodes and they have adapted to this style of shooting.

■ Camera movement is a way to keep the actors in the scene longer. If the scene is three pages long, it would be nice if you could get the whole thing in one shot and make the shot count. If you move the camera and make each shot and each performance count, then you are engaging those actors to give it to you right then and there. Then you are actually getting the scene and you're in it.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

There are numerous kinds of camera moves and they are all quite different. Each suit a unique situation, from dolly moves, crane moves, jib moves, handheld moves, pans and tilts, tracking shots, and Steadicam shots. The movement in a scene can be carefully planned in preproduction or occur spontaneously on set.

Some cinematographers like to watch the blocking during the rehearsal and then make suggestions to the director regarding camera movement, while other directors may have a very specific idea of how they want a scene covered. The important thing to remember is that the movement should be motivated, whether the motivation is physical or psychological. There should be a reason for the camera movement to further progress the story, whether it is a subjective move or a purely functional move, to get the actors from one point to another.

■ There's an enormous difference between following somebody handheld and following somebody with a Steadicam or on a dolly with a crane. You really need to know why you are doing one of these moves. A lot of the time practicality comes into it. You don't always get the Chapman Titan crane; you might have to do it handheld, because it's more expedient. Every time you pick up a camera, you are somewhat compromising what you do and you are trying to do. If you pan on a long lens, it's a very different look than tracking with somebody, there's a very different feel to it.

(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

It is important to consider the style and genre of the film and the movement that best suits the tone of the film you are making. Consider whether it has a high-energy tension throughout, like an action film, or whether it is a slow undulating film that gently draws the viewer into the story.

An important consideration with movement is the contrast of it. When the camera is continually moving and then suddenly stops, the audience becomes very aware of what they are now looking at in the frame. The fact that the movement has stopped conveys information on a subliminal level and forces the audience to pay close attention to what the camera is now paused on. A long dolly shot that brings the viewer into a room and suddenly stops to introduce us to a character is saying to the viewer, here is someone important. In contrast to this, if a film is primarily still and then suddenly moves, the audience is alerted to information the director is providing with the movement, shifting the energy of the film.

An audience will get accustomed to whatever style of the film they are presented with, so constant movement is best highlighted with periods of stillness to accent it. If an actor is running down the street, it makes sense for the camera to be tracking along with him, but if two people are sitting quietly talking or looking into one another's eyes, the camera might be still and listen to the conversation. A camera moving around during this quiet scene could imply a tension between the characters or that they are so engaged with each other that the world outside their peripheral vision doesn't exist. A director should consider why the camera is moving in a scene and whether or not the movement more effectively tells the story. There should be a concept behind the use of movement to cover a scene.

■ *Lately I've noticed some of the films today are handheld and you get to a scene where there is quite important dialogue between two people and they can't keep the camera still. They feel they have to walk around them in circles and they think that is visual energy. I disagree, I think in a movie that has a lot of action — that's where you move the camera, that's where you get your visual energy, but when you have to record plump lines, why not stop it gently and listen, so the audience is forced to listen to what the characters are saying.*  
(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

## THE TOOLS OF MOVEMENT

### THE CRANE SHOT

There are numerous types of cranes available to filmmakers today and they vary in terms of size and cost. A large Titan crane should be considered for a very specific type of move. It is not just sitting around the set in case the director is suddenly inspired to use it. A crane may not be in the budget for all films or may only be used for one or two specific shots. A well-utilized crane shot may open a film or close a film or may be used for a specific shot that with careful blocking will convey a mood or specific information to the viewer.

■ *I think as a cinematographer when you are young, you are more about moves and composition, but as it takes so much time to set up, then light becomes more and more important to you. My work in music videos in the '80s and '90s gave me the chance to work on lighting and more complicated crane shots.*  
(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

![A mobile crane (Chapman Leonard) mounted on a small, four-wheeled vehicle, shown with its boom extended and angled upwards.]()A crane (Chapman Leonard)

![A large truck chassis (Chapman Leonard) fitted with a long boom crane, driving across a flat, open landscape.]()Crane on truck

Probably the most famous example of a crane shot is in the opening sequence of Orson Welles' film *Touch of Evil* (1958). The entire opening sequence runs about two and a half minutes and is created by one very well conceived and executed crane shot. The choreography of this opening sequence shot by cinematographer Russell Metty, ASC, brings the audience into the story by introducing the two main characters walking through the streets of Tijuana as well as the bomb that eventually explodes in a car that drives past the strolling couple, which is the catalyst which begins the action of the story. The movement is utilized so there is no dead space in the shot and the shot is used in its entirety. Many factors had to fall into place in order to make the shot work, including the blocking of the actors, the movement of the crane, the car driving, and the camera movement itself. A great deal of time and expense goes into the planning of a well-executed crane shot.

Another excellent use of a crane shot is in Alfred Hitchcock's *Notorious* in the party scene where Alicia (Ingrid Bergman) has the key to the wine cellar in her

![A black and white photograph showing a scene from Touch of Evil, likely the opening sequence. A car is driving past a building entrance, and a figure is visible near the car.]()![A black and white photograph showing a scene from Touch of Evil, likely the party scene. A woman (Alicia) is standing near a table, and a man is visible in the foreground.]()Touch of Evil

![Black and white film still showing a crowded ballroom scene with people dancing. A chandelier is visible overhead.]()![Black and white film still showing a close-up of a person's foot in a white shoe stepping onto a checkered floor.]()![Black and white film still showing a close-up of a hand holding a small object, possibly a cigarette or cigarette holder, near the waistline of a person wearing a light skirt.]()Notorious

hand and has to pass it off nonchalantly to Devlin (Cary Grant). The shot begins with a wide-angle view of the party scene and ends with a close-up of the key in her hand. Tension is created when her evil husband (played by Claude Raines) grabs both of her hands, keeping in line with the Hitchcock rule that the size of something in the frame should equal its importance in the scene at the time. This key must be passed along to Devlin, but the potential discovery of the key by Alicia's husband at that moment would destroy the plan that eventually implicates him in Nazi espionage. The crane shot covers the scene with one sweeping move that drives the story forward.

Crane shots generally provide a high angle view of a situation before coming in closer to establish the environment. An example would be a panoramic view of a town as a car drives into the shot, and then the crane moves down to follow that car as it passes through the town, as it does in *Far From Heaven* (2002) and *All That Heaven Allows* (1954). In both of these films, the opening crane shot establishes the season as well as the quaint little town in which the story takes place, while introducing us to the main character in the film as she interacts with people in the town. In these films the crane shot is used as an establishing shot before it moves in closer for details. The opposite shot would be to start off with a detail of someone or something and then crane away from them, leaving the character small and helpless at the end. In *Far From Heaven*, the crane shots are used to bookend the film. It opens with a crane shot bringing us into the story and closes as the main character gets into her car and drives away alone; the crane then takes us out of the story. As the crane ascends it creates an omnipresent perspective, pulling away from the story on the surface level and providing a bird's-eye view.

Technically a crane is a device which is used to create large vertical moves in one shot, including going below the level of its mounting surface if needed. It is able to go places that would be difficult for a dolly or a camera mounted on a tripod. Chapman Leonard has helped to create special crane shots with directors who want to take them beyond their usual establishing shot abilities. Renting a crane is not inexpensive and generally will not be seen in low-budget productions. It also should be used for specific shots to help tell the story more effectively. There are many different types of cranes available today. Their size depends on how much of an elevation the device can crane up, 10' or 25', and if it is moving or static.

In the early days we had one of those great big Titan cranes, and we used it out in the desert on *The Hitcher*; you're 27' up in the air and it's a buzz, you know? You crane down, and the kid walks out of the door, and you crane down, and you crane down more, and when you boil it down, the editor is going to sit there and say, "the top of the shot is great, because it's the desert and the kid is walking out," and then starts that interminable drop, and the editor has got his scissors out and he's snoring, waiting for the shot to end and sure enough — snip, snip, he cuts the whole middle section out, so you've got the top shot when the camera starts to move and then cut to the bottom part, where there is movement again, so it's ugly. But when you are out there in the desert, and you say "what if we just do the top shot, because the editor will probably only use the top shot, five seconds, and bam out of that, keep moving." Then everyone would say

"what, are you crazy? We've got this beautiful crane out here, so let's do this magic crane shot." So as you go through life, you watch your past films and think, you've done this 155' tracking shot only to find there is only 10' of it left in the movie. So then it's indulgent; it cost a lot of money to do, and it's not even in the movie. So the moral is, if it's not going to end up in the movie, you shouldn't shoot it!

(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

There are different types of cranes used in films today and different companies that make them. The Akela crane allowed John Toll, ASC, to obtain some unusual, low-to-the-ground shots that moved through the tall grass of a Guadalcanal battlefield in the World War II drama *The Thin Red Line* (1999), directed by Terrence Malick.

■ The whole idea of using that crane was to not make it feel like a crane. We wanted it to look like the most continuous smooth dolly that had ever been built. The 85'-long Akela arm is so long that it really minimizes the arc of the move, so you can actually make it feel like you are traveling in a straight line.

(John Toll, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, October 2003, 85)

## THE DOLLY

■ There is a precision to the dolly shot. You can get in and out of scenes a lot easier on a dolly and you can hold at the end if the dialogue is going on for another two pages. You don't want to be on a Steadicam [for something like that], because even a great operator who can hold his horizon is still going to float if you are trying to be static. There is a precision to the dolly move; there is an elegance to it that is just different.

(Nancy Schreiber, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

Studio dollies were used back in 1940s, but smaller dollies made for location shooting were not available until the early 1970s. The Hustler dolly, developed by Chapman Leonard, has the ability to be used on or off a track and has a hydraulic lift system. Today there are many different types of location dollies used for a variety of situations.

The dolly takes the camera off the stability of sticks and onto the stability of a moving device. It can be used on tracks that are laid down for smooth movement that is either straight, semicircular or circular, or off tracks with different wheels on a smooth surface. The dolly move can be used alone or in combination with zoom lenses or hydro lift devices, which can elevate the camera as it moves on tracks in either a forward or backward motion or in a circular or semicircular motion. If the dolly is used outdoors or on an uneven floor the tracks must be laid down and wedges put in to keep the tracks level. Dolly grips are trained to push the dolly, camera operator, assistant camera, and even the director at the correct speed. Dollies are considered standard equipment on film and television sets today. Cinematographers love to have a dolly available because they can be used to cover longer scenes of dialogue with movement that can be used as one long master shot.

![A Chapman Leonard dolly, a four-wheeled camera carriage with a large, articulated arm and a handle for movement.]()A dolly (Chapman Leonard)

![A Chapman Leonard dolly with a camera mounted on the articulated arm, showing the camera body and viewfinder.]()Dolly with camera mounted (Chapman Leonard)

There are numerous films that effectively used the dolly for stylistic purposes like Quentin Tarantino's *Reservoir Dogs* (1993) or Spike Lee's floating dolly shots in *Do The Right Thing* (1988), where the actor actually steps up on the edge of the dolly while it is pulled back — giving the character the appearance of floating.

Cinematographers like the precision of the dolly's movement, since it can be carefully choreographed to hit specific marks. For the cinematographer who likes to operate the camera, they continue to do so while on the dolly. Besides the movement of the dolly, which can be quite fluid, the camera can also be raised or lowered on a hydraulic lift system, or used in combination with a zoom lens.

■ *With the dolly or crane, I can get the camera going on a very precise path. The reason this is so important is because the lighting style I like to use is cross light, so I tend to shoot with wider lenses than someone more conservative would. So often the lights are right on the edge of the frame. When the camera starts moving, for sure they will be on the edge of the frame, so I don't want the camera movement to completely destroy what I am doing with light. With the dolly I can go precisely where I want to go.*

(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

A well-known use of the dolly-zoom combination occurs in Martin Scorsese's *Goodfellas*, photographed by Michael Ballhaus, ASC. There is a scene toward the end of the film where Ray Liotta's character meets with De Niro and realizes that if he doesn't confess to the cops and go into witness protection, he will be eliminated. The shot tries to maintain the composition of a two shot, while the camera dollies back and zooms in at the same time, the effect makes the background come closer to the subject, due to the zoom and shifting depth of field, which creates a sense of the world closing in on the character. It is a move that connotes subliminal messages to the viewer regarding what is going on emotionally in the story — which makes it a very effective, although somewhat stylized, camera move. (See page 174.)

## THE DIFFERENCE BETWEEN THE DOLLY AND THE ZOOM

Given that both the dolly and the zoom can move from a long shot to a close-up, what is the difference between them? The primary difference is that a zoom is a product of a long lens coming from a static camera mounted on a tripod. As the lens zooms in toward the subject, the background starts to push forward while becoming soft focus. By the time the zoom is completed, the background will be entirely out of focus as the subject now fills the frame. The zoom in tells the audience to look only at the subject in the shot.

On the other hand, the dolly will actually be moving the camera toward the subject, so the background will not have such a radical shift in depth of field. Things will actually shift but still remain in focus in the background, which adds a feeling of motion to the shot. The background at the end of the dolly in could be quite different than it was in the beginning of the shot. If there is more than one

![Three sequential frames from the movie Goodfellas showing two men sitting at a diner booth. The frames illustrate a camera movement where the lens zooms in and the dolly pulls back, maintaining the two-shot but making the background appear closer.]()Goodfellas: Notice how the background appears to move closer as the lens zooms in and the dolly pulls back while maintaining the two shot.

subject in the shot, the dolly will have a more rapidly shifting perspective to the foreground subjects than to the background subjects. As the camera dollies in, the actors in the foreground will move out of the frame quickly as the camera passes them and goes closer to the subject of the shot. Meanwhile, the background actors will be visible from the beginning of the shot to the end of the shot and remain in reasonable focus.

If there were two subjects in the zoom shot, the focus could rack from one subject to the other as the focal length shifts. This is a completely different look than is achieved with the dolly. So it is important for a director to understand the differences for aesthetic reasons.

## THE STEADICAM

The Steadicam was created by Garrett Brown in the 1970s and first used by him on the biopic *Bound for Glory*, directed by Hal Ashby (1976). It combines the stabilized look of a conventionally mounted camera, maintaining an even horizon line, with the fluid motion of a dolly shot and the flexibility of a handheld camera. It allows the camera to go places where a dolly can't fit, like going down stairs or outside on rugged terrain, or running through woods. With its armature and a balancing device that gives it a more fluid motion, the Steadicam is designed to absorb the jerky movements of a handheld camera.

The Steadicam operator wears a harness that is attached to an iso-elastic arm, which is connected by a gimbal to the Steadicam armature, with the camera mounted at one end and a counterbalance weight at the other. The counterbalance includes the battery pack and a monitor. The monitor substitutes for the camera's viewfinder, since the range of motion of the camera relative to the operator makes the camera's own viewfinder unusable. In the film industry the armature and weight are traditionally called the "sled," because they resembled a sled in the early model of the Steadicam. (<http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/steadicam>)

![A black and white photograph of a Steadicam operator wearing a harness and operating the camera rig.]()A Steadicam

■ Howard Gould, who directed *The Six Wives of Henry Lefay*, did not want to use the Steadicam. His opinion was of the floaty look with horizon lines that were not straight. I knew I had a Steadicam operator that was really good, but still Howard was very reticent about it. But after he saw the rehearsal, he was thrilled, because my operator BJ McDonald was just so talented, that it didn't feel like the curse of the bad Steadicam. There was a time when the Steadicam was overused a lot, so there is a time and place for it. Sometimes it's faster if you have to set up a whole dance floor, but other times there is nothing like a dolly. Choreography of a moving camera is a joint effort and some directors are savvier than others. Often that is when I tend to bring in other movies for references, turn off the sound, and look at the shots with the novice director — that's useful.

(Nancy Schreiber, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

Another early use of the Steadicam is in the famous montage workout scene in the first *Rocky* (1976), which culminates in the scene where he runs up the steps of the Philadelphia museum and waves his hands in the air. The Steadicam's smooth movements were captured as the operator ran up the stairs with Rocky; shooting handheld would have been much too shaky. The Steadicam was also used in the boxing ring for some of the fight sequences. The early use of the Steadicam in Stanley Kubrick's *The Shining* (1980), effectively achieved extremely low angle tracking shots of the boy riding his big-wheel tricycle through the long corridors of the hotel. This was the first use of the Steadicam in low mode, which allowed the device to achieve a look that was not possible before.

■ I wasn't in love with Steadicam until *Inside Man*, where we used it a lot, and that was because it worked for the story. I was able to take one character and create a language with his camera and take another character and create his language with the other camera. It was born out of necessity. I had established Denzel Washington's character with a handheld multi-camera feel, so to juxtapose that to Clive Owen's character, I had to come up with something that moved through the space as a single camera feel and Steadicam was the call.
