It ended up working really well. It's taking your technique and giving it a purpose. Steadicam is just one of those techniques. To me it's like pushing your film or shooting reversal and cross processing or silhouetting everybody — it's just one of the techniques you use.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

The Steadicam is a liberating device that enables the camera to go places where it never could before, following actors down narrow hallways, running through the woods or moving through large crowd scenes. It provides a unique movement that is quite different from dolly or handheld moves, yet has the effect of being handheld because of where it can go. The Steadicam shot creates a more fluid movement with a stable horizon line. It can turn 360 degrees, follow another actor, or easily go up and down stairs. It can have a floating ethereal movement or a more stable handheld movement. The Steadicam has been used in numerous music videos since stylistically they were always looking for something a little different, and the Steadicam was a fairly new device in the early '80s, when music videos evolved with the creation of MTV. In essence the look and style of the music videos had a

![Black and white still from The Shining showing a low-angle shot of a child in a wheelchair moving down a long, brightly lit corridor.]()![Black and white still from The Shining showing a low-angle shot of a child in a wheelchair moving through a dining area with chairs and a rug.]()The Shining: A low-angle shot on a Steadicam follows the subject through the long corridors.

![Black and white still from Inside Man showing two men in vests walking forward, with police officers visible in the background.]()Inside Man

huge impact on the look and feel of feature films and commercials that have followed. Not only in the way footage was covered with moving shots but also the editing style that we have now grown so accustomed to, such as quick cuts and overlapping story lines.

■ On 8 Mile we did some Steadicam shots that would have been too jarring if they were handheld, so I made the Steadicam look a little harder than it usually would but smoother than handheld. I had the operator hold the handle a little stronger to get more of a handheld look.  
(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

A trained Steadicam operator, not the cinematographer, always operates the Steadicam rig. So the cinematographer watches the Steadicam operator's movements through a clamshell monitor, at times whispering to them about the composition. So there are some cinematographers who are more reluctant to have a prolonged use of Steadicam shots, because it takes them away from the camera.

■ I think Steadicam is overused, although it's a great tool to achieve particular effects. But when I see it used as a tool just to save time, it seems like the director doesn't have a concept and hopes the Steadicam operator will find the shot. So in that sense, it's a denial of a director's responsibilities [laughs]. You can do it, but it's a compromise. Handheld, I'm doing it myself, so I feel I have control over the image, of when I move and where I move. With a Steadicam operator, I'd always have a clamshell and walk next to the operator, whispering in his ear, "it's a bit too tight here" or whatever. I'm a bit of a pest, really.  
(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

Steadicam is frequently used in television and in motion pictures unless the director and cinematographer are opposed to using it for stylistic reasons. There are many cinematographers who would prefer to shoot handheld or on a dolly and operate the camera themselves, but it is not always their choice. When the director decides that the Steadicam shot is essential, the cinematographer will become closely involved regarding composition and where the lens is pointing through the use of a handheld monitor.

■ I would rather trade the Steadicam days for crane days — I like that device better. I prefer the precision of a camera on a dolly or a crane... [not to say that there isn't some very precise Steadicam work being done]. The lighting side of the cinematographer in me doesn't like the Steadicam guy because they can go anyplace, so they do, and they are either shooting lights or creating camera shadows.  
(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

■ Usually I'm not a big fan of Steadicam, because I feel that it's sort of a loose look, and I like more precise framing. That's why I like handheld, because you can be loose with the camera but still much more precise. I find that sometimes when I have to swish pan to something and find exact framing, I can do so easier being handheld than on a tripod.  
(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ I don't appreciate Steadicam as a quick fix to a problem. Steadicam is not meant to stand still for two thirds of the shot; it's meant to walk around. I have a problem when it becomes a directorial convenience because of time. But of course there are those moments when you panic, because you're trying to make the day, so you throw the gloves down, the meter goes away, and everything is a 5.6, and you run. I think that Steadicam can be valuable. If you are a responsible cinematographer and you are doing justice to the story, then it can be a great tool. If you are just not making your day and it's a panic tool, then you have far worse problems than just the Steadicam.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

■ I don't really like the Steadicam if it is used just because of the way it behaves. I try to use it where you can't put a dolly or just can't do it handheld, and then you have to use the Steadicam. So I try to pick an operator who will make it look like a dolly or keep it as precise as possible. I just don't like "here's the Steadicam" shot, so I try to hide it as much as possible. Some directors like to use it a lot and feel it's very freeing, and if you have a good operator, like George Richmond, then maybe so. There are many shots in Alexander that are Steadicam, and you would never know.

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ I don't usually use Steadicam; I don't think I have used one on anything theatrical. I may use it for a music video sometimes. My attitude is pretty bad about Steadicam. I don't like top light, even though I have seen fabulous top light work. I'll throw one up and inevitably take it down and light from the side. I feel that Steadicam is like that. I've come across situations in music videos where the director says, "if I can just get a Steadicam, I don't have to decide what to do. I just tell the Steadicam operator to go shoot me something cool." But just to make a camera glide, I say, get a dolly — we can make the camera glide.

(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

■ I've used it quite a lot; it's a great tool. But it's not the be-all and end-all. Most directors I've worked with recently dislike it immensely. If you say to the Steadicam operator, stick a 50mm lens on, they'll go 50? This is not going to make them look good. They'd rather put the 21mm on and float through the room like a helicopter. But that's why directors don't want them, because they don't want the camera looking like a helicopter inside the room. So they say to me, like Minghella... or the commercial director: "I don't want Steadicam." They don't want it, but I've used it, and I've had to argue with the pilot about putting a 50mm on. He'd say, "no, a 28mm," and I'd argue "nothing shorter than a 50mm or it won't cut!"

If all the tools are there, let's go. Like when I was operating on *The English Patient* and *Cold Mountain*, I used a mini jib. It's a little tiny jib, about 6' long, and you put the camera on that, and I can go anywhere on that. I can swing down and up and around. I used that in practically every shot in *Cold Mountain*, even inside. We'd punch a hole in the wall and I could gently move this little crane and swing it around. Anthony said very nicely at the end of the movie, "That little piece of machinery gave me a 20% better film than I would have thought," because it gives the operator the opportunity to be on the same wavelength as the director like in the old days, when they would talk with the director, not like today when they are sitting at the monitors.

(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

## WHAT A DIRECTOR SHOULD KNOW ABOUT CAMERA MOVEMENT

1. Know what the motivation is for moving the camera, whether it is a functional move, such as getting the actor across the room, or a subjective move that is providing information to the viewer about the emotional state of the character.
2. Understand the basic types of moves and the time it takes to execute them. For example, if you are planning a long dolly move, carefully block it out and rehearse it. The equipment will have to be ordered in advance, so you should know why you are doing that particular move. It will take longer to shoot a long dolly shot, but a great deal of dialogue could be covered with the shot. It must be scheduled into the day.
3. Understand the difference between what a zoom will give you, what a dolly will give you, and what a handheld camera move will give you, from both a stylistic and an aesthetic perspective.
4. If you are using a crane shot, plan it out so that most of it does not end up cut out of the movie!
5. Do not do movement just for the sake of moving the camera, make sure there is a reason behind the movement in a shot, whether it is a subjective (internally motivated) move or an objective (a more functional) move.
6. Communicate with your cinematographer about the feeling you want to convey in the film. Explain your visual interpretation of the script and listen to the cinematographer's visual interpretation of the script. She or he may have some great ideas for you!
7. Invite the cinematographer to observe you while you work with the blocking of the actors. Sometimes this is an ideal time for the cinematographer to get ideas about how movement may help to cover the scene effectively. One long dolly move could be your master shot, if it is well planned.
8. If you are using Steadicam, make sure you are using it for the right reasons, not just to have it in the film. The Steadicam is best used in situations where a dolly can't fit or handheld is too loose. Talk to the cinematographer about the selection of a Steadicam operator.
9. Remember that movies are motion pictures, so think about movement within scenes to keep the momentum going or to transition from one scene to another. Movement works best when there are moments of stillness to highlight it and vice versa.
10. To get ideas about movement, you could sit down with the cinematographer and watch a film's movement with the sound turned off. There are many great films to study, depending on the type of movement that would best serve your story and your vision.

## VARIOUS FORMATS: “THE CANVAS”

*like having a variety of formats to choose from; they all serve a purpose. I don't like the decision born out of economic necessity. If it works for the story, so be it.*  
(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

There are many different formats to shoot on today and selecting one is a decision that the director and cinematographer should make together. The most basic question to consider may be whether to shoot film or digital. But what aspect ratio of film and what type of digital? What are the aesthetic reasons for selecting one format over another? What will film give you that digital will not and vice versa? Selecting the format to shoot your movie on is a decision that should best serve the project. What works well for one type of film may not work quite as well for another.

The decision should not just be made for arbitrary reasons (“I just want to shoot digitally”) or economic ones (“I have no money so I have to shoot on mini-DV”), although economic reasons can be a strong consideration. What your budget can support should be realistically factored into the decision. If you have a budget of under a million, it is pretty safe to say you will not be shooting IMAX film.

There is a misconception that shooting digitally will cost less than shooting 35mm; this is not necessarily true if you are using the professional formats. In addition, there is a misconception that shooting digitally takes less time to light; again, not necessarily true, because it takes just as long to light for digital cinematography as it does for film if you want it to look good.

Selecting a format also has to do with the final product. When the film is completed, what will be the exhibition format? Will it be shown on a film print in theaters? Will it be shown on DigiBeta at film festivals? Will it be a straight-to-DVD project? Once you start answering those questions and take into consideration your budget, you can start to talk about some options in format.

## Would you ever suggest a format to a director?

■ *All the time — anamorphic. Whether they go for it or not it depends, but I'm going to lobby for anamorphic.*

(Richard Crudo, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ *I've shot everything Super 35 for the past ten or twelve years, from commercials, music videos, to feature films.*

(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

■ *Whatever I think works for the subject matter. I have no preference at all.*

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

## ASPECT RATIO

In discussing format it is necessary to understand **aspect ratio**, because each of the formats mentioned have a different aspect ratio, which has to do with the presentation of the finished project. Aspect ratio is basically the height and width of the frame and it is measured in a ratio that has to do with how much wider the image is in relation to the height of the frame. The ratios are determined by dividing the width of the frame by the height, so they are written as 1.85:1 or 2.40:1. Regular 16mm film has an aspect ratio of 1:33, which is a square frame similar to the 4:3 aspect ratio of digital video. Super 16mm has an aspect ratio of 1:66, which means that it is a bit wider than it is tall, but it is not a completion format and is either blown up to 35mm or presented on High Definition. HD has an aspect ratio of 1:78. Regular 35mm film is 1:85, which means it is wider than both Super 16mm and HD. Super 35mm film has an aspect ratio of 2:35, anamorphic is 2:40, and 70mm is 2:65. These last three are considered wide-screen formats.

There are numerous aesthetic reasons to select one aspect ratio over another and definitely aesthetic reasons to choose film over HD or vice versa. As a director it is important to understand the various formats available, so that you can have an educated conversation with your cinematographer and come up with the best format to suit your film.

■ *[Format is] something I always discuss with the director from the beginning, but I don't have a preference. I prefer to be free artistically to choose whatever I think works great for a scene or a movie.*

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

## FILM FORMATS

In the United States the preferred format for theatrically released motion picture films has been either 35mm with an aspect ratio of 1:85 or anamorphic, which has a wider aspect ratio at 2:40. Occasionally there will be a wide-screen presentation

in 70mm, but this is not as commonplace, due to the fact that it is a more expensive wide-screen format, so not as many films are shot in 70mm. There are also films that are shot on Super 16mm and blown up to 1:85 for theatrical release. So the decision to shoot on film — be it Super 16mm for a blow-up to 35mm, or Super 16mm straight to DVD, or 35mm or Super 35mm or anamorphic, or 70mm (if you have a really big budget and a really big film) — has to do with the inherent qualities of the film and what the exhibition format will be. Most importantly, selecting the format is an artistic decision that has to do with how this format will best serve telling the story.

Aesthetically there are differences between how the new HD formats look and how film looks when projected theatrically or seen on wide-screen monitors. Film has a specific look to it that the digital format does not. Since film is a photochemical process rather than an electronic signal, it absorbs colors through a chemical process. Some say it is a softer, more organic look than the look of the digital image. This is due to the way the image on film is created. The film emulsion is made up of layers of silver halide crystals, the light sensitive materials that react to light when exposed. These particles of emulsion cause the grain that we are accustomed to seeing on the big screen. Sometimes the grain is very subtle; other times it can be increased for a harsher, grittier look. Film reads highlights and shadows a bit differently than HD and has a wide range of exposure latitude that can absorb the extremes of blown out highlights and the darkest shadows. Film is tested to handle this range of exposure well and many cinematographers prefer using film as an origination medium, because they understand how to manipulate it (pushing, pulling, over- or underexposing) as well as the way in which it handles highlights. But the digital image is clean, crisp and sharp and the range of exposure is nearing the latitude of film, with the weakest area still being the highlights. The digital image is helpful when a film is heavy in special effects as well.

In terms of longevity, film has a proven ability to withstand the test of time. The negative can be archived for decades in the proper facility, can be cleaned, and new prints or DVDs can be made. Notice all the old film and television programs that have recently been captured on DVD? This is because they were all shot on film. At this time HD is a new format and the archiving issues have not been tested through time. We know film can last 100 years: It has been proven. The only damaged or lost films are the old nitrate-based prints, which are no longer in existence. If a negative is made from the finished HD project, it will be in an achievable format. Otherwise the hard drive must be stored and taken out and rebooted periodically to make sure all the information remains intact, a process that is more expensive than simply vaulting a negative.

More often than not when you go to the movies you are watching film prints. Most of the time the origination format was also film. Unless you are seeing something that has been shot in High Definition and is being projected digitally, then you are seeing a film. Even projects that have been shot digitally are usually

transferred to film for theatrical presentation; in essence you are watching a film print of a digitally captured image. The integration of the formats is currently the way many filmmakers have chosen to go, with the digital intermediate (DI) the chosen way to complete a film today. (Chapter 9 will go into detail on completion formats.)

![A black and white photograph of a Panavision Millennium studio camera, showing the large lens and the Panavision logo on the side.]()Panavision Millennium for studio. (Photo courtesy of Panavision)

![A black and white photograph of an Arri Arriflex 435 camera, showing the lens and the Arri logo on the side.]()Arriflex 435. (Photo courtesy of Arri)

## 1:85 FORMAT

Many filmmakers select the standard 1:85 aspect ratio of 35mm film for a variety of reasons, aesthetic or budgetary. Shooting in 1:85 means you shoot 35mm film in the 1:85 aspect ratio with normal spherical lenses. You finish on 35mm film, no blow-up is needed, and a digital intermediate can be done or one can finish in the traditional photochemical process. (Doing the DI will add to the finishing time and cost of the production). This aspect ratio is wider and narrower than the typical HD 16:9 format, which is why to see a full-screen image on your wide-screen television, there will be some letter boxing on the top and bottom, the image wider than it is tall.

In comparison to a wider screen format, like anamorphic, some might say that 1:85 provides a more intimate feeling with not as big a picture feel as 2:40, so if the film does not take place where huge landscape vistas are important and more of the story takes place during interior locations, then 1:85 is generally preferred. Close-ups of actors can fill the frame cleanly and transfers to formats screened on television can be accomplished without panning and scanning. There is a vast selection of 35mm cameras from Panavision, Arriflex, and Moviecam, as well as a varied selection of spherical prime or zoom lenses from Panavision, Zeiss, Cooke, Angenieux, and Canon.

There are filmmakers who prefer framing in the 1:85 format as opposed to 2:40 just because of the width of the frame and the intimacy that may be more inherent in 1:85.

■ For *The English Patient* Anthony (Minghella) and I sat down for a long time and talked about format: Why would we go anamorphic, why wouldn't we go 1:85? 1:85 is an actor's format; you can get a nicer single out of a 1:85. With anamorphic you're going to have the other actor in there somewhere with a little dirty foreground, which I do like in some cases. But anamorphic is a different bag in terms of coverage, so Anthony and I went back and forth. No studio was there to tell us what they wanted. Harvey Weinstein had footed the bill and he wasn't hammering us about format. We mulled it over for a long time, and finally the edict we came out with was 1:85, because it's a movie about people in the desert, not a desert with people in it — we shook hands on that. A lot of people have argued [that] because the desert is a flat format, anamorphic [would] fit perfectly, and we knew that, but we also argued against David Lean's *Lawrence of Arabia* — it's fantastic, unbelievable, but we didn't want to do that. Anthony said, "I will never want to start on a beautiful mountain range and come down and find the movie. We'll always cut to the movie." I had to remind him a few times when he'd say, "could we start there and come down?" I'd remind him, "no, we can't, Anthony, the camera is not programmed to do that." Because you cut those shots out in the end, we worked very hard to set up a shot that had that in the background; then the cars or whatever would bring you right into the movie. I think it helps keep the pace up, but the majesty of it is still there. I like that, because it's making the movie first, and the cinematography comes second.

(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

■ On Babel we ended up going 1:85. I think almost a natural choice would have been wide screen, but when we were scouting locations in Morocco, we checked with my Alan Gordon viewfinder, and saw the locations with the 2:35 and the 1:85. Alejandro felt the 2:35 was too beautiful, because the mountain ranges looked fantastic, but he wanted to feel the ground where the people lived, he wanted to see the floor, so we actually ended up favoring compositions that saw the floor, felt the ground. That was the main reason we wanted to go 1:85 on the movie. Even though in the Japanese sequence I used anamorphic lenses, we cut off the sides and used only the 1:85 portion of it. I wanted the anamorphic look for the depth of field but the aspect ratio was 1:85. So it varies.

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

So why not shoot 1:85? Many cinematographers love the 2:40 aspect ratio and feel that 1:85 will not give as sharp a picture due to the fact that the 1:85 negative is smaller than the size of the image used in 2:40. The anamorphic aspect ratio actually uses 55% more area on the negative, so in theatrical presentation more grain will be visible on the 1:85 format than on the 2:40. But with today's improved lenses and film stocks grain is not as much of a factor as in the past. What makes shooting standard 35mm 1:85 easier is the fact that there is no blow-up or additional optical process involved in the completion of the film. A 35mm print with an optical soundtrack can be made photochemically from a film shot in the 1:85 aspect ratio. You've been watching this format in theaters for decades. Not until recently has anamorphic been reintroduced as a popular format to shoot on.

Another possible reason against shooting on 35mm could be that the cinematographer and director choose to shoot handheld with a smaller camera for most of the film, as in *Thirteen* (directed by Catherine Hardwicke), and Super 16mm is a more viable format for that.

![A black and white photograph of an Arriflex Super 16mm camera (SR3) mounted on a tripod.]()Arriflex Super 16mm camera (SR3)

![A black and white photograph of an Arri Arriflex 416 film camera.]()Arriflex 416. (Photo courtesy of Arri)

## SUPER 16MM FORMAT (1:66)

If you like the idea of shooting on film, but think perhaps your budget does not accommodate shooting on standard 35mm with a 1:85 aspect ratio, or if you want to shoot in a smaller format, keep in mind that Super 16mm has all the same film stocks available as 35mm. The negative itself is about half the size of a 35mm negative, so a little more grain might be detected in the image if there were a side-by-side comparison after a blow-up. But as an origination format, it is versatile and can be quite beautiful. It is a great format to shoot for an HD finish.

From an economic perspective, Super 16mm film stock is about half the price of 35mm film stock, and you get twice as much of it due to the size of the negative, for example 400' of single perforated 16mm film runs about 11 minutes and may cost about \$120.00, while 400' of 35mm film runs a little over 4 minutes and may cost about \$240.00. But Super 16mm is not a completion format, since the entire negative has been used for the image; therefore, it has to be blown up to a 35mm negative so an optical soundtrack can be put on the film for projection. Some might say the money you may save up front you are paying for in postproduction for the blow-up to 35mm.

Another reason people select Super 16mm these days is if they are planning to do a digital intermediate in post, then the Super 16mm negative is transferred to a 35mm negative once it has been color corrected on the DI. The cinematographer

would frame with a 1:85 ground glass to match the previously shot 35mm footage or use a 1:78 ground glass for a finish on High Definition that will perfectly fill the frame of an HD monitor.

Cost aside, Super 16mm is a format that allows the cinematographer to shoot with smaller, more lightweight cameras like the Arriflex SR-3 or the brand new Arriflex 416, which was just introduced in 2007. Some shoots may use multiple Super 16mm cameras to get more coverage and be able to run more film through the magazine and still be able to handheld the camera or shoot on a Steadicam for a longer period of time. The Super 16mm cameras are smaller and with shorter prime lenses are lighter than the 35mm cameras. The cameras are unencumbered by cables and can be handheld, moving in small spaces. Super 16mm is great for location shooting.

But since the size of the negative is smaller than 35mm when the film is blown up, more of the grain is visible; cinematographers frequently overexpose the negative to reduce the grain when it is blown up to 35mm (although today's film stocks have much less noticeable grain than ever before). Some filmmakers searching for an edgy, grainy look may select Super 16mm to exploit that feature. Since Super 16 is a smaller format it inherently has more depth of field than 35mm film, which also might be a desired feature. To reduce the depth of field you shoot with longer lenses.

Selecting Super 16mm provides the film look, at a slightly lower cost for production and allows the use of smaller, more lightweight cameras for handheld work or multiple cameras for a single shoot with more coverage. For completion purposes, shooting on Super 16mm allows you the capability to do a digital intermediate and output to 35mm for theatrical release or go straight to an HD finishing format for a master in which to make DVDs for release and bypass the final print stage. The last option is to shoot Super 16mm and do a traditional photochemical finish, which would include blowing up the negative to 35mm with an interpositive (IP) and internegative (IN), with the final print for theatrical release in 35mm. (You can find more about this in Chapter 9.)

The Super 16mm aspect ratio of 1:66 is close to the HD aspect ratio of 1:78, so it is a format that fits nicely on an HD monitor and could be used as the origination medium of a film that is completed digitally. It works well scanned at 2k, with the equivalent of 2500 lines of resolution.

■ *I think Super 16 is phenomenal, really good. I shot the first Texas Chainsaw on Super 16 and that looks like it, but today you can get pretty close to things going on the big screen and I bet I can show you things on the small screen that you wouldn't know were shot on Super 16.... I shot Frankenstein for USA network on Super 16 and I love it. I think the look is right on. Super 16 is fantastic. The cameras are lightweight, and it really makes a difference in your shooting style, [because] it's so easy to move around.*  
(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

■ [For Babel] Alejandro wanted to feel the texture of the film grain and contrast. To me that suggested at least some degree of bleach bypass. When we learned that the film stock I initially wanted to use had been discontinued, I thought, "Why not Super 16?" Alejandro liked the idea, and after testing we decided to go with it. That meant we knew we'd be finishing with a DI, because we wanted to shoot 35mm in the other countries.

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, November 2006, 41)

![A black and white photograph of an Arriflex 535 film camera mounted on a track.]()Arriflex 535. (Photo courtesy of Arri)

## SUPER 35MM FORMAT (2:35)

Super 35mm is a way of shooting a wider-screen format than traditional 1:85 without using anamorphic lenses, which compress the image. But like Super 16mm, it is not a completion format, and usually a digital intermediate is done so the film can be released in the 2:40 aspect ratio. It is a flat, spherical lens format allowing the cinematographer to use the same type of fast lenses as in standard 35mm without the anamorphic squeeze and loss of depth of field that comes with using anamorphic lenses.

■ I prefer Super 35 because it allows you to use short focal-length lenses. I also like the scale of the format — the intimacy — and the texture of the film grain. In some cases I find anamorphic to be almost too clean, too grain-free, and pristine.

(Roger Deakins, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, October 2007, 32)

The Super 35mm 2:35 format is preferred over the 2:40 anamorphic by some cinematographers because of the increased depth of field that the shorter (more wide-angle) spherical lenses provide over the compressed anamorphic lenses. There are many cinematographers who like to shoot in low light and the slower

anamorphic lenses (T.3.5) as compared to sphericals (T.1.7) means that lighting when shooting anamorphic needs to be a bit brighter. The lenses for anamorphic are physically larger and therefore the camera package could be made heavier compared to 35mm with a shorter spherical lens on the camera.

I've shot everything Super 35 for the past ten or twelve years, from commercials, music videos, to feature films with the exception of the Texas Chainsaw Massacre remake, which I fully intended to shoot Super 35, but they wouldn't give us the money for a digital intermediate. On a feature you have to have a digital intermediate or an optical blow-up, and both are quite expensive. A DI is probably going to put about \$250k onto your post, so I always propose to shoot 3-perf, which is a native of 1:79 aspect ratio — [with a] 25% savings on stock and processing, you offset about half the cost of a DI. The production triangle: you can do it good, fast, or cheap, but you can only ever have two. Maybe it's stretching it a little, but I think when you shoot 3-perf, you can have all three. When you shoot 3-perf Super 35mm, you shoot out into what used to be saved for the soundtrack area. Your negative is better than normal — that not only answers the good, but better; it's 23% larger than the regular negative. Cheap: obviously, it's cheap; we're only shooting 3-perf negative rather than four, so we save in film stock and processing; also short ends, 66-67' per minute versus 90' per minute in 35, so that 60' end that you might throw away, you would shoot on 3-perf. (A totally disposable end is not disposable anymore; you can use it.) Is it faster? Yes, because the 1000' magazine, instead of being 11 minutes, is now 14 minutes, so you are reloading less often. In the course of a 55-day shoot, the fact that you reload less often, shoot the short ends, and don't reload, the fact that you shoot 3/4, you have 3/4 fewer reloads, if you have two cameras you shut down 5 minutes anyhow — it adds up. Maybe the fast part is stretching it a little, but it is a bit faster. So I am a huge believer in 3-perf. We shouldn't waste: 3-perf Super 35mm is less waste. Does the negative really need to be Super 35; could it be Super 16? Well, at least we are using it, instead of the 4-perf waste or even shooting academy and not shooting out into the soundtrack area.

(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

Many films choose to use the Super 35mm format to achieve the big picture look without the trouble of shooting with anamorphic lenses. On the down side, you do have either an optical stage for completion or you are committing to a digital intermediate, where the negative will be scanned to create the negative on 2:40 for release. But the anamorphic negative is still using more of the frame for image capture, so it could be argued that it will provide a finer grained image if a digital scan was done at 4k.

When I was hired to shoot *Captivity* for Roland Joffé, I had to talk him into shooting 3-perf spherical, because everything the man had ever done before was anamorphic (*The Mission*, *The Killing Fields*). When Roland said he wanted to shoot anamorphic for *Captivity*, I said, "Roland, this story is a psychodrama; they are not 2:35, they are normally 1:85 pictures. I've received 80 pages of storyboards that you've drawn that all happen to be in the 1:85 aspect ratio." He looked at me and said, "you noticed that, huh? Most people wouldn't notice that." I said, "we don't want those slow anamorphic lenses. They are hard to get; who knows what quality we will get in Russia." But Roland said, "I'm really into anamorphic, I really want that aspect ratio." So I said "Roland, you are the director, and I will give you whatever you want, but

my personal recommendation is that we don't make this film harder to shoot than it has to be. So don't go anamorphic where I'm going to need four times as much light for shooting, you've got this scene lit with only matches, and we're dealing with a Russian crew we don't know... [instead] let me do it spherical. It'll give the 2:40, we'll go 3-perf and do a DI, and Roland said, "no, I don't want to go DI; I've never seen anything digital that I've ever liked." We ended up shooting 3-perf spherical and doing the DI, and at the end when he saw what we could do in the DI, Roland said, "I can't thank you enough. I'm always shooting 3-perf spherical and doing a DI. This is how I will make all my films in the future."

(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

![A black and white photograph of a Panavision Millennium studio camera, showing the lens, viewfinder, and the Panavision logo on the hood.]()Panavision Millennium for studio. (Photo courtesy of Panavision)

## ANAMORPHIC FORMAT (2:40)

■ Anamorphic motion picture photography refers to a process by which an image is squeezed optically at the time of original photography. This geometrically distorted image is then expanded or un-squeezed in presentation by a complementary process of de-anamorphosis.

American Cinematographer Manual, Ninth Edition, 46.

To get a wide-screen image, anamorphic lenses must be used to compress the image while shooting. Because the aspect ratio is 2:40, there is still space on the negative for the soundtrack upon completion. This is a wide-screen format that was basically adapted from the 1950s format of Cinemascope and Vista Vision that was created to lure audiences out of their homes watching television and back into the movie theaters. Ever since the 1950s, motion picture films have been screened in either 2:40 or 1:85.

There are many cinematographers who simply love the anamorphic aspect ratio; the wide-screen negative makes you feel like you've shot a big movie. But cinematographers have their own experiences and their own preferences for format.

■ I love anamorphic. I would never want to shoot anything that is not anamorphic again. You see that big, wide image on the screen, and for me it replicates more the way our eyes actually see. It's a great graphic with the 2:40 aspect ratio. The camera could fall off the truck and almost wherever it lands, it's a great composition.... You have a lot of information on the wide screen so you can let things play out; you can stage actors in depth and not just cut, cut, cut. With wide screen, you are using the largest piece of real estate on the negative, and that translates to a rich image, because the largest negative makes the best print. Super 35mm is smaller, 1:85 is smaller, and even if I was going to do a digital intermediate, I would still want to shoot anamorphic because there is more information for the scanner to pick up, more pixels.  
(Richard Crudo, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ What I would almost say — and I hope no lighting bolt gets me — [is] that [I] would rule out shooting anamorphic. Because the image starts out in an aspect ratio of 2:35 or 2:40, the anamorphic lens will squash the images when they come in and spread them back out in projection, so the focus looks different and they do have a different look. But the lenses are very impractical: The zooms are a 5.6 as opposed to 2.8; the primes will be 2.8 or 4 as opposed to the faster primes, which are 1.4 if you need to open up all the way. Depth of field is extremely shallow; focus is quite critical; there is some distortion while shooting. The two things that are great in anamorphic is the aspect ratio and the way the lenses flare — they will get a nice stripe across the frame. You can't really reproduce the flare in camera, maybe a little. For me it's just not practical for shooting, so I would never propose to shoot in the anamorphic format.  
(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, June 2007)

■ A lot of people push us into shooting in the anamorphic format, and I used to shoot with anamorphic lenses, but they're so damn slow — they're 4.5, and it's a constant battle to get interiors lifted up. You can do it, of course. I resisted Super 35mm for a long time, but then I did a film [with] torches and low light levels and had to have 2.8, so I went into Super 35mm. In the old days Super 35mm had a prism that optically converted Super 35 to anamorphic and everything was fine up to that moment, [but] that prism was the failure. The end print result for the cinemas in full anamorphic squeeze was a disaster, so I resisted using Super 35 for that reason. Then all of a sudden they solved the prism problem, so I swung over.  
(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

■ For more than 20 years, my choice for image capture has been 35mm film in the anamorphic format, and it has been my preference to answer print and release print on film. This is for both aesthetic and technical reasons. On the aesthetic level, I find the 2.40:1 aspect ratio to be openly expansive for large interiors and exteriors, yet also able to embrace the intimate environs of a small-scale drama.  
(John Bailey, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, June 2008, 92)

■ People always comment about the slower lenses and limited depth of field, but I haven't found it to be a problem at all. So you light one stop deeper, use the 500-speed film and rate it at 400 or 320. With lenses, I like to use Panavision's older C series, I like the way they look, but they're all good, just mix and match a good kit and keep them with you. In terms of depth of field, let's say I

light to a T2.8 in 1:85, you light to T4 for scope and I'm back to the same depth of field as 1:85 essentially, because a 50mm lens in scope really has the same depth of field as a 100mm lens in 1:85. So you light a stop deeper and basically gain in back at the same depth of field. Owen Roizman and I were talking about Wyatt Earp, because he shot the whole thing wide open in scope, and it looks beautiful, Academy Award nominated — he did something right.

(Richard Crudo, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ [For *There Will Be Blood*] Paul (Thomas Anderson) knew he wanted to make an anamorphic film, because he prefers the anamorphic lens system to shooting Super 35mm and doing an anamorphic release print. We did *Hard Eight* in Super 35mm, but every movie since has been anamorphic. He just likes the look those lenses produce. When you shoot in anamorphic, there's a different feeling, a different way of staging and different depth of field. Paul loves older films, and those qualities mean something to him.

(Robert Elswit, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, January 2008, 39)

The decision to shoot in one film format over another is definitely a conversation you will have with your cinematographer based on the final release format of the film, the budget, and the aesthetic content of the film. Knowing the differences will help you to make a solid, educated decision that will make both director and cinematographer happy with the finished product.

![A black and white photograph of an IMAX camera, a large, complex film camera system, displayed behind glass.]()IMAX camera

### IMAX (wide-screen film format)

The largest film format is actually known as IMAX, a 70mm negative that runs through the camera and projector sideways. The negative is approximately the size

of a 2-1/4" negative from a still camera. The film is actually 65mm wide with 2.5 mm added along each outer side of the perforations for the magnetic stripes that hold six tracks of surround sound. More recent 70mm prints use digital sound encoding, but the vast majority of 70mm prints predate the digital technology. Each frame is five perforations tall with an aspect ratio of 2.20:1. Because the majority of movie theaters are unable to handle 70mm film, original 70mm films are shown on 35mm prints in the regular Cinemascope ratio of 2.35:1.

The "Todd-AO" system that was introduced in the 1950s popularized the format for use in feature length films, primarily musicals such as *Oklahoma*, *South Pacific*, and *The Sound of Music* or epics like *Ben Hur* and *Cleopatra*. But due to the costs of the wide-screen film stock and the expensive projection system, distribution of such films was limited. It is not often one gets to see the films projected in their original format, but it is a treat to see a film like *Lawrence of Arabia* projected in 70mm — the clarity and depth of the image cannot be compared. There were many films shot on 65-70mm and re-released on 35mm film for wider distribution after the initial debut of the film. Such was the case with Stanley Kubrick's *2001: A Space Odyssey* (1970).

A recent use of the large negative format was for *The Dark Knight*, shot by Wally Pfister, ASC, which combines both 35mm and IMAX 65mm. Director Christopher Nolan wanted to incorporate the two formats and convinced Warner Brothers to allow them to shoot many of the action sequences in IMAX. The cost was approximately four times what it would have cost to shoot 35mm wide screen, but the results are amazing. The clarity of the image and the depth of field is unrivaled by any other format.

■ *It's ironic, because many filmmakers are trying out digital cameras that actually capture less resolution and information, and we're going in the opposite direction, upping the ante by capturing images with unparalleled resolution and clarity.*

(Wally Pfister, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, July 2008, 32)

■ *Many Hollywood features have been presented on IMAX screens via the IMAX digital DMR (Digital Remastering) process, which scans a 35mm interpositive, applies grain reduction and other image-processing algorithms and generates a 70mm IMAX negative. But a feature-length narrative film combining 35mm images with the native IMAX format, in which a 65mm negative travels horizontally through cameras and projectors, had never been attempted [before The Dark Knight].*

(David Heuring, *American Cinematographer*, July 2008, 30)

Because of the prohibitive cost of shooting an entire film in 65mm film, not many films since Kenneth Branagh's *Hamlet* (1996) have used the format for an entire production. However, there have been Hollywood blockbuster films that have been blown up to 70mm for theatrical screenings, and recent 3D films like *The Polar Express* that were successfully presented in 70mm.

## DIGITAL FORMATS

Digital cameras are taking image capture out of the photochemical process and into the electronic world. Terms like CCDs, sensors, and pixels replace the language of grain structure, exposure latitude, and emulsion. Because of the electronic signal used to capture the image in digital formats, the images have no grain structure, which is something we have been seeing for years and has become a part of the character of movies. The digital image is clear and crisp if it is also screened on an HD monitor or plasma screen. As we move away from the tape formats of digital video and into a tapeless system of recording directly onto a hard drive or a memory card, the various tape formats that have evolved over the years — including DigiBeta, Beta SP, DVCAM, and Mini-DV — fall by the wayside as obsolete formats. The evolving technology of electronic image capture is gaining strong momentum in the twenty-first century, with the development of new 4k cameras that some say are rivaling the look of 35mm film. But the question that frequently arises is, why select a digital format over a film format? Is there an aesthetic reason, an economic reason, or a new technology that you want to try? Some cinematographers feel that the evolution of the digital camera is fixing something that wasn't broken in the first place. But cinematographers are quick to agree that the evolution of digital postproduction is absolutely amazing. In terms of image capture, many would rather stay with film for now, while others are open to trying out the new format and still others who feel that it depends on the project for feature films. For television, moving into the world of HD was inevitable for many dramatic programs, news, sitcoms, and commercials.

Film is recorded at 24 frames per second and traditional video formats run at NTSC 29.97 or 30 frames per second. When filmmakers in the late '90s started shooting on tape, they often selected the video cameras that were set for the European PAL rate of 25 frames per second, because in transferring to a film print for release there was less frame drop than at 30 fps. Then the 24P cameras were created, which could shoot on tape at a true 24 frames per second, which made transferring the images to a film format for release less complicated. The 24P cameras use a progressive scanning technique of scanning sequential horizontal lines of information within the frame. With the creation of the 24HPCAM system, filmmakers now had a video camera that acted more like a film camera.

Based on this concept, a low-cost camera still using tape that was great for a few years was the Panasonic DVX 100 (and the improved A & B models), which were able to shoot both standard video and 24P in the advanced mode giving 720 lines of resolution, an improvement over the image quality of mini-DV (420). For a mini-DV camera, the Panasonic DVX-100 provided excellent-looking video that came closer to the look of film than standard 30fps mini-DV. Canon improved their XL1S to the XL 2 in a tapeless format, then Panasonic came out with the DVX 200, and Sony came out with a low-end HD camera, and now there is HDV. Every

year a new camera and a new format would come out, making the selection of a digital camera a difficult one, because it could be obsolete in a year's time.

But all of these formats are all vastly inferior to Super 16mm film, which provides the equivalent of 2500 lines of resolution, if one were to transfer the full image at a 2k scan. As the cameras evolve, so does the use of tapes to capture the image. Now there are cards that must be downloaded into a hard drive. Whatever digital camera you select, make sure you back up all footage on a hard drive and a stable tape format that will continue to be around for awhile, like HDCAM-SR.

Since it is time for the United States television broadcast system to change the standardized broadcasting to a High Definition format, the technology of image capture for broadcast quality has been scrambling to improve and standardize an image capture format.

## PROFESSIONAL DIGITAL CAMERAS

The "big boys" of professional high definition cameras are currently the Panavision Genesis, Arriflex D-21, Grass Valley Viper Cam, the DALSA, the Sony CineAlta F-23, the Panasonic VariCam, and the new RED camera. These digital cameras, designed to shoot at 2k or 4k resolutions, are what professional cinematographers are using to shoot some feature films.

One reason a director and cinematographer may choose to shoot in the digital format is if the film they are doing has a great deal of special effects. In that case, since it is digital to digital, it is a cleaner look in terms of creating the amazing special effects that are in films today. Others may like the clean, crisp, absence of grain in the image, or they may be thinking of how the image quality will look on Blu-ray DVDs on an HD monitor, and still others may be interested in seeing what the hype is all about and taking the digital camera out in the field for a hard run on a feature film. Since it is a digital format, there are no film print stages to go through for low-budget features that are going straight to DVD, and the image quality will look quite good. Probably what directors like most is that with a properly calibrated HD monitor, what you see is what you get, without the mysterious process of negative being processed at the lab and telecined to a tape format to screen. There is an immediacy to HD that is an attractive feature.

Following is a brief description of each of the professional digital cameras and their basic capabilities. It is strongly encouraged that you and your cinematographer do further research and testing before embarking on a major decision to shoot in one format over another. While all the cameras are designed for high-end uncompressed digital capture, some are more film friendly than others, meaning that they utilize film lenses and a true film viewfinder. Therefore they do not have the problems of unlimited depth of field and lack of resolution that the low-end digital cameras have.

Not all of these cameras can be purchased; many are rented, just as you would rent a 35mm camera package. The Panavision and Arriflex D-21 cameras can be used on the same support systems as their film cameras. They are not inexpensive to use, but are

the alternate choice to shooting on film. This is a sampling of what is available right now. The evolution of the 4k digital camera is without a doubt going to continue over the next decade with new and improved models appearing each year at NAB.

There are conflicting opinions on the precise amount of pixel resolution there is in a single frame of 35mm film. Some say a negative has anywhere from 5k to 8k lines of resolution available if properly scanned. But generally it has been agreed that a 4k resolution is comparable to a 35mm "answer print," and that some release prints could be slightly less. It is based on this concept that the 4k cameras have come of age as an alternative format to the traditional film camera.

![A black and white photograph of the Panavision Genesis digital camera, a large, professional motion picture camera.]()The Panavision Genesis. (Photo courtesy of Panavision)

### The Panavision "Genesis"

Panavision is a company that has been manufacturing professional motion picture cameras for decades. Their high-end digital camera is called the Genesis. "The Genesis CCD is a 12.4 megapixel sensor arranged in a 1920x1080 array. It is comparable in size to a 1.78:1, Super 35mm film frame. It is a true 4:4:4 RGB array (4k) with the same number of pixels used for Red, Green, and Blue, producing equal resolution in RGB, unlike other digital cameras. The camera uses the same lens mount as the Panavision 35mm cameras, so you can use any Panavision spherical 35mm lens with the Genesis, which provides equivalent depth of field to the film cameras. The Genesis can provide an anamorphic aspect ratio without the use of anamorphic lenses by extracting a 2.40 frame from the 1.78:1 image and then doing a digital squeeze when going out to film from the DI. Traditional film filters can be used and various speeds can be used on the camera." (Panavision)

There is a master tape recorded with two audio channels fed from the sound recordist, and copies are fed through a display processor and into an HD video projector for dailies.

The camera has been used to shoot numerous feature films. *Superman Returns* (2006) was shot on the Genesis and released theatrically on film prints with a wide-screen aspect ratio. Other films shot with the Genesis include *Apocalypto*, *Before The Devil Knows You're Dead*, *Cloverfield*, *The Comebacks*, *The Condemned*, *Click*, *Déjà Vu*, *Fantastic Four: Rise of the Silver Surfer* (limited portions), *The Ferryman*, *Flyboys*, *Grindhouse* (Planet Terror scenes only), *His Majesty Minor*, *I Now Pronounce You Chuck and Larry*, *The Lookout*, *La Maison du bonheur*, *Next*, *The Other Boleyn Girl*, *Reign Over Me*, *Slipstream*, *Scary Movie IV*, *Superbad*, *Superman Returns*, and *A Tiger's Tale*.

![A black and white photograph of the DALSA Origin digital cinema camera mounted on a large camera rig, featuring a large lens and viewfinder.]()The DALSA Origin. (Photo courtesy of DALSA)

## The DALSA Origin

"The DALSA 'Origin' is the first commercially available digital cinema camera to capture 4k resolution, which is the emerging quality standard for film content. The camera was originally shown at NAB 2003 in Las Vegas, where DALSA received four industry awards for innovation." (DALSA Digital Cinema)

The DALSA combines 4k resolution, a 16-bit output, cine lenses, and a real optical viewfinder like the 35mm motion picture cameras. The frame transfer CCD offers four times the resolution of “Hi-Def” and provides exposure latitude of the best film stocks, claiming 12 stops of linear response. It uses standard 35mm lenses, allowing the depth of field and focus of film. In terms of output options, it is compatible with the industry’s existing 2k and 4k infrastructures, created to move to whatever new postproduction or display standard emerges in the future. The camera weighs about 35 pounds, but it has not yet been tested extensively in motion picture films, because 4k as a postproduction process has not been extensively utilized at this point.

DALSA cameras with CCD image sensor chips are aboard the NASA rovers “Spirit” and “Opportunity” to capture images from the surface of another planet. DALSA Origin delivers 4k with wide-range exposure latitude. 4k material downsampled to HD resolution can look much better than material that was originally shot on HD, a difference that would be visible on Blu-ray or HD-DVD. Like the Panavision Genesis camera, the Origin is only available as a rental and not for purchase.

![A black and white photograph of the Arri D-21 digital motion picture camera mounted on a large, professional camera head (likely an Arri Aputure). The camera features a large viewfinder screen displaying a waveform monitor, and various controls and lenses are visible.]()The Arri D-21. (Photo courtesy of Arri)

## The ARRIFLEX D-20 & D-21

Arriflex is another motion picture company that has been manufacturing film equipment for decades. Borrowing from the Arriflex motion picture film cameras, the D-20 and D-21 are promoted as being film-style digital motion picture cameras. The D-21 is the newer, refined model that was introduced at NAB 2008. The D-20 was first introduced in the fall of 2005. The cameras are compatible with all the Arriflex film camera accessories and support equipment. The major attributes are the optical

viewfinder and a CMOS sensor that is the size and shape of a Super 35mm film frame. Most HD cameras are based on the conventional television concept of a round prism with three separate 2/3" sensors. Each one of the sensors is smaller than a 16mm film frame and uses video style signal processing. The D-20's larger sensor and image processing makes the material captured look more cinematic.

When used with the current 35mm PL mount lenses, the cameras provide the same field of view and depth of field as a 35mm motion picture camera. The captured data is recorded to either flash drives or hard drives. The D-20 is capable of running at speeds from 1-48 frames per second.

The Arriflex D-20 has been used on numerous television commercials, a TNT miniseries, and director Guy Ritchie used the camera for his recent feature *RocknRolla*, to be released by Warner Brothers Studios. The camera has also been used for several independent features. It is best known for giving "filmic" images and is similar to the Arriflex 435 film camera in some of its features.

![A black and white photograph of the Grass Valley Viper Cam, a large digital cinematography camera system.]()The Viper Cam. (Photo courtesy of Grass Valley)

## GRASS VALLEY VIPER CAM

"The Viper Film Stream Camera System is the only digital cinematography camera designed from the ground up to capture every detail needed for brilliant, uncompromised, uncompressed output. With three 9.2 million pixel frame transfer CCDs

capturing 1920x1080 resolution, the Viper Film Stream camera system delivers an RGB 4:4:4 10-bit log output—uncompromised by electronic camera signal processing—to a field recorder. Part of the Grass Valley Film Stream workflow, the Viper Film Stream camera delivers its output directly into the post production process for finishing.” (Grass Valley)

The Viper cam is technically not a 4k camera, but it has frequently been used in feature films. It was used for the production of *Zodiac* (2006), directed by David Fincher and photographed by Harris Savides, ASC. *Zodiac* is the first feature-length film to be shot digitally onto a hard disk in its entirety. Many cinematographers in the industry think *Zodiac* is one of the best digital films shot to date.

■ *My concern was mostly about workflow and how we were going to ingest the data. It was also about getting used to watching things through a 32-inch LCD HD monitor and being able to direct from that. The image is kind of flat and green, but it offers an immediacy that allows you to hone and shape the image. And it's all in real time, so when you've shot the shot, you know you have it.*

(David Fincher, director of *Zodiac*, in *American Cinematographer*, April 2007, 35)

The Viper cam was also used to shoot *Cloverfield* (2007), *Collateral* (2004), *Domino* (2005), and *Miami Vice* (2006), just to mention a few titles. There are also current television programs shot with the Viper cam, too numerous to list.

Director Michael Mann (*Collateral*, *Miami Vice*) selected the digital format entirely for aesthetic reasons.

■ *There was a quality to the Viper cam that I responded to. It had to do with the sensitivity of reds and yellows and oranges. This was not only seeing deeply into the night, seeing what you see with the naked eye and something more than you can see with the naked eye, but also the color information. It had an aesthetic that I wanted.*

In addition to the aesthetic look of the digital format, some directors and cinematographers may decide to shoot on a digital format due to the length of recording time over what a film magazine will give you. (This goes for all of the digital cameras, not just the Viper cam).

■ *The benefit is that there is a 55-minute tape, so in that sense there are fewer interruptions. [With film] if you are handheld and you have a 400-foot mag in there, every three minutes and 45 seconds you are having to stop.*

(Michael Mann, Director, *Collateral*, in Carolyn Giardina, “Revving Up Digital Cinematography,” *Hollywood Reporter*, August 2008. www.hollywoodreporter.com/hr/search/article)

■ *I would advise anyone thinking of using (Viper cam) to test it for his specific project, just as he should any camera. My ideal would be for Thomson to come up with a modular version of the Viper with different imaging-chip sets, one set for highlights and one for the toe, just as we can choose different film stocks for different situations. There is no all-around film stock, so how can we expect a single digital camera to do everything?*

(Harris Savides, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, April 2007, 41)

![A black and white photograph of a Sony F-23 digital cinema camera mounted on an ARRI camera stand.]()The F-23. (Photo courtesy of Sony)

## SONY CINEALTA DIGITAL CAMERAS

In 2002 George Lucas released *Star Wars: Episode II – Attack of the Clones*, his first feature entirely shot in the digital format. To shoot his film, he used the first CineAlta camera developed by Panavision and Sony, the Sony HDW-F900, which at the time was the first 24P High Definition camera. Since that time the Sony F-900 has been used on numerous productions, and is still in use for dramatic programming that is aired on television, such as Showtime's *Tudors*, *The L Word*, and *Dexter*.

George Lucas is a long-time advocate of digital technology and has said he sees no reason to ever shoot another project on film. Ironically, his friend Steven Spielberg says the exact opposite: that he hopes he never has to shoot a digital film. All of the Lucas films have heavy special effects, which works well with the origination format created digitally. For *Star Wars: Episode III – Revenge of the Sith*, Lucas used the more advanced Sony HDC-950, with higher resolution and better color reproduction than its predecessor. The film was cropped to a 2.35:1 aspect ratio from its native 16:9 frame. As a result, only 817 of the 1080 vertical pixels were actually used; but there is an anamorphic adaptor lens from Canon, which allows shooting in 2.35:1 without losing any pixels. Other films to use the 950 include *Fahrenheit 9/11* (2004), *An Inconvenient Truth* (2006), and *Mission Impossible III* (2006).

All of the Sony CineAlta cameras record onto HDCAM tapes, XDCAM Professional Discs, or SxS flash memory cards. They have the ability to shoot at various frame rates including 24fps and have a resolution of up to 1920x1080 pixels. Some other notable movies that were shot with CineAlta cameras include: *Sky Captain and the World of Tomorrow*, *Sin City*, *U2 3D*, and *Spy Kids 2: Island of Lost Dreams*.

The Sony family of CineAlta cameras includes: F-23, HDW-F900, HDC-F950, HDC-1000W/1500L, PDW-F350, PDW-F330, and PMW-EX1.

![A black and white photograph of the Panasonic VariCam 3700 camera, a large professional digital cinema camera.]()The Panasonic VariCam 3700

## PANASONIC VARICAM

The VariCam is the Panasonic camera in competition with the Sony CineAlta camera. It is mostly used to imitate the look and feel of motion picture cameras. The VariCam has been used for numerous feature films, commercials, television programs, and documentaries. The VariCam's main feature is that the frame rate can be varied from 4 to 60 fps, offering flexibility in creating in camera fast or slow motion effects. At NAB 2008 Panasonic introduced two new members to the VariCam family, the VariCam 3700 and VariCam 2700, with 2/3" Native HD resolution. The new models greatly advance digital filmmaking, with features including "master-quality, full-resolution 10-bit 4:2:2 AVC-Intra 100 recording; variable frame rates in one-frame increments; HD-SDI output of 23.98PsF/24PsF; and a multi-gamma function including Film-Rec for the prized VariCam look closely matching the color and response of film stocks. The new flagship model of the P2 HD VariCam line, the VariCam 3700 offers full native 1920x1080-pixel acquisition and independent frame recording with 10-bit/4:2:2 sampling. The HPX3700 2/3" 2.2 megapixel 3-CCD camera also offers a 4:4:4 RGB dual-link live output to deliver pristine images ideal for composition of visual effects even while recording." (Panasonic)

The VariCam can be rented or purchased. The purchase price is between \$45,000 and \$70,000, similar to the price of an SR-2. The VariCam has become popular with independent filmmakers as an alternate to film. The cameras can also be rented from professional camera rental companies.

Panasonic also has a 4k camera in the works, which could be available as soon as 2010.

![A black and white photograph of the RED ONE digital cinema camera mounted on a tripod.]()The RED ONE. (Photo courtesy of Brook Willard)

## RED ONE

Highly anticipated and hyped, the new RED ONE received thousands of pre-orders based on the prototype that was presented at NAB 2007. Because of its basic list price of \$17,500 (not including accessories or lenses), it is more affordable than the cameras previously mentioned. The camera is capable of recording resolutions up to 4520x2540 using a Super 35-sized CMOS sensor. (At NAB in April 2008, RED also revealed details about their future product, the Scarlet, a pocket professional digital cinema camera.) Now that people have received the RED ONE cameras, they are taking them through the paces and testing them in various situations.

"The sensor has a 12 megapixel CMOS (brand name: 'Mysterium') that is 24.4mm x 13.7mm, with 4520x2540 active pixels, 4900x2580 full pixels, and >66dB dynamic range. The Mysterium sensor has the same active area as a Super 35 film frame (masked to a 16:9 aspect ratio), allowing the same shallow depth of field to be produced in conjunction with lenses designed to cover the 35mm format. The camera also allows the sensor to be used in a 'windowed' mode, in which the sensor can emulate the active area of a Super 16 film frame while capturing footage at 2K. This allows the camera to be used with lenses designed to cover the Super 16 format. Mysterium is the company's proprietary sensor design. The REDCODE RAW codec allows 4k sensor data to be recorded at 24 frames per second with a data rate of around 27.5 MB/s (220 megabits per second). This data rate is low enough that on-camera recording (for example, to a RED-DRIVE digital magazine containing two 2.5" hard drives) is possible. REDCODE only functions at up to 30 frames per second at 4k or 96fps for 2k using the camera's

internal processor. RED is targeting the low-budget independent world of filmmakers with the reduced cost of the camera itself for high quality recording.” (RED Digital Cinema Camera Company)

The camera is brand new in 2008 and the reviews will soon follow. It is never advisable to jump into the purchase of a new model camera in the digital format until it has been tested and reviewed. The inevitable glitches with the technology will arise and get worked out in the year following its initial release. Be aware that each accessory for the RED ONE is a separate purchase and none is universal; currently you must use the Red products exclusively, such as their lenses.

The RED ONE camera ships with a PL lens mount, a common mount for modern 35mm and 16mm motion picture cameras. The company also has plans to release adapters for Canon FD and Nikon F-mount lenses, and for 2/3" B4 lenses. Availability is currently unknown, but the Canon and Nikon mounts are expected to be available before the 2/3" B4 mount. At least one company has announced other lens mount options such as Canon EOS EF in cooperation with RED, and other third party solutions may become available. The camera is a work in progress that is still in development. With the input of cinematographers putting the camera through various tests will come feedback that will improve upon the features that have not yet been entirely worked out.

According to the RED official website, director Steven Soderbergh is currently shooting the first of his two upcoming features *Guerrilla* and *The Argentine* entirely with the RED ONE camera. Soderbergh is very enthusiastic about the camera, saying that “*this is the camera I’ve been waiting for my whole career: jaw-dropping imagery recorded onboard a camera light enough to hold with one hand. I don’t know how Jim and the RED team did it — and they won’t tell me — but I know this: RED is going to change everything.*”

The film *Wanted* — directed by Timur Bekmambetov, and featuring James McAvoy, Morgan Freeman, and Angelina Jolie — was the first feature film to use the RED ONE digital camera (released in June of 2008). The upcoming motion picture *Game* is also to be shot with the RED ONE.

The excitement about the RED ONE is due to its lower sticker price, which is close to that of a used Super 16mm camera. The camera is fairly large, and does render a beautiful image, but a RED technician is needed on set to help with the workflow issues, so it is not a simple camera to use at this point. If you are interested in this camera, search the Internet for discussions and reviews.

■ Yes, I am considering a digital camera. The problem for me is figuring out what the tradeoff is in terms of how much it speeds us up and is cost-effective, compared to any possible loss of quality. I'm still a die-hard celluloid guy, but at a certain point, you have to say, if this is the way things are going to go, then you might have to try it. Depending on the subject matter, there are certain films it would make sense for. I recall back in the 1970s, for *Taxi Driver*, we talked about doing it on black-and-white [standard-def] video because of financing issues. We had some tests screened on a video projector, but the quality wasn't very good in those days, and we pulled back on the idea. But the technology is far superior now, obviously, so I'm sure I'll try. I

just don't want to be learning with new equipment on the set. That's the real problem. I expect I'll try something smaller first on high-def and we'll see what happens.  
(Martin Scorsese, *Millimeter*, October/November issue, 2006.  
[http://digitalcontentproducer.com/hdhdv/depth/hd\\_assist\\_scorsese\\_09262006/](http://digitalcontentproducer.com/hdhdv/depth/hd_assist_scorsese_09262006/))

## THOUGHTS FROM ASC CINEMATOGRAPHERS ON DIGITAL CAMERAS

■ I would always try to stay away from digital, but various things in the screenplay will make those decisions, some based on budget and schedule, practically speaking.

(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ What is the net gain of shooting digitally? So far, from my perspective, any benefit of using digital cameras lies in postproduction, not production. In the future, I see smaller, better cameras; fewer crewmembers needed to service them; and a method of production that's freer and less encumbered by technology. But that's not the situation right now.

(Harris Savides, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, April 2007, 41-42)

■ Digital technology came along as an answer to a question that nobody asked. If it had existed all along, and somebody came along with film and they saw how versatile it is, how simple it is, how archival it is, how lightweight it is, you'd say, wow, this is brilliant — where has it been all our lives? So you've got digital technology, a brand new way of doing the same thing, reinventing the wheel, but it started at a point forty notches down from the best of what the technology had to offer and it was being sold as the second coming. A combination of brilliant marketing and advertising — and people tending to believe that anything new is automatically better — gave this stuff its roots.

(Richard Crudo, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ I did shoot Ten Tiny Love Stories in HD. We used the Sony F-900, but it was simple monologues to camera, so I didn't put it through the works. Then I tested a Viper camera for Babel, for the Japanese story. If you're going for a certain look, it becomes a creative decision. I do find the technologies are getting better.

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ Should a movie look like film or like something else? It's subjective. The Viper does give the images an almost hyper-real quality. To my eye the Viper's digital images have a synthetic quality, it reminds me of the vivid colorful look of a Cibachrome photo. With the Viper the audience will see more than what they normally see in a movie — literally, the pores on people's faces and every hair on their head — so it may have an almost immersive effect.

(Harris Savides, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, April 2007, 41-42)

■ They [the studio] wanted to shoot Iron Man with the Genesis, [but] I had so much day exterior in that film that I preferred to work with film. I like to manipulate film, to have a certain feel and look and "bake it in." That's a term I hear used by visual effects people, like when you bake in a bleach bypass. Postproduction people hate it, because then they have to contend with it. The computer programs don't read grain on a bleach bypass level, so [postproduction

wants] everything to be RGB, so they can build the look later. But I am a strong believer that those looks are genuine. I think if you look at the Genesis films they only have a few ways to go, and most of the time they go with the higher contrast, because it looks better on the screen with the DI. There's a button in Photoshop called "posterize." I don't want that button. I think that it takes people out of the story. I have strong feelings about the DI, for sure, and I'm sure I will end up shooting with the Genesis or the Viper or whatever format comes next, but it has to be the right one for me.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

■ When I used to shoot documentaries I always shot on film, because that's how it was done in those days, so I would go to Africa to shoot for three months and carry all my stock, like 100 rolls of 16mm with me and shoot the film and come back and take it all to the lab and hope I got a good report the next morning. But there is something about the fact that you have a limited number of rolls, and you really have to think about what it is you are going to shoot. You can't just shoot and shoot, because it's not about the amount you shoot, it's about the moments you capture. Especially in a feature film, you are just looking for the moments. You might say, okay, I'm in a close-up and I'm trying to get a performance on HD, and I'll keep it rolling for an hour and maybe I'll get the moment, and maybe there's an argument for that on HD. But a number of actors have even said this to me: There is something about the whole ritual of film. You load this magazine, you've got 400' or 1000' and a particular amount of time and everybody is building up and doing their jobs. The lighting has got to be right, and makeup, and you're ready to shoot, and it's all leading up to this very important moment when they run the camera and put the board up. Now we've got to create this scene, maybe it's antiquated and traditional, but I think there is something in the buildup of this one moment to get this piece of magic on film. I think that's important. So it's not the difference in the image quality between film and HD, it's the way you work. Film forces a particular way of working that I think is good. There is no reason that you couldn't do that with HD, but from what I hear, people don't.

(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

■ The only successful looking HD film I've seen is Zodiac, but that was [made by] an extremely visual director with an extremely precise cameraman, and they created a very beautiful look for that film. I've seen Flyboys — too much contrast. I've seen Superman, which had a very clean RGB feel, and I didn't want that look for Iron Man. I wanted Iron Man to have a juxtaposition of looks between environments, and I don't think the Genesis would have done that for me. I don't care what people say about the DI, and what you can do with it, there are only so many ways you can slice it in that room before it doesn't look real. I want my films to look like films; I don't want them to look like cartoons.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

## Do you think you will ever shoot digitally?

■ I haven't yet, but I want to do it. It's the future and I'm not frightened of it. I've talked to a lot the guys who have used digital cameras, and they are not averse to it at all. I've always believed that HD is simply another style. I was over at Panavision and they had a comparison between a 35mm film and a digital camera, and I couldn't tell the difference. I mean boy, it was

subtle, too subtle for me to worry about. Once again, I figure the audience will be used to it in the first three shots; after that, they just watch the movie.... I noticed that Panavision is gearing all the way to HD; they've refined that Genesis down to a heavy handheld camera. So it is the future, and I think cinematographers are going to have to bite the bullet and realize they are not the magic man who walks around with the cape saying "spot that flood" and that nobody knew what you were doing. It's gone. Because now they are all going to watch the monitor. Nobody will have to go to dailies, because they know what they are getting right then. If it's a properly color-corrected monitor, and it would be, you just light for that monitor — that's what you're doing. It won't take long before a smart producer will say get rid of those DPs — put a good operator on and the lighting could almost be done by committee.

(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

■ I still would prefer to shoot on Super 16mm. You get a better quality image. You could work faster in terms of getting that image quality you want. Sure, there would be other restrictions, but I think I would have that argument. Right now, I don't think I'd take a project if they said "we're shooting on HD and that's it." I'd have to think about it and ask, does it suit the project? How do I feel about that, can I have fun with it, whatever, but right now I don't really feel I'd want to.

(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

## Do you think it's harder to light for digital?

■ I lit *Zodiac* just as I would any feature I've shot on film. Where the light comes from and how you model it doesn't change because you're using a different camera. But the sensitivity of the Viper and how you can learn to light by eye was interesting. Once I got used to it, I rarely used meters and got used to looking at the image on those high-end monitors.
