The shadow detail was quite good, but the images weren't truly representative of what we were getting, so I had to mentally adjust for the difference based on our tests. I'd liken it to using a Hazeltine monitor to adjust printer lights — you're not looking at a true image, but a representational one, and you have to learn how to read it to get what you want.

(Harris Savides, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, April 2007, 41-42)

■ I found that when I did the *Ten Tiny Love Stories*, we had windows in the backgrounds and I had to worry about the highlights, and I didn't like how they were clipping, so all this hype about it being easier and cheaper, and you don't need lighting — it's ridiculous. So I experienced it first hand in that case. It can be more complicated to light, but in low light, it does have its applications.

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

## THINGS A DIRECTOR SHOULD KNOW ABOUT FORMATS

1. Know *why* you want to shoot in a certain format, be it film or digital and what your workflow for postproduction is going to be.
2. Understand the basic differences between formats, both aesthetically and economically. (And don't try to shoot outside your budget range.)
3. Know what your intended release format is going to be. Are you finishing to a film print or going directly to a digital format to master for DVD?
4. Be aware of image quality between a digital image and a film image. Which works best for your project?
5. Understand the archiving issues. How will the project be stored over time?
6. What really is the difference between the film image and the digital image? Select the one that best suits the story.
7. Don't assume because you are shooting digitally that it will be cheaper.
8. Do the same tests on the equipment as you would with film stock and processing.
9. Make sure you back up your hard drives after each day's work. If possible, shoot both tape and HD, so you have a back up in case the HD is corrupted.
10. Don't assume because you are shooting HD that it is going to be faster. Prep the same way you would for a film shoot.

## THE LAB, THE DI, AND ACHIEVING "THE LOOK"

The DI is the way to go now; it has become the standard. About four years ago I proposed to the ASC that they give a photochemical award and a DI award, one is based in reality and the other is what you can imagine. There were very mixed reactions: Some liked the idea; others adamantly disagreed. But today there are very few films done photochemically; most everything goes through a DI.

(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

Completing production and editing of a project is the beginning of the next stage of your journey, the finishing process. Once your project is at a picture lock and the sound is mixed, comes the next task to overcome, the one that takes you to the lab or postproduction facility on a daily basis. Until recently there was just one way to finish a film, the traditional photochemical process, but now there is a new technology that has recently evolved and taken over the way films are being completed: the digital intermediate or the DI.

### THE LAB & TELECINE

When shooting on film, whether it is 35mm or Super 16mm, the film has to go to the lab for processing of the negative. The film should be taken to the lab as soon as possible after shooting so that any problems such as a hair in the gate, a scratch, camera registration problem, or soft focus are known immediately while there is still a chance to reshoot, if necessary.

Once the negative has been developed, it goes through a telecine transfer to a tape format for viewing. The tape format depends on the equipment you have for editing. Transfers can be done on formats such as DVCAM, HD-CAM (1440x1080), HD-SR (1920x1080), or directly into a hard drive. During the transfer process, color corrections can be made to the dailies or just an overall one-light transfer for offline editing. If you know you will be color correcting the fine cut on FCP or if you plan an online transfer for color corrections after the edit, then a one-light transfer will suffice for offline editing.

During the transfer there will be a telecine operator who works to balance out the colors and exposure based on the color chip charts shot during production and makes adjustments to the film accordingly. The director and cinematographer should be present for this process to supervise the color corrections. Any corrections done during the telecine transfer will affect the entire scene. If more magenta or blue is added in or the exposure is brightened, not just one area but the entire scene will be affected. Certain exposure issues can be addressed during the transfer. The exposure latitude of the film allows for corrections three stops in either direction, so if a scene is two or three stops overexposed, it can be brought back down, and if a scene is underexposed, it can be brought back up. Keep in mind that bringing up underexposure also brings up grain. That is why both director and cinematographer should be present for a timed transfer to make these decisions, since they will affect the aesthetics of the image.

The telecine transfer can be done at a postproduction facility or a film lab that has a telecine machine in house. Basic adjustments — such as correcting tungsten to daylight, bringing down overexposure, or color correcting for fluorescents — can all be done during this process. If you have a lot of footage, it might be cost prohibitive to color correct every take, in which case, you could correct only the selects the director has chosen during production. Or you could do a one-light transfer and color correct your fine cut when you do the online edit. If you have a very short film and don't know what selects you will be using, you could color correct all the footage provided that you did not do an excessive number of takes.

Once the transfer is completed, the editor will input the footage into the system, where it will be edited on Final Cut Pro or Avid. If the footage is on a tape format you can screen it, preferably on a large screen, to check for soft focus shots or if there is something in the frame that shouldn't be there, like a light stand, a barn door, or the slate. If the footage is only viewed on a small computer screen or monitor, it is harder to see these problems. If you have been sitting with the telecine operator, you have probably already seen the problem shots and should make note of them in case you do not have access to a larger monitor once you begin editing. What you see on the computer screen is not necessarily how the film looks in terms of color rendition and exposure, which is why your editor should have an additional external monitor set up to see how the color and exposure really look.

## SCREENING DAILIES

It used to be common practice to have a one-light film print made for viewing dailies from the processed negative. That way the cinematographer and director could screen a film print of what they had shot the previous day. You have no doubt seen this process in films, even if you haven't done it yourself: when the lights go down in the screening room and the dailies are projected up on the big screen. When screening print dailies, there is no mistake in seeing what was captured on film. The

cinematographer will be checking exposure and the full frame composition of the shot. Unfortunately, this is not common practice anymore, although it is still done on some big budget films, where the director and cinematographer really want to see what was captured from celluloid to celluloid without the telecine operator correcting colors. What is screened, sometimes at higher speeds, is the un-color-corrected (one-light print) version of the film. If shooting in low light conditions the cinematographer can really see how the exposure was read on film.

There are still cinematographers and some directors who prefer screening printed dailies because they are seeing the full screen version of how the film emulsion reacted to the exposure and lighting. Since this is the way dailies have been screened for decades, there are directors and cinematographers who are just accustomed to this process and prefer it. But more often today, directors are viewing dailies on HD (high definition) or DVD.

■ *Spielberg still likes to watch print dailies every day and Janusz Kaminski goes and watches print dailies in the morning, at high speeds. That's fantastic — he kind of taught me that, to go see your dailies at the start of every day. When I printed dailies on Josie and the Pussycats, I went to the lab every morning, because it was so close to the place where I was staying. When I did Phone Booth, I went to the lab every morning. On Iron Man I printed dailies, and I would watch them in a projection trailer every morning. I watched the print, because that was the only way I could really determine what I was doing and achieving on film. I don't really trust telecine. The operators have too many options, and they could tweak colors, and I don't know if that's a true representation of what's on the negative. I don't want the exposure to get lost within the pipeline that goes into no man's land. Somebody has got to have a sense of what's there, so that if a director says, "can we make that darker, or is there going to be more there," I can have an honest answer because I've seen the film print.*

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

The postproduction process has been greatly altered by computer technology in the last decade. The traditional way in which film completion has been done for decades is quickly fading away. Editing on flatbeds and splicing film with tape seems archaic at this point. Computer editing was introduced as a new concept with the first Avid in the mid '90s, and almost two decades later, 99% of films are edited on a computer. Many filmmakers today have never even seen or touched a Steenbeck or Moviola flatbed. The concept of editing film to film is now considered a dinosaur.

The photochemical process of completing films to the print stage is still being done, but with the growing evolution of digital technology and the numerous attributes of doing a DI (digital intermediate), this is becoming less frequent. It is estimated that at least 90% of all films are being completed with a digital intermediate today. This number will inevitably increase within the next few years as costs of the DI are reduced and minor problems are resolved.

Initially, as recently as the year 2000, it was cost prohibitive to do a DI. It was more of a luxury then, since the photochemical process was a less expensive way to go. But the cost of doing DIs has become more competitive as more facilities are expanding

to include a DI suite, making it affordable for almost all productions. The integration of film and digital technology affords filmmakers possibilities that were never available to them before, and many are very excited about working with this process.

■ Spike loves the DI, so every film I do with him, I know we're going to do one. On *The Fountain*, with Darren Aronofsky, I knew going into it that we would do the DI. In 2001 when I shot Josie and the Pussycats, we didn't know if we could do the DI, because the studio was fighting against it. The only other film that had done a DI at that time was the Coen brothers' *O Brother Where Are Thou?* (2000), shot by Roger Deakins. So we used the same facility, and we all went through a lot of hiccups. By virtue of that, the two films I did after that were both photochemical. I wanted to stay away from the DI for a few years, and when I came back, [I saw that] they had worked out a lot of problems. I use EFILM now for my DIs. *The Fountain* was heavy effects, *Iron Man* was heavy effects, so I knew going into those that we'd be doing the DI.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

■ Even if you know there will be a DI, you don't necessarily know for sure that you're going to be there to do it. It's not as simple as being sloppy on the set, knowing you can clean your mess up later. I don't leave a lot of stuff for the DI if I can help it, and that was true on this movie [Mr. Brooks] as well. It's a funny thing, at the time I shot Pleasantville, the DI was considered by some to be sort of cheating. ("You did all this stuff to the image after the fact, so what good are you?") This year, every film nominated for the cinematography Oscar except one (*There Will Be Blood*, which won the Oscar) went through a DI. It's pretty standard, and nobody thinks twice about it, and I'm happy about that.

(John Lindley, ASC, International Cinematographers Guild, June 2007, 27)

## FINISHING PHOTOCHEMICALLY

Until very recently all films were completed through the photochemical process and had been for close to a hundred years. The film lab played a huge part in the completion of a film, where the negative was vaulted and stored, and various prints were made from existing intermediates for release prints. Color timing was done off of the prints that were struck at the lab and the entire finishing processing was done on celluloid.

■ For *There Will Be Blood*, no DVD dailies were made, and there was no digital intermediate (DI); everything was done photochemically. We've never done anything digitally except some visual effects. There are a few digital effects in this film, mostly involving the [creation of multiple] oil wells or the removal of modern elements from the landscape. If you know you're doing a DI, you can cheat a bit [on set], but you can't do that when you're shooting for Paul (Thomas Anderson).

(Robert Elswit, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, January 2008, 44)

The lab you choose to work with to complete the film plays a huge role in the completion process. They not only process the negative and prepare dailies, but they will ultimately be involved in the color timing and completion of the project to

![Two black and white film frames stacked vertically. The top frame shows a man sitting outdoors, looking down. The bottom frame shows a close-up of a person's face and arm, possibly in a dark setting.]()There Will Be Bloodwhatever final format the film print will be, whether it is a blow-up from Super 16mm to 35mm, 35mm prints, or a blow-up from Super 35mm to 70mm.

The basic process of completing photochemically is as follows: once the film is finished being edited, either on a computer system like Avid or FCP, an edit decision list (EDL) is generated. This is the guide to every shot used in the film, so that a negative cutter can do a match back and pull the original negative to create AB rolls of the film. It is a checkerboard process of alternating each shot with black leader on the opposite roll, or overlapping for dissolves.

A roll | <---scene 1---> | <---black leader---> | <---scene 3---> |

B roll | <--black leader-> | <-------scene 2-------> | <--black leader-> |

AB rolling is done in a clean room, where the negative cutter physically pulls the negative from the original negative rolls, matching back to each shot used in the film. When the ABs are completed, each strip is run through the printer, if white titles are needed the print film is run through again, exposing the C roll, containing the main titles or subtitles. Fades and dissolves are also made during this

time, overlapping the proper number of frames (24, 48, 72, or 96) for the dissolve. Meanwhile an optical sound print is made from the final sound mix, and that optical track is married onto the first answer print.

The process of creating the answer print is as follows: the print film will run through the printer three times, first exposing the A roll, rewinding, then exposing the B roll, rewinding again and adding on the optical soundtrack. The completed print, known as the “answer print,” contains all of the scenes in order without any visible splices as well as an in-sync soundtrack. It is the first completed print of the film with picture and sound.

The answer print (AP) is then screened for color corrections and to check the titles, dissolves, and any other optical effect that has been done to the film. It is the “answer” to how the exposure looks and how the overlapping dissolves and other processes will look on the finished print.

This A & B roll method is not always necessary for 35mm, because there is enough area around the frame line that is masked off in projection to permit splicing the film negatives into a single strand, which can be printed in one pass, instead of two, through the printer. The A & B rolls are still necessary for dissolves between scenes, as well as for superimposed images.

With widely distributed feature films needing large print runs exceeding 1500 prints, an intermediate process is created to save the valuable camera negatives. The A, B, and C (if needed) rolls are all printed onto an interpositive (IP). The (IP) has lower contrast than release-print stock because contrast builds up during the internegative (IN) and release print stages. The interpositive (IP) is then printed onto one or more internegatives, which are then used along with the separate soundtrack negative, containing optical tracks and any digital tracks or timecode that might be used for that particular film to make the theatrical prints. For foreign distribution, the C roll, which contains the titles, will also contain the subtitles, and is sometimes printed separately on its own interpositive and then both interpositives are printed onto the internegative(s). This is done so that different versions of a film's titles can be made in several languages for foreign prints. Subtitles for foreign prints can also be added by splicing them into the “title” interpositive.

If the film was shot on Super 16mm, it will need to be blown up to 35mm (IN/IP) during this process. Since Super 16mm uses the entire negative space for image, it must be blown up to 35mm to be screened as a film print theatrically.

While making film prints, you may notice that the print stock adds a bit more contrast to the images that you may not have seen on your dailies, which are usually a one-light transfer process. Once the colors are approved, you would make a tungsten print or a xenon print (which has more of a throw in terms of brightness) for screening purposes on film. If you choose to also have a DigiBeta or HD master for DVD authoring made, you will need to have a low contrast print made for that transfer, due to the higher contrast on print stock. Or the IN can be used for the mastering version (now an HD format) to author the DVD.

The costs involved in the photochemical process include striking the numerous prints, with the answer print being the most expensive print struck. The creation of the IN/IP is also costly, and if a blow-up from Super 16mm to 35mm is being done during this process, that adds a considerable extra cost. Postproduction can easily be over half the budget of a low-budget film (if you also include the sound mix). Once this has all been completed, the release prints are at a substantially reduced price from the cost of the initial print, depending on the quantity ordered.

■ *I'm either old fashioned or quite stubborn, or maybe both, but at the moment I don't really like DIs, and I'm not sure what the advantage to the process is if you're shooting anamorphic. I have a hard enough time making up my mind about things without going into a DI suite; I don't think I'd ever get out of there. The process creates too many options. At any rate, I don't like the way it looks.*

(Paul Thomas Anderson, *American Cinematographer*, January 2008, 44)

In the color timing of the photochemical process, any adjustments made affect the entire scene, not portions of it, so if you are adding magenta or blue it will affect the entire scene, from the skin color on the actors' faces to the trees in the background. That's the way it works. You leave with a film print in your hands, and when you thread it through a projector, everything has been done photochemically to celluloid, not digitally. If any alterations were done such as a bleach bypass, it affects the entire print from a photochemical process in the IP stage, so if you decide you don't want to use the skip bleach print, you can go back to the IN and make a standard print and the negative remains unaffected.

### *Would you ever finish another film photochemically?*

■ *Sure, although the last one I did was a while ago, The House of Sand and Fog (2003), which frankly I was quite glad we did photochemically, because Vadim Perelman, the director, was from commercials and was very into tweaking imagery. I was quite happy with what we did, and when he was in the lab watching the answer prints, he said "can't we change the contrast here, or can't we change..." and I said "no, this is the lab; this is film, it's not digital. We can change the colors or we can change the density, but basically, that's it, and we can't do anything on a per shot basis." Sometimes it's an advantage. In the old days you'd shoot what you wanted and that was it; you lived with it, and it was no problem.*

(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

### *Working with the lab to achieve the final look, do you still do it photochemically?*

■ *Occasionally. It depends on the movie; it's not my call. Another reason to get it right on the negative, is that you can't always depend on a DI; what if they run out of money? It happens*

all the time. Then what? So just shoot like old school and act accordingly. There is a real art to getting it right on the negative, and that is getting lost among the young people coming up. You've got to be so precise and on the ball with it; you can't let certain things go.... There is a whole philosophy and way of learning that I am afraid is being lost.

(Richard Crudo, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

## LAB PROCESSES TO ALTER THE COLOR PALETTE CHEMICALLY

In the photochemical process there are manipulations that can be done to the negative involving the alteration of chemicals to create a specific look to the film. There are the traditional manipulations that have been done to the negative during various stages of the process, from initial exposure (pushing or pulling), to timing the prints, to altering contrast, cross processing, and saturation. The film negative is an organic material alive with silver halide crystals that can be altered chemically to create specific effects.

The cinematographer will test various film stocks using conditions similar to those they will be shooting under and have the processes done at the lab to get an idea of how it will look when the film is completed. The process that is selected to get the “look” of the film will ultimately be what the director and cinematographer decided in preproduction for the color palette, whether it is de-saturated, high contrast, low contrast, saturated, grainy, or a combination of several processes. Certain colors should be tested for the background sets as well as wardrobe to see how they will read on film with the process being done to the film, since colors will be affected based on the specific photochemical process being used.

## BLEACH BYPASS

Bleach bypass is a color processing technique where the bleaching stage is either omitted (skipped) or reduced. The silver halide grains remain in the emulsion, which on a color negative is similar to superimposing a black-and-white image onto a color image. The result of this process is increased contrast in the darks and lights, reduced color saturation, and a more monochromatic color palette. This process can be done directly to the negative, with the effect on the print made from the negative resulting in blown-out highlights, higher contrast, and increased grain. When the process is applied to the print, the effects are evident in the shadow areas, making them darker and richer with less shadow detail and blacker blacks. If this process is done during the intermediate stage, it doesn't affect the negative at all, so if skip bleach is not desired by the producers or the studio, the negative can still be printed normally.

Doing any skip bleach process directly on the negative should be tested prior to production, because of the way in which the silver affects the colors in production

design and wardrobe once the film is processed. There are also varying degrees of how much silver can be left on the negative in terms of percentages that should be tested in the lighting environment in which they will be used. Each lab refers to this process of silver retention with a somewhat different name, but it all means leaving silver halide crystals on the color negative.

The bleach bypass process is used to de-saturate color and enhance the grain structure, which give films a more gritty look. If the story is a grim drama, this look enhances the story all the more by creating an ominous environment where color seems to be drained from the characters' worlds. This process has been used effectively in films such as *Traffic*, *Seven*, *Fight Club*, *21 Grams*, and *Babel*. Many films today are using variations of this process to de-saturate the intensity of colors in subtle ways. This technique is frequently used on genres such as suspense, drama, horror, and science fiction, but would generally not be seen in comedies, romantic dramas, children's films, or musicals. Films that are thematically light and comedic would avoid using a de-saturated color palette, since it is conveying a dark feeling or mood to the audience.

Skip bleach or bleach bypass is frequently done during the DI process now, which also doesn't affect the original negative, so alterations in color and contrast can be manipulated in the DI suite and adjusted accordingly. If skip bleach is done on the camera negative, it is not a reversible process so should be tested in advance on various sets and wardrobes.

On *21 Grams* cinematographer Rodrigo Prieto and director Alejandro Iñárritu selected the bleach bypass process because they wanted a raw, handheld visual style along with the de-saturation of colors.

■ *We were trying to get into the actors' eyes, to feel what they were feeling...each of three narratives has a visual design that evolves as the stories progress and converge. We were separating each story with colors we felt were appropriate. We pictured Paul's (Sean Penn) story in cool colors. By contrast we went for warmer colors for Jack (Benicio Del Toro), and Cristina's (Naomi Watts) story is presented neutrally, as something in between. In general the lighting is white, but her story mixes so much with Paul's that they both have blue-green night exteriors. And when they finally meet Jack, all three color schemes become slightly red-orange. We also played with different film stocks to keep the grain structures different contrasts as the stories developed.*  
(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, December 2003, 39-40)

■ *Prior to shooting we did many, many tests involving wardrobe, palettes of background colors, film stocks, and lighting colors. We didn't initially approach those choices in a way that actually gave them intellectual meaning; we just went with what we felt. We kept making tests and we arrived at the final scheme through discovery, not design.*  
(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, December 2003, 41)

![Black and white film still showing a close-up of a man's face, looking forward, with a hand visible near his head, suggesting a moment of tension or distress.]()![Black and white film still showing a person lying down, possibly injured or unconscious, with visible wounds on their face and arms, suggesting a scene of conflict or aftermath.]()![Black and white film still showing a person sitting up in bed, looking towards the camera, with a window visible in the background, suggesting a moment of reflection or recovery.]()21 Grams

## FLASHING

Flashing the film is another process that is used to de-saturate the color. This process can be done two different ways, through "neutral flashing" which is used to lower the apparent contrast and improve shadow and highlight detail. Or through "color flashing" which is used to create specific moods by lowering the contrast of the three-color layers on the negative unequally, depending on the artistic intention of the cinematographer. In both of these methods the film is subjected to a low intensity exposure, which causes an increase in the minimum density of each color layer in negative films and a decrease in the maximum density of each color layer in reversal films. When the negative is printed, the shadow areas should appear lighter and the overall color saturation will be reduced. The process can be done either before or after the film has been exposed, but again it should be tested prior to beginning any production.

Master cinematographer Vilmos Zsigmond, ASC, most notably used this technique in Robert Altman's film *McCabe and Mrs. Miller*, in combination with push processing and double fog filters to achieve the final effect. Many films will combine effects such as flashing with a skip bleach process, as was done on *Se7en*, photographed by Darius Khondji, ASC.

![A dark, grainy film still showing a scene from McCabe and Mrs. Miller, likely featuring the main characters.]()McCabe and Mrs. Miller

## FORCED PROCESSING

Also known as "pushing" the film, forced processing is basically making the film stock go beyond what it was rated to do — to push into darker areas more. This means underexposing the film, which in turn enhances the grain structure. It also means the negative is being overdeveloped to compensate for underexposure while shooting.

## CROSS PROCESSING

This process was developed by still photographers and involves taking reversal film stock (which renders a positive camera original) and running it through the chemical process that is used for negative films: basically putting the film through the wrong process for creative effect. The result is that a negative image appears on clear-based film, so it lacks the orange mask that negative films have. Depending on

how the film is exposed when shooting, the effects can range from subtle to quite radical in the rendering of contrast and grain. This process was used in the film *Three Kings* and the Mexican sequences with sharp contrast in *Traffic* (along with bleach bypass in other sections; see images on page 121).

Many of these techniques are now being recreated digitally through the digital intermediate process. But it is still a choice, with some cinematographers preferring to test the chemical reactions on celluloid. Eventually the DI will be the only way films are finished. Once that is the case, all the processes that were once done chemically will be done during the DI.

## THE DIGITAL INTERMEDIATE

The majority of major motion films today are completed with a digital intermediate. Since one of the first DIs was done on *O Brother, Where Art Thou?* (2000), photographed by Roger Deakins, ASC, many of the initial glitches in the process have been corrected. What was once a cost-prohibitive luxury has now become the new technology by which films are completed. The digital intermediate can be used from a film to tape output or a tape to film output. In both instances the data is scanned into the computer for color timing and correction.

The digital intermediate is an electronic system that takes the information captured on film or HD (Hi-Def) and scans it into a computer hard drive system. The basic process of doing a digital intermediate is that the film is scanned and digitized into the hard drive, conformed, and color corrected and when finished, recorded out to a film, tape, or a digital format. In the process many changes and decisions can be made in the digital realm in terms of color, shadow, and light. Unlike the photochemical color timing process, the entire scene is not necessarily affected, just certain areas, if that is what you choose. Early DIs were scanned at 2k, which reduces the data load to 12MB per frame while maintaining filmic color space. But the technology is still evolving and many agree that currently 4k data is the ideal format for the digital intermediate due to its filmic color representation. Higher scanning is currently in the works, increasing the data to 6k or 8k. The standard is still evolving and has not yet been set.

■ Right now you scan at 4k and you down res (lower the resolution) to do the corrections and then you up res to 4k again, you down res to 2k so you can work with the files. Wherever they land in the middle will be where those standards are going to end up. Whatever technology is used to down or up convert is going to determine that [standard] as well. All I know is that right now people are working in 2k, although they are scanning in 4k. Iron Man will be the first movie I've done that is scanned in 4k. Every movie I've ever done up to that had been scanned in 2k. I've fought against HD scans in the past. On one movie they wanted me to do a telecine scan to HD, and then use that HD material for the scan, and I said absolutely not, I'm not going to lose the film before I even have a chance to look at the film cut. It's a tonal range thing; why would I agree to that? It's like, hey, can we shoot you in the foot? It's an ongoing discussion as to what you are going to scan to and what you are going to output to. It's our

responsibility [as cinematographers] to know as much about that realm as possible, because the visual effects supervisors are really protecting themselves and their work, talking about different types of scans and bits and logs and ways to adjust the film. If you don't stay on top of that, you will be in for a big surprise if you do a big effects film, because you don't speak the language. The language has been altered: it's beyond timing lights and film processes, it's become digital nomenclature that we are going to have to understand or we're going to be left in the weeds.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

■ 4k scans are always better. I've been around since the genesis of the whole thing, and I remember when 2k was going to be the standard. Even at the very beginning, there were people saying that it made no sense. It's still evolving today. I remember one-inch tape and two-inch tape. It's scary for people to make huge investments in something that isn't going to be around forever.

(John Lindley, ASC, ICG, June 2007, 27)

The 4k 48MB per frame does increase the demand on processing time and storage. This is a glitch that is being worked out, since it is becoming the way in which films are completed. Initially quite cost prohibitive, DI facilities are popping up all over and rates are dropping as competition increases.

The DI is fast becoming the way films are completed rather than just an expensive option. Even traditional filmmakers like Martin Scorsese are now dipping into the digital world. His film *The Departed* is the first DI he and Michael Ballhaus, ASC, have done together.

![Black and white photo of a man, likely Martin Scorsese, wearing a suit and tie, looking slightly off-camera.]()Departed

■ The DI is the most significant contribution that has happened to image making since I've been a cinematographer. My experiences with it have been quite good. Obviously doing *O Brother, Where Art Thou?* was quite taxing, because it took a long time, since the system we were working on was like a telecine system. We were timing from the original negative for ten to twelve weeks and that was horrendous, not just because we were worried about the negative being up there, but the time it took. But now you basically scan the negative once, and you've got a digital file that you manipulate. You can do different timings and watch them, and you can flip through different versions of the timings to see which way you want to go. You could never do

that when we were doing O Brother, so the advances are enormous in this area since that time, because of the memory in the storage systems.  
(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

I love the DI. It's one of the greatest tools we've ever had. Some of the boys are disagreeing with it, because they want to stay traditional. I think they're crazy. Because you always have the problem of actors and emotion. If you've got an actress and she's all wound up, she's going to cry; it's a very emotional scene for four hours, and you're changing angles and relighting and things like that. I always have an appreciation for the actors' problems—that they have to maintain this emotion for 4 or 5 hours—and I'm trying to help as much as I can by going as fast as I can with the relighting...so that it's only 6-1/2 minutes and not an hour.... But what I've found with the DI is you can help them even more, because if all of a sudden the sun goes in and it goes black, and you think, oh my gosh, I've got to peel that ND off the window, but the actress is crying and she's ready—I just say okay, I'm ready, and I go, because now I know I can rotoscope the window, pump it back up, and put it in again. So because of the DI, the actress can do her thing, and we're not slowing her up. Instead of saying, sorry, it's going to be 20 minutes and throwing a ladder outside and peeling off the gel—rubbish, you just rotoscope it and re-trim it to match.... If you can't get a light into their eyes and see their emotion, you can put sunnies on and pump their eyes up and, pop, there are the eyes. Lots of little things you can do to make a better film. Not necessarily to make us [cinematographers] look better, but just to make a better film.  
(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

I don't think the DI exists on its own; it's just another tool. Some people use it well, and some people don't. I see movies that don't look good, because they've used it too much. Then I see movies where it's used a lot for manipulation, and I think it looks great. It just depends whose hand it's in, whether it's a tool for good or a weapon. I don't think it has a personality. I think it has the personalities of the people who use it.  
(John Lindley, ASC, ICG, June 2007, 28)

It's always about working with the pipeline. The great danger of cinematographers coming up now is that I think a big part of the craft of cinematography will be lost, something like timing lights will be a thing of the past. The ASC is spending a great deal of time and energy working with DIs so that cinematographers can work with the same nomenclature—why? It doesn't really matter, honestly. I do believe in printing, because it's the only honest way to see what you really shot; print it, see what it does photochemically. That's just me, because that's how I came up. Cinematographers had to understand their pipeline in their day. Connie Hall and Gordon Willis had to know how they were going to go through it, knowing they were going to go through the intermediates, knowing how many prints they were going to make and how many prints were going to come off their original negative. It hasn't really changed. I think cinematographers know their pipeline. If they are going to do a DI, who is going to do their scan? Are they going to scan at 2k or 4k? Are you going to use a Luster; what system are you going to use? What system are you going to use to output; is it going to be Arri laser or is it going to be Northlight, and what negative stock is it going to be printed on? Once you understand the pipeline that you are going to go [through] then [you can] test from that standpoint and sort of reverse engineer what you are going to do. It's our responsibility as technicians to do that.  
(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

## “FIX IT IN POST”

Quite often on set, directors needing to make their day and move on may throw around the phrase “we’ll fix in post.” There are misconceptions regarding how much or what exactly can be fixed during post. There are numerous things that can be fixed, such as adding a bit of an eye light, or softening up facial imperfections, or changing the color of something, eliminating a shadow in the background, many things, but not everything. A film must still be exposed properly and in focus and performances must be excellent. The general lighting and color palette the cinematographer is going for should be present during production, so they can be enhanced or tweaked during the DI process in post.

■ One thing with a DI that complicates matters is that a director is expecting that you can do anything with the DI, and it's not really like telecine. In telecine you can do more than a DI because you have the print on film. Sometimes I lit in a certain way, where I lose detail in certain shadows and I want that, but on telecine there is detail there, because the negative has it (details), but the final print won't have the same details. So the director sees their dailies on video and they see all these things that you won't have on the final print, but they want it. So I've been suffering with DIs a bit, because they sometimes want to brighten the shadows more than was my intention. How do you get dailies to look the way your print will look? The difference between how the negative responds and the print is just not the same. I am now using EFILM, where you scan the negative in from the beginning so you see dailies that are color corrected to match with the look of a print. So hopefully our dailies will look more like the end result. For visual effects, you can color correct as you go and it stays with it. That will make the DI, in the end, more effective and will take less time. Then everyone agrees where you are going, so there's less tweaking in the end.

(Rodrigo Prieto, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ The DI has changed the way I shoot quite a lot. But you can't do everything. There's a total misunderstanding in some courts about this, usually [among] the people who hold the purse strings — the thought that you can fix anything on the DI. Maybe you can in CGI with computer graphics, but you can't in a straightforward DI suite. You can do a lot. It certainly helps balancing sequences where the light is not matching.... You can certainly help it with adjusting color and contrast. You can suppress certain colors. Whatever you can do in Photoshop on your laptop, you can do in a DI suite. It's a really important tool.

(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

■ I was infuriated on the set of Iron Man once because we were at Edwards Air Force Base, and I had requested a wet down and they didn't bring the wet down trucks. They just had firemen who didn't know what we were doing, and they thought we were just going to wet down one little area. I wanted the whole thing wet down. They usually bring a water truck down and spray water in a zigzag pattern across and it's done. So they say, “okay, are we ready to shoot?” The water is all drying up, and I say “no, the wet down sucks,” and the director says “don't worry, we're going to fix it in the DI.” [I knew he was] kidding, but it infuriates me when they think the DI can fix anything!

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

Knowing that a digital intermediate is going to occur in post is helpful to the cinematographer so they can make various choices on set. For example, if they know for sure a DI will be done, they can move faster with some of the lighting situations, knowing it can be corrected during the DI. If not, they may need to take the extra time to set an additional light or flag off a wall. Of course it is still essential to light the set the way it is intended, to get good exposures, and sharp focus. But knowing that a DI will be done can expedite certain situations.

## Has the DI changed the way you shoot?

■ I would be lying if I said it didn't change the way I shoot a film. For example, if I'm on a set and there's a light on the wall, I know I can take it down later. It gives me the option of shooting S16mm and blowing it up without having an optical blow-up. That makes Super 16mm a viable choice for a film. The one look that is kind of easy to recreate in the DI is high contrast, so if I want a higher contrast, more colorful look, I may expose the stock a little differently knowing that I'm going to do that. I might overexpose the stock and give it a lot of density so I know I can do a lot of manipulation later. After testing, it makes me expose things differently if I know I'm going to manipulate it later. What's difficult to achieve is a more subtle effect, to get the zone system of rich blacks and crisp highlights, to get that in a DI. I don't care what they say, you are going to lose that magical tonal range in the middle, and that's what people don't understand. There is a discernible difference between having a mid range of six stops as opposed to four. You look at how crisp the photography is in a film like Manhattan (shot by Gordon Willis); it's anamorphic, no blow-up, a tonal range that's out of this world —it's not going to happen in the DI. In a DI for that film, you would basically have to shoot it right, input it right, and maintain the look; it's not about tweaking it and manipulating it.  
(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

■ [In the case of] a hot wall or something, the gaffer will say "that wall is hot; should we knock it down or something?" and I'll just say, "DI." He'll say, "I can do it," and I'll ask, "how long — 20 minutes?" He'll say, "yeah?" I'll say, "no, let's go, we'll roll, we're ready, just DI," and we can track it out as the camera pans. It's fantastic.  
(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

■ We can flip a shot right to left. There are all kinds of shortcuts. You can throw in a window, add highlights, so you don't have to do as much flagging on set. You can move much faster.  
(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ We can do some filtration gags to soften details. They build a window and isolate areas, and they can even track it and let the shot move and it will record that tracking for the shot and put in that correction. It's interesting, the manipulations are fantastic.  
(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

■ The DI is not a cure for sloppy cinematography on set. It is something that you use in post that makes it that extra 3% better. Occasionally, you can take a little less time to do something, but as a rule that can't be your thought process. Otherwise you're going to have a really ragged-looking movie. Because letting one thing go breeds another... and before you know it, the whole

thing becomes sloppy. So the DI is a tremendous tool, but you still have to get it on that negative as always, get it on there, chisel it in stone, and then go and turn it into a Michelangelo.  
(Richard Crudo, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ The DI has definitely altered the way I shoot a film, because I know what I can get away with and what's easily fixable and acceptable in manipulating the film. But it's a scary time, really, for me, because I think as cinematographers we are committing ourselves to much more time in postproduction. But the more precisely I shoot the film, whether it's under- or overexposure, then its only going to help me get to the look of the film faster. Number 23 [directed by Joel Schumacher] is a great example. There's something about shooting anamorphic; it just comes off looking good, it just tastes good, and all of a sudden it was easy. I was finished in five days, because it was shot the way I wanted it be shot; every look that was created in the film I had already done in camera. So I was basically timing the movie. But to go in thinking you are going to create a look — if you haven't already thought about the look before shooting it — you're not going to create a look. Plus you've got to finish the movie! Going into the DI, if there is something you are trying to achieve that you haven't already baked in, if you haven't shot it correctly, then it's not going to go that way. It's just the nature of it. You are laying all these digital artifacts on top of an organic image. The grain is going to come out; you're going to be too bright, too dark; people are going to complain; it's just a disaster. In all honesty, if you don't bake it in, it won't represent itself in the dailies, and the director is not going to accept it in the end. They are going to ask, why does it look like that [now, when] it didn't look like that before? Since the advent of Avid, they gotten used to watching everything on such bright monitors that when they finally get to the film, the editor may say, "it's a lot darker than I expected." Well, they've been staring at it for months on a bright screen; of course, it's going to be dark for them.  
(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

## What are the basic things you need to do when you are shooting for a DI?

■ You always want a good solid negative. I always shoot a slightly thick negative. The colorists seem to love it. I overexpose a little, one ASA lower on the meter. For 500 I shoot 400, for 250 I shoot 200, for 200 I shoot 160, 100 I got to 80, 50 I shoot 40, which gives you a quarter to a half. The colorists like a good negative because they are not locked and they are freer to create. If it was shot underexposed, it is difficult to fix. Skip bleach on a negative you can undo; you can go back and put it back in the soup and put the rest of the bleach in.  
(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007.)

## ADAPTING TO CHANGE

Adapting to working with a new technology still has some glitches to iron out. From the first DIs in the late '90s to today, things have been steadily improving in terms of technical issues of compression and economic concerns. But on top of that, ethical issues have arisen, such as how the integration of the DI is affecting the way cinematographers have been working for years. Decisions are being made in terms of lighting and color correction in postproduction that were typically the

domain of the cinematographer, which means the cinematographer needs to be in the DI suite to make sure the light and color remains how it was intended when the film was conceived and photographed. Some of the problems have to do with the integrity of the image: What if suddenly the director has a different idea, or the producer or even the studio, has a different idea about how the film should look? Which DI will be the one that is used for the film that is released in theaters?

When timing a film photochemically, cinematographers were generally committed to an additional week or two at the lab to make sure the prints were tweaked prior to release. Now that time is expanding, since the time in a DI suite can go from two to four weeks, which means that a cinematographer who is already shooting another film now has to go back and forth to make sure the correct decisions are being made on the DI. So there is still some controversy and debate over this new technology and the dust has not yet settled. New negotiations in contracts will need to be addressed as well as the issue of authorship of the image.

■ *There's the issue of not being paid for the time spent doing the DI; the studio does not want to put it in your contract. So, the question is, do I shepherd the film through the final stages to maintain the integrity of the cinematography? Or do I not do it because I'm not getting paid? It's a sore subject for me. Sometimes I say on set, "I don't know who is going to be there to fix it (in post), because it's not going to be me." It's one of those conveniences the studio has now.*  
(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

**There are some people who say bad things can happen  
after you deliver a good negative, because anyone can  
alter the look of the film — the director, the producer, the  
cinematographer — and whose DI is the winner?**

■ *That's the big problem, but it goes back to the basic relationship you have with the director, whether it works or doesn't work. If it doesn't work, you could get into a situation where the director ends up doing the DI. I heard of one case where the cinematographer had done the DI, and the director went in and re-did it afterward. It's a hard one. Every film I have done a DI on, I have been the one in the DI suite, and then the director comes in and watches it and we discuss it, and make changes or not, depending. When it was straight lab work, you could be out shooting another film, and there was not much they could do; they would send you an answer print and you made some notes and called the color timer, and they would do another print, and you didn't have to be there. But with the DI, you have to be physically in the suite. The biggest advance that I have done this year was while I was shooting Revolutionary Road in Connecticut with Sam (Mendes) and I needed to time both The Assassination of Jessie James by the Coward Robert Ford and In the Valley of Elah. So Ethan set me up with this digital suite in Connecticut, this portable system, basically a 20' screen and the whole works that you would get in a digital suite in Hollywood, and in the evenings and weekends I went in and did digital timing. During the day the timer was working on my corrections, and in the evenings I would sit with him, and we would go through and make adjustments. So that was a big advance.*

It was funny, because here I was shooting the film for Sam (Revolutionary Road) and timing Jesse James... when I was shooting Jesse James, I was flying every weekend from Calgary to L.A. to do the digital timing of Jive, which was Sam's last film and going back to Canada to shoot Jesse James. I'd catch a late plane on Sunday at 11 p.m. and be shooting the next day. I was exhausted.

So it's a huge advance that they can set up a portable facility so that I could work in the same location on both projects. On Elah I did a pass in Connecticut and sent it to L.A. It's not a big deal, because it's a program that affects the original files, which could be done over the Internet, so Paul (Haggis) would watch a version of the film. Then he spent a few days doing a pass on it himself. When I came back, we sat down together and watched what he did and what I did and talked about it, and that's how it ended up. If I hadn't been able to do it on location, I would have been in trouble on that one, because you definitely need to be there, you and the director to see it, and you need to have that time with the director to explain if something you have done is very different than the cutting copy that was done from the HD file. A director watches a scene over and over while it's being cut. It may not be the way you originally intended it when you shot it, but now they are accustomed to it, so when you go back to timing it the way you intended, you have to remind them of your original idea.

The big issue now is you shoot a shot and conceptually you've got an idea in your head that you might not see printed, and it gets transferred and it's totally different. Then on the Avid, they flatten it out so it's easier to see when they cut; then they manipulate it for a screening for somebody else, but you're not there doing it. So by the time you see what you originally intended to do when you photographed it, it might be quite different.

(Roger Deakins, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

■ I've had some experiences in the DI where you just nod your head, and you can see where it is going unless you protect it. It goes back to having a good relationship with your producers and the director. If you are on the same page, that's all that you can hope for. If you all lose together, then you all lose together, because somebody else changed it, but if you lose within that relationship, then that's where it gets grim. Not losing the mutual trust, and hopefully not losing the power of the timing session, which [is] something that the studios are fighting against right now.

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

■ I had a terrible DI once — a disaster, because of the control they [the studio] can get, what they can do with the image that I had created on the negative. Where I feel it was just a polishing thing, they were changing the whole concept of the visuals.

It is, and it is going to continue to be, a downside for all cinematographers. There are terrible stories, like directors not telling the DP that they are doing a DI, and then he does it. Or the power hungry executive producer who does his own DI. This is a true story: the director is doing a DI in one room, and down the hall the DP is doing his DI, and unbeknown to him the executive producer is upstairs doing his own! He's not a cinematographer; he's a storyboard artist made good — it's frightening what can happen, what will happen.

(John Seale, ASC, Frost interview, October 2007)

■ There are nightmare stories where the director changes the entire look of the film. Is that ethical? Well, ultimately it is his film. But as a cinematographer, you are hoping you work with good people who will not violate your intentions as they were initially discussed before shooting.

(Daniel Pearl, ASC, Frost interview, July 2007)

■ *The first DI I did, for Josey and the Pussycats in 2001, Universal didn't want to do. They said it [the technology] wasn't ready. Seven years later, it's the only way they want to do a film, only because all the deliverables are wrapped up in a nice little bow, and they give it to them and they like that, and they have all this "control." It's not coming from a good place; it's coming from a place of convenience for them, and I have a problem with that and the idea of fixing it.*

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

Along with the continuing evolution of digital technology in terms of image capture is the continued expansion in postproduction technology in terms of image completion. Filmmakers in the early 1900s continued experimenting with cameras and film stock until a standard was reached, which was utilized and maintained for decades. The advent of sound altered the way silent films were made and that technology was improved upon and a standard was set. Color replaced black-and-white images, a wide-screen projection system lured audiences back into the theaters in the 1950s when television came along. Improved sound, lenses, film stock, and post-production sound made the motion picture experience more encompassing. The creation of special effects and green screen enabled filmmakers to create images they never could before.

We are in the midst of a technological evolution in the creation of motion pictures which has been evolving and expanding for over a decade. It has not yet settled or standardized, but it is close. This new technology will set the standard for the way in which films are made now and in the future. Cinematographers and directors who are coming up now will have to learn this technology and the new nomenclature, and understand the pipeline for the execution and completion of their projects.

■ *A lot of new DI facilities are opening up, but you have to do a lot of research and development. You can't just set up a 2k projector and say, okay, we do DIs now. It's not easy. What does the film look like at the end? Until the same image is projected off the 2k projector that is projected into a movie theater, the be-all, end-all result is going to be a film print — and unless it looks better than that, then a facility is not ready to handle it.*

(Matthew Libatique, ASC, Frost interview, September 2007)

Some feel there is something being lost along the way in the development of this new digital technology — as the more hands-on method of dealing with celluloid is being altered into a computer-generated medium. But the essence of why we make films continues: the creation of stories that transport us into another world. The new technology and the continuing advances in CGI will make the creation of other worlds more possible.

## WHAT A DIRECTOR SHOULD KNOW

1. Understand the pipeline in which you are working, the selection of image capture, whether it is film or HD, and how it goes down the line to the final product.
2. Be aware of what CAN and what CANNOT be fixed in post!
3. Know the color palette of the film prior to shooting. Do not expect a color palette to be created in postproduction without knowing what you are going for along the way.
4. Don't get lost in the technology. Remember first and foremost that you are a storyteller using a medium to create your story.
5. Maintain attention and focus on your talent and continue to communicate with your cinematographer, who is your closest associate on set.
6. Maintain communication and respect the intentions of your cinematographer during the creation of the digital intermediate. It should be done together, maintaining the integrity of what was originally discussed in preproduction.
7. Since it is a technology that is still developing, be aware that some glitches could come up along the way in terms of the "pipeline." Back up all of your footage in another format in case there is a problem.
8. Consider the archival issues. What format do you want your project to be stored on? Costs of HD storage are expensive and not proven. A back-up film negative should be considered.
9. Respect the time and intentions of the cinematographer. Maintain the collaboration that was established in the early stages of preproduction.

## A HISTORY OF CREATIVE COLLABORATORS

My job is not just about how a film looks. It's about making sure I'm communicating visually what the director intended. It's still the director's film. I try to remain the partner.

(Nancy Schreiber, ASC, Frost interview, November 2007)

The creative collaboration between the cinematographer and director dates back to the beginning of cinema itself. The first historic pairing of a director and cinematographer was D.W. Griffith and Billy Bitzer. Their collaboration created the first true cinema in America with *Birth of a Nation* (1915), a film that is historically important for its pioneering use of a dynamic technical means of storytelling that had never been seen before. Among his many innovative achievements, Billy Bitzer is credited for being the first cinematographer to use artificial lighting on a film. Together he and Griffith created the iris shot, the fade out, the use of a backlight, and a moving camera. In addition they even shot from an air balloon to get an aerial perspective of the gates of Babylon for the epic film *Intolerance*. Bitzer and Griffith were the first to free the camera from a static position and did numerous tracking shots, which revolutionized the new medium of film. Today we take for granted that camera motion and certain types of lighting tell us something about the human experiences of characters in a film; Griffith and Bitzer were among the first to create such meaning.

In the course of forty-three years, Griffith and Bitzer collaborated on hundreds of films, starting with one-reel shorts in 1908 and ending in 1951 with a film entitled *Footlight Varieties*, the last film Bitzer shot before his death.

As it happened, the two not only rose to fame together but also fell into obscurity together when the influence of German Expressionism changed the look and feel of American cinema. Some of their most successful films include *The Battle at Elderbush Gulch*, *Judith of Bethulia*, *Birth of a Nation*, and *Intolerance*.

There are many great cinematographers from the silent era who also worked successfully in sound films. One notable example is Gregg Toland, ASC, who

![A black and white still from the film Birth of a Nation, showing a man in a suit interacting with a woman in a light dress in an indoor setting.]()Birth of a Nation

worked with auteur directors John Ford, Howard Hawks, Erich von Stroheim, King Vidor, William Wyler, and Orson Welles. Toland was nominated for the Academy Award five times and won the Oscar for *Wuthering Heights*. Although he died at 44, his prolific career remains inspirational, perhaps especially for his work with Orson Welles on *Citizen Kane*. Welles thought so highly of Toland that he shared the title card with him, a singular gesture in Welles' career on a film that holds its place as one of the most celebrated films in American film history.

![Title card from Citizen Kane. The card is dark gray with white text. The top line reads: ORSON WELLES. The second line reads: Direction-Production. The third line reads: GREGG TOLAND, A.S.C. The bottom line reads: Photography.]()Title card from *Citizen Kane*

Much later, on films that were in their day not considered as “high art” as those of Griffith or Welles, the collaboration between director Alfred Hitchcock and cinematographer Robert Burks lasted over a decade. They made twelve films together. The two created the distinctive and unified look of some of Hitchcock’s most memorable

films in America: *Strangers on a Train* (1951), *I Confess* (1953), *Dial M for Murder* (1954), *Rear Window* (1954), *To Catch a Thief* (1955), *The Trouble with Harry* (1955), *The Man Who Knew Too Much* (1956), *The Wrong Man* (1956), *Vertigo* (1958), *North by Northwest* (1959), *The Birds* (1963), and their last collaboration, *Marnie* (1964).

■ Burks proved to be the perfect cinematographer for Hitchcock, who also had an encyclopedic knowledge of special effects and often wrote scenes that would create the opportunity to indulge in unusual camera imagery.  
(<http://www.theasc.com/protect/nov98/collab/pg.4htm>)

![Black and white still from the film Vertigo, showing a close-up of a man and a woman looking at each other.]()Vertigo

Although Hitchcock was nominated for an Academy Award five times, he never won for direction. Robert Burks, however, was nominated for an Academy Award for the black-and-white cinematography of *Strangers on a Train* (his first collaboration with Hitchcock), and also for the color photography of *Rear Window*. He also won an Oscar for his work on *To Catch a Thief*. The collaboration between Hitchcock and Burks ended when the cinematographer tragically died in a fire. It was said that Hitchcock was devastated by the loss.

In the 1950s the collaboration between director Douglas Sirk and Russell Metty produced the stylistic look of Sirk's classic melodramas, including his most popular films: *Magnificent Obsession* (1954), *All That Heaven Allows* (1955), *Written on the Wind* (1956), *There's Always Tomorrow* (1956), *A Time to Love and a Time to Die* (1958), and *Imitation of Life* (1959). Sirk and Metty experimented with color coding, symbolism, the use of mirrors, dramatic lighting effects, and cranes — all with a sophisticated visual style that made the films much more than just “women’s weepies.” Douglas Sirk’s films are subversive commentaries on the social climate of America in the 1950s, underscored by the richness of Technicolor to amplify his themes.

Director Todd Haynes and cinematographer Edward Lachman, ASC, paid homage to Sirk and Metty with their 2002 film *Far From Heaven*.

■ We wanted the audience to enter Sirk's world, so we decided to be true to the means he used to create this world of total artifice. We decided not to use any techniques that Sirk and his cinematographer Russell Metty didn't have. We wanted to create a saturated "Technicolor" look, but we didn't want to use digital methods.  
(Edward Lachman, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, December 2002, 63-64)

![Black and white film still showing a woman leaning out of a car window, with a man visible inside the car.]()![Black and white film still showing a man looking out a window, framed by vertical bars or curtains.]()Written on the Wind

Douglas Sirk and Russell Metty collaborated on ten pictures together. When asked how they started working together, Sirk replied:

■ I saw some pictures he had done and I asked Universal to get him. He was very expensive, and very much in demand, but I finally succeeded. We always agreed about everything: we just had the same way of seeing things, and we had a great time working together. I liked working with a group. I am sure this way of working with a group stems from the theatre, where I always had my own troupe.

(Douglas Sirk, *Sirk on Sirk*, [London: Faber and Faber, 1971], 100)

Russell Metty also shot the 1957 classic *Touch of Evil*, where Orson Welles revisited his high-contrast lighting, and unique camera angles and compositions. He and

![Black and white still from the film 'Touch of Evil' showing three men in suits, one wearing a fedora, looking upwards.]()Touch of EvilMetty also executed the famous opening sequence, which still remains one of the most well-choreographed crane shots in American film history.

The collaboration of Sven Nykvist, ASC, and Ingmar Bergman lasted for three decades, and they became one of the most famous teams in international film history. Their work together established Bergman as a filmmaker who essentially created his own genre. Together they created the “Bergman film,” with highly stylized compositions and meticulous lighting, and as with the other collaborative pairs discussed, at a level of quality widely compared to the great artists in other art forms. They worked together first on *Sawdust and Tinsel* in 1953, for which Nykvist shared the cinematography credit with Hilding Bladh. On subsequent films, however, Nykvist continued to work with Bergman — with the sole cinematography credit — on many other films: *The Virgin Spring* (1959), *Through a Glass Darkly* (1961), *Winter Light* (1962), *The Silence* (1963), *Persona* (1966), *The Hour of the Wolf* (1968), *The Passion of Anna* (1970), *Cries and Whispers* (1972), *The Magic Flute* (1973), *Scenes from a Marriage* (1973), *Face to Face* (1976), *Autumn Sonata* (1978), *The Serpent's Egg* (1978), *Fanny and Alexander* (1983). Nykvist won Oscars for both *Cries and Whispers* and *Fanny and Alexander*.

As with Sirk and Metty, perhaps this level of collaboration has something to do with the tradition of tight ensemble work in theater. In Sweden, Bergman had an excellent reputation in theater, and like a stage director, he has frequently used the same actors and crew from project to project. To an extent the same tradition applies to Griffith and Bitzer, who worked in the early film period in which the stage play was an important model for film, and some of their innovations — such as intensity of focus or lighting changes at key plot points — have their counterpart in the use of stage-lighting and blocking. In any case, the especially intense connection of artists exemplified by the director/cinematographer relationship is patently clear in the mutual respect and understanding noted between them by Bergman and Nykvist.

![Black and white film still showing a close-up of a person's face, heavily shadowed, with a hand pressed against the side of the head or face, suggesting intense emotion or distress.]()![Black and white film still showing a close-up of a woman's face (likely Bibi Andersson as Elisabet Vogler in Persona) looking intensely, with a hand resting on her head. A second person is partially visible behind her.]()Persona

![Black and white film still showing a close-up of two faces in profile, likely from The Passion of Anna. The face on the left is heavily shadowed and appears distressed, while the face on the right is more visible.]()The Passion of Anna

Regarding his collaboration with Sven Nykvist, Bergman said:

■ We've developed a private language, so to speak. We hardly need to say a word. Before the filming begins we go through the film very carefully, to see how we imagine the lighting, check the lighting conditions, and then solve all lighting problems together.  
(Ingmar Bergman, Bergman on Bergman, 206)

■ Ingmar Bergman is unique. I have had the privilege of working with him since 1953 and through him, I have learned to better understand the ultimate possibilities of cinematography.  
(Sven Nykvist, [www.theasc.com/protect/nov98/collab/pg.4.htm](#))

Woody Allen paid homage to Bergman with his film *Interiors* (1978), which was photographed by Gordon Willis, ASC. Allen also made it a point to work with Sven Nykvist, ASC, himself on three of his films: *Another Woman* (1989), *Crimes and Misdemeanors* (1989), and *Celebrity* (1998).

![Black and white photo of Woody Allen and Diane Keaton smiling together, likely from the film Annie Hall.]()Annie Hall

Woody Allen has had frequent collaborations with two cinematographers in his long and prolific career. Allen and Gordon Willis, ASC, collaborated on eight of Allen's best films, starting in the 1970s with *Annie Hall* (1977), *Interiors* (1978), *Manhattan* (1979), *Stardust Memories* (1980), *A Midsummer Night's Sex Comedy* (1982), *Zelig* (1983), and *Broadway Danny Rose* (1984). Their collaboration culminated with *The Purple Rose of Cairo* (1985).

After that point, Allen took up a twelve-film collaboration with cinematographer Carlo Di Palma, starting with *Hannah and Her Sisters* (1986) and continuing with *Radio Days* (1987), *September* (1987), *Alice* (1990), *Shadows and Fog* (1992), *Husbands and Wives* (1992), *Manhattan Murder Mystery* (1993), *Bullets Over Broadway* (1994), *Don't Drink the Water* (for television, 1994), *Mighty Aphrodite* (1995), *Everyone Says I Love You* (1996), and finally *Deconstructing Harry* (1997). Allen's is a remarkable record of twenty films with only two cinematographers. Lately, Allen has been more eclectic with his selection of cinematographers — working with Vilmos Zsigmond, ASC, for *Cassandra's Dream* (2007) and with Harris Savides, ASC, for his 2009 film *Whatever Works*.

The somber films of Bergman and the more serious side of Allen's films were served by these collaborations. In a partnership that has involved films very different in mood and storytelling, Martin Scorsese and Michael Ballhaus, ASC, have collaborated on seven films to date starting with *After Hours* (1985), *The Color of Money* (1986), *The Last Temptation of Christ* (1988), *Goodfellas* (1990), *The Age of Innocence* (1993), *Gangs of New York* (2002), and *The Departed* (2006). Ballhaus was nominated for an Academy Award for his work on *Gangs of New York*.

![Black and white still from Goodfellas showing two men, one smiling, possibly in a dimly lit interior setting.]()Goodfellas

■ *Marty is my favorite director, because he's the most visual filmmaker I've worked with in America, but if you have a philosophy about violence, you'd better put it aside when you work with him. In general, I'm not a big fan of violence, but in Marty's case, I accept it. If I had an offer from a different director to shoot this type of movie, I might not do it. Sometimes you can feel hurt or insulted by the violence, but the world Marty is portraying is violent, and the way he presents those scenes tells you something about the characters. So I see a reason for it in terms of the story.*

(Michael Ballhaus, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, October 2006, 36-37)

The first film Ballhaus shot for Scorsese was the offbeat, New York comedy *After Hours*, an unusual film for Scorsese, made after the one-two punch of *Raging Bull* (1980) and *The King of Comedy* (1982) with Robert De Niro. Disappointed by the success of the later film, Scorsese decided to do something very different and *After Hours* was the result.

■ *After Hours* was made for \$4 million, and it was right down my aisle, because it was shot fast and almost entirely at night. Marty wasn't used to that kind of speed, but I was very glad we could do it that way. We had to complete 16 shots every night, and Marty got every shot he wanted. He was very happy, and at the end of the shoot, he told me the experience gave him a new feeling for how you can make movies. Since then, we've collaborated whenever our schedules

have allowed it. Over the years, of course, the projects have gotten bigger, and now we usually have more time in the schedule.

(Michael Ballhaus, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, October 2006, 38-39)

![Black and white photograph of a soldier, likely from the movie Platoon, holding a rifle.]()Platoon

The collaboration between Oliver Stone and Robert Richardson has produced some of America's most notable political statements on film. Their collaboration includes eleven films to date: *Salvador* (1986), *Platoon* (1986), *Wall Street* (1987), *Talk Radio* (1988), *Born on the Fourth of July* (1989), *The Doors* (1991), *JFK* (1991), *Heaven and Earth* (1993), *Natural Born Killers* (1994), *Nixon* (1995), and *U-Turn* (1997). The association between the two is reminiscent of the collaboration between D.W. Griffith and Billy Bitzer, because like Bitzer, Richardson achieves a new visual style for every film they shoot together. His camera is always alive with movement and the collage use of textured imagery from various formats powerfully amplifies emotions. In several of their films, Richardson and Stone have combined Super 8 reversal stocks, black-and-white 16mm film, and various video formats interwoven with vintage stock footage and 35mm color anamorphic images carefully intercut by Stone and his editors.

The collaboration on *Natural Born Killers* was a challenge for Richardson and pushed the boundaries of their friendship. According to Richardson "the situation was quite clearly that I didn't want to do the film. I simply didn't have the level of respect that I'd had for the written material on, say, *Born on the Fourth of July* or *JFK*. Both of these aroused in me a great historical respect and intellectual curiosity." Stone had requested, as a friend, that Richardson stay on the film with him and Richardson agreed, "only out of love for Oliver and our relationship." Once the project began it was difficult, but, "in the end all my strife was what provided me with the creative juices to do my work. Had I been in love with the material, I might not have been so aggressive in my approach; my angst wouldn't have been as much a part of the camerawork."

(Robert Richardson, *American Cinematographer*, November 1994)

![Black and white photo showing a scene from the movie Natural Born Killers, featuring a man and a woman in a car.]()Natural Born Killers

■ We know each other like a husband and wife do. We fight. We disagree. But it doesn't get so emotional that we lose our bond. It was something we felt right from the beginning. It was one of those situations where someone walks into the room, and you just know that he's going to be connected to you in this life.

(Oliver Stone, <http://www.theasc.com/protect/nov98/collab/pg.4htn>)

After eleven films together, Richardson and Stone parted ways in 1997, when there was a scheduling conflict over *Any Given Sunday*. Richardson went on to shoot three films for Scorsese — *Casino* (1995), *Bringing Out the Dead* (1999), and *The Aviator* (2004) — and has since shot two more: *Shine a Light* (2008) and the 2009 release *Shutter Island*. Richardson sees himself more as the substitute for Scorsese's usual collaborator, Michael Ballhaus. But just as one recovers from divorce and finds new relationships, Richardson began a new one with Quentin Tarantino. Richardson shot *Kill Bill: Volume 1 & 2* and said of the experience:

■ The purest rhythm I have ever had with a director — ever.

(Robert Richardson, ASC, *American Cinematographer*, October 2003, 35)
